2007 WL 2588591 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEROY CHAMBERS A.K.A. JAMICA CHAMBERS A.K.A. JAMAICA CHAMBERS
FILE: AXX XX3 311 - OAKDALE, LA
August 8, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorraine L. Griffin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - Nonimmigrant - remained longer than permitted
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation Notice:
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(A) of the Act
 
  
APPLICATION: Relief
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s timely appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 2, 2007, finding the respondent removable based on his admissions (Tr. at 9-10) and record of conviction¿FN1º (Exh. 3); and ordering his removal to Jamaica, will be dismissed. The respondent’s request to proceed on appeal in forma pauperis is granted under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(c). See Matter of Chicas, 19 I&N Dec. 114 (BIA 1984).
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s appellate contentions that he was denied constitutional due process, first by the Immigration Judge, and secondly, by his former counsel, based on allegations of ineffective assistance in the presentation of his case.
 
Initially, we find no support for the respondent’s claim that he was denied constitutional due process by the Immigration Judge. We have held an alien must demonstrate that he has been prejudiced by a violation of a procedural rule or regulation before his deportation proceeding will be invalidated. Matter of Garcia-Flores, 17 I&N Dec. 325, 329 (BIA 1980). This is consistent with the rule that an alien must have been prejudiced by a procedural defect in his deportation proceeding before he will be found to have suffered a denial of due process. See Ka Fung Chan v. INS, 634 F.2d 248, 258 (5th Cir. 1981). In the matter before us, no such prejudice has been shown. Moreover, the record reflects that the respondent had ample opportunity to prepare for and address the removal charges.
 
The respondent has also raised a claim of denial of due process premised on his allegations of ineffective assistance by his former attorney’s presentation of his case. Where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is made, the Board has found that certain criteria must be met. Specifically, the claim must be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make, counsel must be informed of the allegations against him and provided an opportunity to respond, and the respondent must indicate whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162 (5th Cir. 2006) (citing Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-498 (5 th Cir. 2000) (upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada)). We note the respondent here has failed to meet any of the criteria set forth in Lozada, in order to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Notwithstanding, even if were to find the respondent had complied with the Lozada requirements, he must also establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his former attorney’s ineffectiveness. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra at 640. The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, acknowledging that it “has never squarely held that an alien has ‘a constitutional right to effective counsel in removal proceedings,”’ nevertheless has stated that an “alien’s right to due process is violated when ‘the representation afforded ¿himº was so deficient as to impinge upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing,’ and, as a result, the alien suffered substantial prejudice.” Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 fn.15 (5th Cir. 2006) (citing Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 fn.2 (5th Cir. 2001) (quoting Paul v. INS, 521 F.2d 194, 198 (5th Cir.1975)) (internal citations omitted)). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, supra at 561. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998); see also Assaad v. Ashcroft, 378 F.3d 471, 475 (5th Cir. 2004) (ineffective assistance of counsel may violate an alien’s right to due process under the Fifth Amendment if the “representation afforded ¿the alienº … was so deficient as to impinge upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing.”).
 
The respondent has failed to demonstrate what actions his former attorney should have taken that would have warranted a different result and so as to demonstrate he was “prejudiced.” See e.g.,Dakane v. United States Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005) (Prejudice is shown where counsel’s actions is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that, but for counsel’s ineffectiveness, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different). The respondent’s contention that his former counsel should have objected to the documentary evidence establishing his 2002 New Jersey drug trafficking conviction (Exh. 3) is specious and unavailing, as there is no argument that he stands convicted of that offense and that it is final for immigration purposes. Moreover, we find no support for the respondent’s appellate argument that his former counsel rendered ineffective assistance when she failed to argue that 2002 New Jersey drug trafficking conviction for which he was sentenced to 20 years in state prison, 54 month of which to be served without parole, was not an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B). We find there is nothing in the record, nor has the respondent provided any evidence to suggest that his former counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair.
 
*3 Finally, with regard to the respondent’s appellate constitutional challenges, we note that this Board and the Immigration Judges lack the authority to consider constitutional challenges to the statutes and regulations we administer. See Matter of Fede, 20 I&N Dec. 35 (BIA 1989); see also Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 1988); Matter of Valdovinos, 18 I&N Dec. 343 (BIA 1982).
 
The respondent raises no other arguments on appeal which were not addressed by the Immigration Judge or which would cause us to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. Based on the record before us, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States based on the respondent’s admissions¿FN2º and the conviction record (Exh. 3) submitted in this case. See section 240(c)(3)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. 1229a(c)(3)(A). Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he was either statutorily eligible or warranted any relief from removal to which he may have been otherwise entitled.
 
Accordingly, for the reasons discussed above, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record reflects the respondent was convicted on September 13, 2002, in the Superior Court - Law Division, Somerset County, New Jersey, of the offense of Possession with Intent to Distribute Cocaine, in violation of the New Jersey Statutes 2C:35-5a(a)/2C:35-5b(1), and sentenced to 20 years imprisonment, 54 months of which to be served without parole (Exh. 3).

2

The respondent admitted that his 2002 New Jersey drug trafficking conviction is an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, at the hearing through his representative and conceded his removability (Tr. at 9-10). In addition, noting the respondent did not appear eligible for any relief from removal, his attorney made no applications for relief (Tr. at 11). Absent egregious circumstances, a concession made by counsel of record is binding upon the alien as an admission. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986).

2007 WL 2463926 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HAROLD GUEVARA-CASTILLO
FILE: AXXX XX4 345 - OMAHA, NE
August 7, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Barton C. Winter, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ann M. Tanke
Acting Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s May 22, 2007, decision denying his motion to reopen removal proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against him on September 12, 2006. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) urges affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Guatemala who entered the United States on or about October 3, 2005. In the motion to reopen filed before the Immigration Judge, the respondent asserted that he did not receive notice of the scheduled hearing. In addition, he claimed that his former attorney rendered ineffective assistance in failing to enter an appearance in Immigration Court or assist him in maintaining his current address with the court, arguing that the deficient representation constituted exceptional circumstances to excuse his failure to appear at his removal hearing.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded if the alien files a motion to reopen, within 180 days of the date of the order of removal, demonstrating that he failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, or at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
Despite the respondent’s claim that he did not receive notice of the removal hearing, we find that the respondent was properly served with notice of the removal hearing at his last known address. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c). The record reflects that the respondent was personally served the Notice to Appear on October 5, 2005 (Exh. 1). Thus, the respondent was informed in writing of his obligation to notify the Immigration Court immediately by using Form EOIR-33 whenever he changed his address or telephone number during the course of proceedings, as well as of the consequences of his failure to notify. See Section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act. The respondent also received oral notice in Spanish of the consequences of his failure to attend a removal hearing (Exh. 1). The record further reflects that the hearing notice was mailed to the address that the respondent provided to the DHS at the time of charging. While the respondent’s father stated in his supplemental affidavit in support of the respondent’s amended motion to reopen that he and the respondent moved to Minnesota in “January/February of 2006,” the respondent failed to file a change of address with the Immigration Court on form EOIR-33 as required by statute. See Section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act. Thus, the hearing notice mailed to the respondent on March 10, 2006, at the last address supplied to the DHS, constituted sufficient service under the statute.
 
*2 Although the respondent also argues that exceptional circumstances existed for his failure to appear, those claims are untimely, as he did not raise them in a motion to reopen filed within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s September 12, 2006, decision.¿FN1º See section 240(b)(5)(c) of the Act see also Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dee. 140 (BIA 1998) and Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day time limit under section 242B(e)(3)(A) of the Act).¿FN2º Nevertheless, we would also find that the respondent has failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel that would constitute exceptional circumstances. Regardless of former counsel’s actions in failing to enter an appearanee or to instruct the respondent about his address reporting requirements, the respondent had the statutory responsibility to notify the Immigration Court of his change of address, and the record reflects that he received written notification of his statutory obligation. We thus find that the respondent has not demonstrated that he received ineffective assistance of counsel that eaused him to fail to appear for the September 12, 2006, heating. He therefore has not established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed on May 1, 2007, more than 180 days after the Immigration Judge’s September 12, 2006, in absentia decision.

2

We note that Matter ofA-A, supra, and Matter of Lei, supra, arose in the context of deportation proceedings pursuant to section 242B of the Act. However, as section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act sets forth identical language to that contained in section 242B(c)(3), we can apply the reasoning set forth in Matter of A-A-, supra, and Matter of Lei, supra, by analogy.

2007 WL 2463935 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WYNDHAM JAMES
FILE: AXX XX4 988 - BUFFALO, NY
August 6, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Albert Van Lare, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on October 3, 2002, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s June 29, 1999, decision denying his motion to terminate his proceedings. The respondent has now filed an untimely motion to reopen. The motion is denied.
 
Through his motion, the respondent, through new counsel, argues that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel during his proceedings before the Immigration Judge and the Board. In particular, the respondent asserts that his prior counsel failed to notify him of the June 29, 1999, removal order entered against him and told him that his case had been resolved favorably in 1998; filed an appeal with the Board without his authorization; failed to notify him of any court appearances beyond his original appearance in early 1998; and, failed to have any contact with him after 1998. The respondent asserts that he has complied with the Board’s requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Although not specifically raised by the respondent, it appears that the respondent seeks to have the motions deadline equitably tolled under Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000).
 
Under Iavorski v. INS, supra, the Board must first address whether the respondent was denied due process in these proceedings based upon the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. At the outset, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, supra. In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id at 640.
 
Further, the Second Circuit has concluded that a valid ineffective assistance of counsel claim occurs when “counsel’s performance impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing in violation of the fifth amendment due process clause.” See Iavorski v. INS, supra quoting Rabiu v. INS, 41 F.3d 879, 882 (2d Cir. 1994). Such a deprivation of fundamental fairness is established when “(1) competent counsel would have acted otherwise, and (2) the alien was prejudiced by counsel’s performance.” Esposito v. 1NS, 987 F.2d 108, 111 (2d Cir. 1993). It is only when these conditions are met, that the filing deadline for an untimely motion to reopen proceedings based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be equitably tolled. See id. at 108 (motion to reopen to permit filing of appeal based on claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
*2 In this case, we find that even if the respondent complied Matter of Lozada, supra, he has failed to sustain his burden of proof in establishing his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel based upon the evidence contained in the record. In this regard, we note that the respondent notified former counsel of his complaint and provided former counsel with an opportunity to respond. The respondent’s former counsel disputes the respondent’s claims and has provided evidence to support his position. In addition to the documentation submitted by former counsel in response to the respondent’s Lozada claim against him, we find that the independent evidence of record supports former counsel’s position that the respondent has misrepresented the events during his proceedings both before the Board and the Immigration Judge in order to support his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
First, the respondent’s claim that former counsel notified him in 1998 that his case had been resolved favorably is belied by the transcript contained in the record which shows that the respondent, represented by former counsel, was present and testified during his merits hearing on May 24, 1999 (Tr. at 30-37). This evidence also dispels the respondent’s contentions that he was not provided the opportunity to participate in his own proceedings, that he was not notified by former counsel of any court appearances after 1998, and that he had no contact with former counsel after 1998. It is disingenuous at best for the respondent to contend that he was informed of a favorable disposition of his case in 1998 by his attorney, that he was not notified of any court appearances after 1998, that he had no contact with his former counsel after 1998, and that he was unable to participate in his “defense” when the record clearly establishes that the respondent was present, represented by former counsel, and actively participated in the May 24, 1999, merits hearing (Tr. at 30-37). Moreover, the record contains a Notice of Appearance, Form EOIR-27, filed with the Board on July 28, 1999, in conjunction with the respondent’s appeal which has been duly executed by both the respondent and former counsel. This evidence contradicts the respondent’s claim that he was completely unaware that former counsel filed an appeal with the Board on his behalf. Under these circumstances, the respondent has failed to establish that the motions deadline applicable to his current motion should be equitably tolled.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 2463935 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 2463944 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDA ELISABETH CRUZ ESCOTO A.K.A. EDA FUENTES
FILE: AXX XX0 720 - ATLANTA, GA
August 3, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Neil Taylor, Jr., Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s January 18, 2007, decision denying her motion to reconsider his November 17, 2006, decision denying her motion to reopen in absentia proceedings. On appeal the respondent, through counsel, cites Rule 60 of the Federal Rules of Civil Procedure in arguing she should not be penalized for her attorney’s mistake in having her come to his office on the day of his uncle’s funeral even though a motion to waive her presence at Immigration Court on that same day had not been granted. The respondent also cites Matter of Cruz-Garcia, 22 I&N Dec. 1155 (BIA 1999) in claiming the Immigration Judge incorrectly required her to demonstrate “exceptional circumstances” rather than “reasonable cause” for failing to appear at her scheduled hearing (Resp.’s Br. at 4-5). The Department of Homeland Security has not offered any response to the respondent’s appeal.
 
The respondent misstates the holdings in Matter of Cruz-Garcia, supra, and Matter of Mancera, 22 I&N Dec. 79 (BIA 1998), which discussed the timeliness of motions to reopen proceedings conducted in absentia under section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act.¿FN1º As the respondent’s proceedings were initiated on March 23, 2006, with the issuance of the Notice to Appear, her proceedings are governed by section 240(b)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), which requires a showing of exceptional circumstances, not “reasonable cause,” for failure to appear. Consequently, the Immigration Judge correctly applied the “exceptional circumstances” standard for the respondent’s failure to appear. We also note that the Federal Rules of Civil Procedure are not applicable in deportation proceedings. See Matter of Magana, 17 I&N Dec. 111 (BIA 1979). As such, the respondent’s arguments in this regard are unavailing.
 
However, we find it difficult to conceive a set of facts upon which a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was so clearly demonstrated as those which are presented before us.¿FN2º The respondent’s counsel has repeatedly admitted he knew his motion to waive the respondent’s appearance at her master calendar hearing had not yet been acted upon. Nonetheless he still had his client travel to his office rather than the Immigration Court as he had a funeral to attend that day, on the assumption that a favorable decision would be rendered. He predicated this assumption on the actions of another Immigration Judge in another Immigration Court, who allowed such waivers (Motion to Reconsider at 1). Counsel admittedly is new to the practice of immigration law, and essentially presented “common sense” and humanitarian arguments in his motions that had nothing to do with either the law, or Immigration Court practice (Motion to Reopen; Motion to Reconsider, tab A). In essence, he contended that as a close relative’s funeral was being held where his attendance was necessary, the Immigration Judge should have excused his telling his client, the respondent, not to appear in Immigration Court, but instead come to his office for a telephonic hearing, despite the fact that a decision had not been rendered on his motion. Such action, facially, constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 The Immigration Judge correctly cited our decision in Matter of Lozada, supra, in noting the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We find such a claim is implicit in the respondent’s motion. However, as noted, we are hard-pressed to discern a situation in which ineffective assistance was so clearly presented. Rather, by his own account, counsel has repeatedly provided the respondent with inaccurate and/or incomplete assistance, both with regard to her appearance at court, and in the filing of her motion to reopen. Given the highly unusual situation presented, we find that the concerns addressed by the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied in the instant situation. Counsel clearly admits his deficient service, has had an opportunity to address the same, his errors occurred in the course of representation he clearly agreed to perform, and the respondent was unquestionably prejudiced as a result of his advice. Id. While the requirement that a bar complaint be filed has not been complied with, we shall refer this case to the EOIR Office of General Counsel for review, thus addressing all of the concerns expressed in Matter of Lozada.
 
In sum, we find the respondent was afforded ineffective assistance of counsel. As such, she has demonstrated exceptional circumstances excusing her failure to appear for her October 10, 2006, hearing. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s October 10, 2006, and November 17, 2006, decisions are vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s October 17, 2006, motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

Section 242(b) applies when service of the notice of hearing is made prior to June 13, 1992. See Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). Here, proceedings were initiated in 2006 (Exh. 1). As such, the cases cited by the respondent are inapplicable to her case.

2

We assume the same thought came to the Immigration Judge, as he outlined the requirements for such a claim as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), in his denial of the motion to reopen. The fact that counsel chose to ignore this suggestion supports rather than undermines the inference of ineffective assistance of counsel.

2007 WL 2463945 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EZEQUIEL MIRAMONTES-ARTEAGA
FILE: AXX-XX9-677 - LOS ANGELES, CA
August 3, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mario Acosta, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joyce Bakke
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of an aggravated felony
 
Lodged: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; Termination; Section 212(c) Waiver
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals an Immigration Judge’s March 1, 2007, decision granting the Department of Homeland Security’s (“DHS”) motion to rescind an order granting the respondent’s September 22, 2006, motion to reopen the instant deportation proceedings. In addition, a motion to reopen filed by the respondent on March 23, 2007, and supplemented by a filing on May 4, 2007, is currently before the Board. The appeal is dismissed and the motion is denied.
 
On January 1, 1998, the Immigration Judge found the respondent deportable under former section 241(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii), as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. The respondent was convicted on July 21, 1994, of the offense of transportation/sale of a narcotic substance, to wit: cocaine, in violation of California Health and Safety Code § 11352 (I.J. at 2 (1/13/98)). The Immigration Judge further found that the respondent was ineligible for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), pursuant to Matter of Soriano, 21 I&N Dec. 516, 533 (A.G. 1997). The respondent did not appeal this decision and was physically removed from the United States in 1998 pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
Five years later, on January 24, 2003, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court, arguing that the Immigration Judge: (1) erroneously concluded that his 1994 criminal conviction involved an aggravated felony; (2) failed to inform him of his right to seek a section 212(c) waiver; and (3) led him to believe that it would be futile to file an appeal. The Immigration Judge denied this motion on March 17, 2003, finding that it was untimely, as it was filed well over 90 days after the January 13, 1998, entry of the final administrative order (I.J. at 2 (3/17/03)). The Immigration Judge further found that his prior decision was in accord with the law and facts at the time, and that he properly advised the respondent of his right to appeal (I.J. at 2 (3/17/03)). On November 25, 2003, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of this decision on the merits and further rejected an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, which the respondent had raised for the first time in the appeal. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We noted in our decision that the respondent’s motion was also barred because it was filed after his physical departure from the United States under a final order of deportation—an act that brought the deportation proceedings to a close. See Matter of Okoh, 20 I&N Dec. 864, 865 (BIA 1994).
 
*2 On September 22, 2006, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen with the Immigration Court. The respondent noted that after reentering the United States illegally in 2001, he was arrested and indicted for violating 8 U.S.C. § 1326, and sentenced to serve 77 months of imprisonment after the entrance of a conditional guilty plea. On appeal to the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the respondent argued that the 8 U.S.C. § 1326 charge should be dismissed because the January 13, 1998, deportation order was issued in violation of his due process rights. The counsel for the Government in the criminal appellate proceedings agreed and filed an unopposed motion for remand to the District Court, which the Ninth Circuit granted, asserting that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that the respondent’s 1994 conviction was an aggravated felony without employing the modified categorical approach. See United States v. Rivera-Sanchez, 247 F.3d 905, 909 (9th Cir. 2001). In the second motion to reopen filed with the Immigration Court, the respondent argued that reopening was appropriate under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2, because of material changes in the law and because his deportation order amounted to a deprivation of due process. The Immigration Judge granted this motion on September 28, 2006.
 
However, in a decision issued on March 1, 2007, which is the subject of the instant appeal, the Immigration Judge granted the DHS’s motion to rescind his order granting reopening. In pertinent part, the governing regulations provide that an Immigration Judge may reopen any case in which he has rendered a decision, “unless jurisdiction is vested with the Board of Immigration Appeals.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Furthermore, the Board has appellate jurisdiction over decisions of Immigration Judges in deportation cases. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(b)(2). Additionally, a request to reopen a case in which a decision has been made by the Board, which is made by the party affected by the decision, must be in the form of a written motion to the Board. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a)(1). Based on this authority, we agree with the Immigration Judge that jurisdiction became vested with the Board on June 13, 2003, when the respondent appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial of his January 24, 2003, motion to reopen (I.J. at 5 (3/1/07)). The cases cited by the respondent-Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974), and Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 156 (BIA 1998)—are distinguishable because they involved situations in which the Board permitted the filing of a motion to reopen with an Immigration Judge after dismissing an appeal of an earlier decision issued by the Immigration Judge for lack of jurisdiction (I.J. at 5 (3/1/07)). On the contrary, on November 25, 2003, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s January 24, 2003, motion to reopen on the merits (I.J. at 5 (3/1/07)). Therefore, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s March 1, 2007, decision and the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
*3 Turning to the respondent’s motion to reopen filed with the Board, we note that he is seeking to have the January 13, 1998, deportation order vacated and the proceedings terminated, or in the alternative, to be allowed to pursue section 212(c) relief in subsequent proceedings. The respondent claims that there is a basis in law for reopening these deportation proceedings, despite the fact that he physically departed from the United States pursuant to an order of deportation in 1998. Specifically, he argues that he is entitled to benefit from material changes in the law effectuated by Rivera-Sanchez, supra, and United States v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). He further claims that he has experienced a denial of due process, which is an extraordinary circumstance warranting sua sponte reopening. In immigration proceedings, however, collateral attack on an executed order of removal is warranted only when that order resulted in a “gross miscarriage of justice.” Matter of La Grotta, 14 I&N Dec. 110, 111-12 (BIA 1972). Furthermore, such a gross miscarriage of justice exists only when it can be conclusively determined, upon the basis of then-existing judicial and administrative decisions, that the prior finding of deportability was not in conformity with the law as interpreted at the time the order was entered and executed. Hernandez-Almanza v. INS, 547 F.2d 100, 102-03 (9th Cir. 1976); Matter of Farinas, 12 I&N Dec. 467 (BIA 1967); Matter of Malone, 11 I&N Dec. 730 (BIA 1966). With these principles in mind, we conclude that the respondent’s 1998 deportation order is not susceptible to collateral attack in these proceedings.
 
At the time of the respondent’s deportation in 1998, aliens in deportation proceedings who had been convicted of aggravated felonies were deemed to be categorically ineligible for section 212(c) relief. See Matter of Soriano, supra, at 533-40. While the Ninth Circuit and the Supreme Court ultimately overruled the rationale of Matter of Soriano, those decisions were not issued until 1999 and 2001, respectively, well after the respondent’s deportation occurred (I.J. at 2, (3/1/07)). See St. Cyr, supra; Magana-Pizano v. INS, 200 F.3d 603 (9th Cir. 1999). Likewise, the Ninth Circuit had not yet adopted the framework requiring the application of the modified categorical approach to divisible statutes when determining removability in aggravated felony cases. See, e.g., Rivera-Sanchez, supra. Because the Immigration Judge’s January 13, 1998, determination that the respondent was deportable and ineligible for section 212(c) relief was in conformity with the law as interpreted at the time, we cannot find that the deportation order resulted in a “gross miscarriage of justice,” such that it is subject to collateral attack in the instant proceedings. Ramirez-Juarez v. INS, 622 F.2d 174, 175-76 (9th Cir. 1980); see also Robledo-Gonzales v. Ashcroft, 342 F.3d 667, 682 n. 13 (7th Cir. 2003). Specifically, the respondent’s 1998 deportation order is not invalidated simply because the law may have changed subsequent to the issuance of that order. Ramirez-Juarez, supra; see also Alvarenga-Villalobos v. Ashcroft, 271 F.3d 1169, 1172-73 (9th Cir. 2001) (holding that a removal order that. was lawful when executed is not invalidated by subsequent changes in law) (citing Teague v. Lane, 489 U.S. 288, 307-08 (1989)). Therefore, we conclude that the respondent’s 1998 deportation order did not violate his due process rights.¿FN1º
 
*4 Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Furthermore, we find that the cases cited by the respondent with respect to the “gross miscarriage of justice” issue are distinguishable. Unlike the scenarios in those cases, the respondent’s 1994 conviction for transportation/sale of a narcotic substance has not been vacated. See Cardoso-Tlaseca v. Gonzales, 460 F.3d 1102 (9th Cir. 2006); Estrada-Rosales v. INS, 645 F.2d 819 (9th Cir. 1981).

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Review Granted in Part, Cause Remanded by Hernandez-Gonzalez v. Holder, 9th Cir., March 24, 2010
2007 WL 2463968 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARTHA GABRIELA HERNANDEZ-GONZALEZ A.K.A. CYNTHIA PULGARIN
FILE: AXX XX2 817 - SAN FRANCISCO, CA
August 2, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martin Resendez Guajardo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James Gildea
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGES:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
237(a)(3)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(3)(D)(i)º - False claim to United States citizenship
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on May 3, 2006, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our February 6, 2006, decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision finding her removable, and denying her application for adjustment of status. The respondent appealed our decision to the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. In an order issued January 24, 2007, the court granted the respondent’s unopposed motion to remand proceedings.
 
In the motion to remand the respondent contended the Board neglected to address the claim in her motion to reconsider that a remand was warranted as she was eligible for cancellation of removal. On remand the respondent newly alleges in a brief that she, in fact, did seek cancellation of removal during the proceedings before the Immigration Judge (Remand Br. at 3). The respondent avers the Immigration Judge and this Board erred by “ignor¿ingº her requests” for cancellation of removal, and failing to address her eligibility for such relief (Remand Br. at 7).¿FN1º She also maintains the Immigration Judge should have considered her eligibility for cancellation after determining she was ineligible for adjustment of status (Remand Br. at 8).
 
The respondent’s above-noted arguments contain misstatements of fact. During the proceedings below the respondent did initially indicate she wished to seek cancellation of removal in addition to adjustment of status (Tr. at 2). The Immigration Judge granted her a continuance of more than 11 months in which to submit any applications for relief, specifically referencing a cancellation application (Tr. at 3-4). However, when the hearing reconvened, the respondent indicated the only relief she intended to pursue was adjustment of status with a waiver of inadmissibility (Tr. at 7-10). To that end, the sole application for relief submitted by the respondent was for adjustment of status (Exhs. 2-3).¿FN2º Contrary to the arguments in her brief on remand, the respondent neither expressed any desire to pursue an application for cancellation of removal, nor did she submit the requisite application for such relief within the time limit set by the Immigration Judge. As such, she waived her opportunity to pursue an application for cancellation of removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (stating that an Immigration Judge may set and extend time limits for the filing of applications, and that if an application is not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed waived). Accordingly, we find no error in this regard.
 
*2 For the respondent’s benefit, we observe that her subsequent dissatisfaction with a strategic decision by counsel is not grounds to reopen unless she can show counsel behaved so egregiously that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B- et al., 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310 (BIA 1998) (finding no ineffective assistance of counsel where the attorney’s insistence on corroborating evidence discouraged the aliens from applying for asylum), citing Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931 (9th Cir. 1986); Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (absent a showing of egregious circumstances, a tactical decision by an attorney is binding on the alien). Here, the respondent has not alleged, much less established, she had ineffective assistance of counsel during the proceedings below. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637(BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Given these facts, we find no merit to her argument the Immigration Judge erred in not addressing her eligibility for cancellation of removal after her claim for adjustment failed. Absent showing that her counsel engaged in egregious conduct as noted, she cannot now recant her decision to pursue only one form of relief. Id. Therefore, we find no merit to these allegations of error.
 
In her brief on remand the respondent states she is currently pregnant with a United States citizen child, and asks that her case therefore be reopened for consideration of an application for cancellation of removal (Remand Br. at 11-13). This assertion is in the nature of a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); see also Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). However, it is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Board decision she seeks to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent has not contended that her pregnancy constitutes an exception to this deadline, nor do we otherwise find as such. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). We also do not find that her pregnancy constitutes an exceptional circumstance exempting her from the time requirements for motions to reopen. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (Board retains limited discretionary power under the regulations to reopen or reconsider only in exceptional circumstances). Therefore, reopening in this regard is not warranted.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Douglas B. Schoppert
*3 FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent contends it was “fundamentally unfair” for the Immigration Judge to find her ineligible for adjustment of status and conclude proceedings as the DHS had filed a new charge of removability against her only seven days before the hearing (Remand Br. at 8). However, the respondent neither raised this claim on appeal, nor was this part of the Circuit Court remand. As such, we do not find that this claim is properly before us. Moreover, this argument is in the nature of a motion to reconsider, and is untimely as it was filed more than 30 days after the Board decision she seeks to have reconsidered. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Therefore, reconsideration is not appropriate.

2

Thus, this issue was never presented before the Board, and we did not err in failing to address it in our initial decision.

2007 WL 2463970 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BRUNO ALFREDO DELLEPIANE
FILE: AXX XX8 123 - DALLAS, TX
August 1, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Princy Sethi, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present in the United States without being admitted or paroled
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal under section 240A(a)
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Peru, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of March 15, 2006, that denied his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (hereinafter “Act”). The appeal will be dismissed. The request for oral argument before the Board is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 3.1(e).
 
The Immigration Judge determined that the applicant, a legal permanent resident, had been convicted of multiple crimes involving moral turpitude and, as the respondent had failed to report for a deferred inspection, was presently in the United States without being admitted or paroled and, therefore, he was removable from the United States. See I.J. at 7-9. The Immigration Judge further determined that as a matter of discretion, after balancing the positive and negative factors, cancellation of removal was not warranted. See I.J. at 9-14. See also Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7, 11 (BIA 1998) (ruling that in exercising discretion under section 240A(a) an Immigration Judge must review the record as a whole to balance the adverse factors of undesirability with social and humane considerations.)
 
Regarding the crimes involving moral turpitude, the respondent argues that he was only convicted of one crime for theft (shoplifting) under section 609.52 of the Minnesota Statutes, which is considered a misdemeanor. See Matter of Jurado, 24 I&N Dec. 29 (BIA 2006) (holding that retail theft is a crime involving moral turpitude.) While the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had admitted under oath to a second conviction for shoplifting in Pennsylvania, the respondent argues that his testimony on this offense did not amount to an admission of a conviction, but merely that he was arrested for this offense, and, therefore, he is admissible under the petty offense exception at section 212(a)(2)(A)(ii)(II). The record does not contain any court documentation of a conviction and we are forced to rely on the sworn testimony. During his testimony, the respondent admitted being arrested for a theft in Minnesota in 1989, for shoplifting in Pennsylvania in 1992, for assault in Missouri, and for theft and “execution of documents by deception” in Texas in January 2006. See Tr. at 43, 45, 48-50. We agree that the respondent clearly admitted to the conviction for theft in Minnesota, but the record reveals that when questioned about the other arrests he denied any convictions, and the Immigration Judge committed error in finding otherwise.¿FN1º As discussed by the Immigration Judge during the hearing and in his decision, the respondent, through his attorney, conceded the charge of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I). See I.J. at 3; Tr. at 2-3. However, the respondent’s attorney admitted in open court that this was a mistake, then appears to have made an oral motion to correct this mistake, and, although the Immigration Judge noted at the hearing and in his decision that the attorney’s concession amounted to ineffective assistance by counsel, the Immigration Judge refused to invoke the petty offense exception at section 212(a)(2)(A)(ii)(II) as he found the respondent had admitted to a second conviction for a crime involving moral turpitude (that is, shoplifting in Pennsylvania in 1992). See I.J. at 7-9; Tr. at 70-72. As the Immigration Judge clearly indicated that the respondent’s concession of the charge under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) was due to ineffective assistance of counsel, and erroneously determined that the respondent had admitted under oath to more than one conviction to a misdemeanor crime involving moral turpitude, we conclude that the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent was ineligible for the petty offense exception is also clearly erroneous.
 
*2 However, even if respondent is not prohibited from admission under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), he is still removable under the charge at section 212(a)(6)(A)(i). The record reveals that the respondent attempted to reenter the United States on September 5, 2004, after a 10-day trip to Peru, but his inspection for admission was deferred apparently due to the suspicion that he was inadmissible under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I). See Tr. at 37, 39. See also section 101(a)(13) of the Act (“admission” and “admitted” means the lawful entry of the alien into the United States after inspection and authorization by an immigration officer.) The respondent never reported for this deferred inspection and has not lawfully reentered the United States. See Tr. at 40-41. The respondent argues that the charge under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) is merely derivative of the section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) charge and his inspection would not have been deferred but for the latter charge. Regardless of the Department of Homeland Security’s (hereinafter “DHS”) reasons for deferring the respondent’s inspection, the respondent has acknowledged that he never reported for the scheduled inspection and he has conceded the charge.¿FN2º There have been no allegations that his attorney made this concession in error. Thus, the respondent is removable under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) unless he can demonstrate that cancellation of this removal is warranted under section 240A(a).
 
The positive factors for cancellation of removal include the respondent legally entering the United States in 1979 and becoming a legal permanent resident in 1984. See Tr. at 24, 34. He has significant family ties in the United States to include his parents, siblings, wife, two biological children, and two adopted children. See Exh. 3(8-9); Tr. at 35. He also has maintained steady work with a car dealership for a substantial period and a lengthy history of paying his taxes. See Exh. 3(10); Tr. at 38, 46. The respondent also has significant economic ties to the United States, to include his career which pays him approximately $10,000.00 a month, a house worth approximately $280,000.00 with equity of approximately $60,000.00, and a bank account with $1,000.00 to $1,200.00 balance. See Tr. 38. His wife and youngest child appear to be not just emotionally attached to him, but also economically dependent on him as her small travel agency has yet to be profitable. See Tr. at 75. Mitigating his family ties to the United States is the fact that the respondent does not appear to have a close relationship with his parents or his oldest child, who did not provide any type of statement in support of the respondent and the respondent did not testify to any ongoing relationship with these individuals. In addition, his business ties to the United States appear to be mitigated by the charges surrounding his arrest in January 2006 that are in connection with his business practices in an automobile dealership. See Tr. at 45-46.
 
*3 However, the most troubling and significant unfavorable factor is the respondent’s repeated omissions, evasions, and fabrications connected with his cancellation of removal application and testimony. Prior to his testimony, the Immigration Judge gave the respondent the opportunity to amend his application, after which the respondent added his arrest in January 2006. See Tr. at 15. However, this action was only undertaken after counsel for DHS had confronted the respondent and his attorney with the arrest record prior to the hearing. See Tr. at 48. After amending the application, the respondent swore under oath that the contents were accurate and true. See Tr. at 17. However, it was clearly revealed during testimony that the information on the application was incorrect and that the respondent knew this at the time he attested to its truthfulness. On his application, the respondent claimed that he had only been married one time (to his current wife), that he had married his wife in December 2000, that he obtained his legal permanent residence status in connection with an amnesty program, and that he had only been arrested on two occasions in 1989 and again in 2006. See Exh. 2. However, his testimony revealed that he had previously been married to another woman, that he had married his current wife in December 1998, that he obtained his legal permanent residence status through his first marriage to a United States citizen, and that he in fact had been arrested on four different occasions, to include an arrest for shoplifting in 1992 and an arrest for assault. See Tr. at 35, 43, 45, 48-50, 54-56. The respondent acknowledged during his testimony that he had knowingly submitted an incorrect application and failed to make any explanation for his behavior. Id. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s criminal and arrest record, with the most recent arrest just months prior to his hearing, and due to the numerous omissions and fabrications on his cancellation application, for which he offered no adequate explanation, shows evidence of a lack of rehabilitation and a lack of credibility. We find that the respondent’s fabrications, omissions, and evasiveness while under oath is an unfavorable factor against the grant of cancellation of removal that far outweighs the positive factors in this case.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion in giving an undue amount of weight to the issue of the inaccurate application, evidence of rehabilitation, and his credibility. We disagree. See Matter of A-S-, 21 I&N 1106 (BIA 1998) (ruling that the Board defers to an adverse credibility finding when the discrepancies and omissions described by the Immigration Judge are actually present, the discrepancies and omissions provide specific and cogent reasons to conclude that the alien provided false or incredible evidence, and a convincing explanation for the discrepancies and omissions has not been provided by the alien.) The respondent claims that the inaccuracies on the application were corrected during testimony, but we find that this characterization is itself inaccurate as our review of the transcript shows that the respondent reluctantly acknowledged the inaccuracies and omissions on his application only after counsel for DHS confronted him with evidence that he had knowingly made false statements on the application and during his prior testimony. See Tr. at 47-48, 52-56. See also Matter of Jurado, supra. (ruling that unsworn falsification to authorities is a crime involving moral turpitude.) We also find that the Immigration Judge gave this unfavorable factor its appropriate weight as the respondent’s lack of credibility undercuts the veracity of his claims of favorable factors to include his rehabilitation, economic ties to the United States, and hardship incurred on him and his family if removed. See Matter of O-D-, 21 I&N Dec. 1079 (BIA 1998) (holding that an alien’s presentation of a counterfeit document, absent an explanation or rebuttal, indicates an overall lack of credibility regarding the entire claim.) In addition, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion by not weighing the totality of the evidence. We disagree, as our review of the Immigration Judge’s March 2006 decision reveals that it includes consideration of all pertinent factors concerning cancellation of removal as discussed in Matter of C-V-T-, supra,
 
*4 Finally, the respondent claims that the DHS’s failure to submit a reply brief must be treated as its acquiescence with the respondent’s position on appeal. We find no merit in this argument as the DHS is not required to file a reply to the respondent’s appeal and its decision to not submit a reply brief in no way abrogates the respondent’s burden of proof in establishing that he meets the criteria for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act.¿FN3º See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(c).
 
Based on the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the negative factors outweigh the positive factors and, therefore, the respondent does not merit cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act as a matter of discretion. We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s exercise of discretion.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

The pertinent testimony includes the following: Q. All right. In 1992, were you convicted of that crime? A. I don’t remember. I don’t think so, sir. Q. Where did that crime occur? A. (Indiscernible). Q. Pardon me? A. (Indiscernible.) I think it was (Indiscernible). Q. Well, did you plead quilty? Or not contest? A. No contest. I don’t remember, sir. (Indiscernible) it was no conviction. Q. Did you plead no contest? A. Yes, sir. See Tr. 63. We find this testimony is not a clear admission to a conviction for shoplifting in 1992 due to the fact of the multiple “indiscernibles” in the transcript and that the respondent does not appear to understand the meaning, or the legal the implications, of pleading “no contest.”

We note that during the hearing the respondent claimed that he did not, or could not, take time off from work for his delayed inspection as his employer did not look favorably on allowing absences. The respondent cites Rarogal v. INS, 42 F.3d 570 (9th Cir. 1994), for the proposition that where the government has the opportunity to concur with the Immigration Court’s findings and to file a brief in support of the Immigration Court’s decision or request for summary affirmance, the government’s silence should be considered as acquiescence with the position advanced by the respondent. The decision in the Rarogal case does not contain such a holding. The facts of Rarogal included government counsel, in open court, conceding the respondent’s rehabilitation from his criminal past and announcing that the government no longer opposed relief from deportation of the alien. Id. at 572. In the respondent’s case, the DHS has not conceded his rehabilitation, nor has the DHS changed its position on opposing cancellation of the respondent’s removal. Therefore, the holding in Rarogal has no bearing on the outcome of the current case.

IN RE: OSCAR DAVID CONTRERAS-GUZMAN
FILE: AXX XX8 881 - HARLINGEN, TX
July 31, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Francisco J. Hernandez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dona J.F. Justus
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on June 23, 2004, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s September 24, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order entered on June 9, 2003. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C).
 
On April 2, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen these proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion because it is time and number barred and the respondent has failed to establish prima facie eligibility for relief from removal. The motion will be denied.
 
As the respondent was ordered removed in absentia, we must look to in absentia case law and statutes for guidance in this matter. In a deportation case, we found that an alien is not required to rescind his deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). Section 240(b)(7) of the Act sets forth a similar limitation on discretionary relief for failing to appear at a removal hearing, as follows:
Any alien against whom a final order of removal is entered in absentia under this subsection and who, at the time of the notice described in paragraph (1) or (2) of section 239(a), was provided oral notice, either in the alien’s native language or in another language the alien understands, of the time and place of the proceedings and of the consequences under this paragraph of failing, other than because of exceptional circumstances (as defined in subsection (e)(1)) to attend a proceeding under this section, shall not be eligible for relief under section 240A, 240B, 245, 248, or 249 for a period of 10 years after the date of the entry of the final order of removal.
 
 
In this case, the Notice to Appear was served personally on the respondent, and he was provided oral notice in Spanish of the consequences of a failure to appear as provided in section 240(b)(7) of the Act (Exh. 1). Consequently, it appears that the respondent is ineligible to seek cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240(b)(7) of the Act.
 
Additionally, the motion will be denied because it was not filed within the statutory and regulatory 90-day time limit for filing a motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
*2 The respondent has not provided any evidence that warrants rescission of the in absentia removal order entered June 9, 2003. In his motion, the respondent states that his prior attorney failed to file a motion to change venue. See Respondent’s Motion at 4. The record reveals that a motion to change venue was untimely filed with the Immigration Court. He also states the he lacked sufficient funds to travel to the June 9, 2003, removal hearing held in Harlingen, Texas. These generalized statements made by respondent’s counsel in the instant motion to reopen are insufficient to constitute an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent’s request for a change of venue is moot.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GERARDO GARCIA-ORTIZ
FILE: AXX XX4 679 - LOS ANGELES, CA
July 27, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nora E. Milner, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen proceedings and remand to the Immigration Judge for a hearing on his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“the Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). He bases the motion on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and has submitted evidence to show that he has Complied with the requirements set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the motion.
 
The respondent has not alleged that the late filing of this motion was due to ineffective assistance of counsel, and it is denied as untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Moreover, we find that the prejudice required to establish ineffective assistance of counsel has not been shown. See Azanor v. Ashcroft, 364 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2004) (stating that alien must demonstrate prejudice from counsel’s ineffective assistance in order to succeed on a due process challenge to an order entered in deportation proceedings); see also Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785 (9th Cir. 2005) (stating that the ineffective assistance must have been so fundamentally unfair that alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case). The respondent does not assert that he is not removable. He claims instead that the evidence to show his removability is not clear and convincing. We disagree. The documentary evidence in the record showing three drug possession convictions and the respondent’s admission that he was convicted of possession of methamphetamine and served 16 months (Tr. at 5) are sufficient to show that he is removable as charged.
 
With respect to the relief of cancellation of removal, the respondent has not shown that he is not barred from relief as an aggravated felon. See section 240A(a)(3) of the Act.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 This case was last before us on September 22, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his motion to remand.
 
On May 2, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen these proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status. He also premises his motion on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Given the totality of the circumstances presented in this case, we will reopen these proceedings on our own motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997): The respondent’s motion is supported by evidence that establishes his prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1255(a). See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 252 (BIA 2002) (allowing reopening of proceedings where a visa petition based on a marriage during proceedings was already pending and there was completed adjustment application supported by clear and convincing evidence of the bona fides of the marriage).

2007 WL 2463897 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEON EARL DALEY A.K.A. DEON DALEY
FILE: AXX XX7 858 - YORK, PA
July 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey T. Bubier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(C), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(C)º - Convicted of firearms or destructive device violation
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 11, 2007, decision. In that decision the Immigration Judge, inter alia, denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), as a matter of discretion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of facts by an Immigration Judge, including those as to the credibility of testimony, to determine whether the findings are “clearly erroneous.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).¿FN1º The Board may review all questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues on appeal from decisions of the Immigration Judge “de novo.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(iii).
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica who entered the United States as a lawful permanent resident in 1982, contends that he did not receive a fair hearing as the Immigration Judge was biased. He asserts that his 1999 conviction for criminal possession of a weapon was not valid as he received ineffective assistance of counsel and the police lied, and that the Immigration Judge erred by improperly weighing the equities in denying his application for cancellation as a matter of discretion. We disagree. We first note that the respondent cites to no specific instance, series of events, or any conduct or action by the Immigration Judge which suggests that the Immigration Judge acted in any way improperly or failed to conduct the respondent’s hearing in a proper and impartial manner. Accordingly, there has been no showing that the respondent’s hearing was not fairly conducted.
 
The respondent insists that his 1999 conviction for criminal possession of a weapon is not valid. Despite the availability of post-conviction motions or other forms of collateral attack, such as where there has been ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent’s conviction is final for immigration purposes unless and until it has been formally overturned. See Matter of Onyido, 22 I&N Dec. 552 (BIA 1999). The respondent has provided no indication that his conviction has been overturned. Consequently, the respondent remains an alien convicted of criminal possession of a weapon.
 
Turning to the underlying issue on appeal, the respondent, who was 8 years old when he entered the United States in 1982, has had no contact with family members in the United States for some 7 to 8 years, is single with no children, and has no assets, argues on appeal that he wants to remain in the United States. He had previously been employed at a car wash and as a bus driver, had received the equivalent of a high school education, had been of assistance to an elderly neighbor for several years, and does not use drugs; all of these factors were considered by the Immigration Judge in denying his application for cancellation as a matter of discretion, and on appeal, the respondent did not dispute any factual findings made by the Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge considered all of the favorable factors, individually and cumulatively. Nonetheless, the Immigration Judge concluded that the serious adverse factors of record, including the respondent’s 1999 convictions for attempted assault, menacing, and criminal possession of a weapon; his 2000 arrest for impersonating a police officer, trespass, and criminal possession of a weapon; and his 2005 conviction for criminal possession of a weapon, all crimes for which the respondent insists he was wrongfully charged, outweighed those favorable factors. After fully considering and weighing all of the favorable and adverse factors of record, we agree with the Immigration Judge that relief was not warranted in the exercise of discretion; we therefore affirm the decision for the reasons stated therein. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998); Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581 (BIA 1978). Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
*2 The following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 2463884 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BOSEDE BELLO A.K.A. ABISUNMI AKINBOLU
FILE: AXX XX4 220 - DETROIT, MI
July 24, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David K. Wenger, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael B. Dobson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(G)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(G)(ii)º - Marriage fraud - failed or refused to fulfill marital agreement (withdrawn)
Sec. 237(a)(3)(B)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(3)(B)(iii)º - Conviction relating to violation of 18 U.S.C. § 1546
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s March 19, 2007, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, as untimely.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, was ordered removed from the United States by an Immigration Judge on October 20, 2006, and the respondent waived appeal of that order. On January 19, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court. She contends, through counsel, that, although the motion was untimely, the 90-day Statutory deadline on filing motions to reopen should be equitably tolled in her case in view of her belief that she received ineffective assistance of counsel; she asserts that she accepted voluntary departure after being misinformed by previous counsel that she was barred from self-petitioning for adjustment of status, as a battered spouse, by reason of a lack of good moral character. The Immigration Judge concluded that, while the 90 day deadline is inapplicable where (1) the basis of the motion is to petition for relief as a battered spouse, (2) the motion is accompanied by a copy of the self-petition or cancellation of removal application, (3) the motion is filed within 1 year of the entry of the final order of removal, and (4) the alien is physically present in the United States when the motion is filed, the respondent’s motion contained neither a cancellation of removal application nor a copy of a self-petition. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(iv). The Immigration Judge further concluded that, with respect to her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent had not met the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent attesting to the relevant facts, former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond, and, if it is asserted that prior counsel’s handling of the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, the motion should reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not). The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent had not been prejudiced as she was, in fact, ineligible to seek a waiver of her visa fraud conviction under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), so as to establish good moral character.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent reiterates her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, indicating that, as she first learned on December 20, 2006, that her previous counsel had erred, equitable tolling was appropriate. She also argues that the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion was erroneous by failing to allow her to self-petition pursuant to section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) of the Act, as she had not received a copy of her self petition (I-360) from her previous counsel.
 
We find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s decision. An alien seeking reopening of a removal order must file the required motion within 90 days after the entry of the final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23 (b)(1). The respondent did not comply with this requirement, and therefore her motion was properly dismissed as untimely. As to the respondent’s invocation of the equitable tolling doctrine, she provides no explanation as to why, having learned, on December 20, 2006, of the alleged misadvice by previous counsel, she was unable to file her motion in a timely fashion, by Thursday, January 18, 2007. While the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this proceeding arises, has held that the time for filing a motion to reopen can be equitably tolled, they declined to do so where, as here, no explanation for the failure to file the motion to reopen with the 90 day deadline is offered. See Harchenko v. INS, 379 F.3d 405, 409-410 (6th Cir. 2004). Furthermore, while the Immigration Judge may waive the 1 year filing deadline to reopen proceedings pursuant to section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv), where there is a showing of exceptional circumstances, the statute requires, without exception, that the motion be accompanied by either a cancellation of removal application or a copy of the self-petition that has been or will be filed with Department of Homeland Security (DHS). Thus, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 2463884 (BIA)
End of Document
