2007 WL 2463889 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARDIFF CHISTPHOR FRASER A.K.A. CARDIFF CHRISTOPHER FRASER
FILE: AXX XX4 831 - MIAMI, FL
July 24, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Orville McKenzie, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jill Tavlin Swartz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Remand; termination
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. Upon review of the record of proceedings, we adopt and affirm the February 28, 2006, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s request for a continuance and ordering him removed to Jamaica. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
The respondent asserts that he received ineffective assistance of counsel because former counsel failed to file an application for cancellation of removal prior to his withdrawal from representation. The respondent’s former counsel has filed a response that persuasively contests the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance, and indicates that the respondent failed to adequately cooperate with counsel in his representation. We find that the respondent has failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Moreover, even assuming ineffective assistance of former counsel, we find that the respondent did not act diligently in pursuing his claims for relief. The record indicates that the respondent was given until July 6, 2004, to file an application for cancellation of removal (Tr. at 7-8). The application was not filed by this deadline. On August 3, 2005, the Immigration Judge granted former counsel’s motion to withdraw from representation. In granting the motion the Immigration Judge stated, “¿tºhe respondent should seek new counsel as soon as possible if he wishes to be represented at the ¿February 28, 2006º merits hearing.” Despite this warning, the respondent waited to obtain new counsel until the day before the February 28, 2006 hearing (Respondent’s Motion for Continuance, dated February 27, 2006). At the February 28, 2006 merits hearing, the Immigration Judge denied counsel’s request for a continuance and indicated that although the respondent missed the July 6, 2004 deadline, he “could have even filed ¿the applicationº ten days before this hearing” (Tr. at 12). We find the respondent’s delay in obtaining new counsel unreasonable under the circumstances. His lack of diligence indicates that reopening is not warranted as a matter of discretion.
 
Further, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for a continuance. A party seeking a continuance has the burden of establishing good cause. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6; Matter of Silva-Rodriguez, 20 I&N Dec. 448, at 450 (1992); Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987). In determining whether an alien has demonstrated “good cause,” an Immigration Judge has broad discretionary authority. See, e.g., Matter of Perez-Andrade, supra. As discussed above, the respondent failed to diligently pursue his claims for relief. Thus, we cannot find that the Immigration Judge abused her discretion in denying the continuance.
 
*2 During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to dismiss proceedings. In support of his motion, the respondent submitted evidence indicating that his October 21, 1996, conviction for burglary and grand theft has been vacated. We have held that if a court vacates an alien’s conviction solely to allow an alien to avoid immigration hardships, the conviction would not be eliminated for immigration purposes. See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003); see also Ali v. U.S. Atty Gen., 443 F.3d 804 (11th Cir. 2006) (finding that a conviction was not vacated on the basis of a substantive or procedural defect simply because a motion for new trial was granted). Although counsel for the respondent argues that the conviction was vacated for procedural and substantive defects,¿FN1º the evidence submitted by the respondent does not identify the basis upon which he sought to vacate the conviction, nor do the orders from the criminal court identify the basis. As the respondent has failed to establish the basis upon which the court vacated his conviction, such conviction remains for immigration purposes. See Ali v. U.S. Atty. Gen., supra, at 813 (upholding the Board’s determination that the alien has the burden of proving that his conviction had been vacated because of a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings); see also, e.g., Rumierz v. Gonzales, 456 F.3d 31 (1st Cir. 2001) (upholding the Board’s determination that an alien bears the burden of proving that he has met the standards under Matter of Pickering, supra).
 
Accordingly, in view of the above, the appeal is dismissed and the motion is denied.
 
Gerald S. Hurwitz
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Statements in a motion are not evidence and thus are not entitled to any evidentiary weight. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Rodriguez v. Holder, 9th Cir., June 16, 2011
2007 WL 2299658 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YESICA VENEGAS RODRIGUEZ
FILE: AXX XX4 847 - SAN DIEGO, CA
July 18, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrea Guerrero, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kathryn E: Stuever
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on January 18, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision finding her subject to removal as charged. In that decision we stated our agreement with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), because she did not have the 5-year period of lawful permanent residence required by section 240A(a)(1) of the Act.¿FN1º The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent alleges that her former counsel failed to file an application for cancellation of removal on her behalf and failed to make the argument that the respondent met the requirement for cancellation of removal found at section 240A(a)(1) because her father’s lawful permanent resident status could be imputed to her. The respondent acknowledges that there was no controlling law supporting her argument of imputed lawful permanent resident status. However, she contends that it is a reasonable extension of Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2005) (holding that a parent’s period of continuous residence may be imputed to a child).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has stated that an inquiry into whether counsel provided ineffective assistance must begin with determining whether competent counsel would have acted otherwise before examining whether an alien was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective actions of counsel. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004). Counsel’s actions must have rendered the proceedings fundamentally unfair such that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting her case. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003). In addition, the alien must show that the alleged ineffectiveness affected the outcome of proceedings. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra, at 973.
 
The respondent has failed to show ineffective assistance on the part of her former counsel. Former counsel’s failure to submit an application for cancellation of removal and support it with an argument as to imputed lawful permanent residence did not constitute deficient performance. At the time of the respondent’s hearing there was no controlling case law supporting the proposed argument. Therefore, it was not unreasonable for her former counsel to fail to make that argument. See Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668, 690 (1984) (holding that in analyzing an ineffectiveness claim, it is proper to judge the reasonableness of counsel’s challenged conduct as of the time of that conduct). Moreover this Board has recently held that the argument proposed by the respondent is without merit. See Matter of Escobar, 24 I&N Dec. 231 (BIA 2007) (holding that a parent’s lawful permanent resident status cannot be imputed to a child for purposes of calculating the 5 years of lawful permanent residence required to establish eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a)(1)).
 
*2 For these reasons, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s eligibility for cancellation of removal was considered even though no application had been filed.

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Order Vacated by IN RE: EDUARDO ALFREDO VERA-MORLAS A.K.A. EDUARDO ALFREDO VERA-MORALES, BIA, January 18, 2008
2007 WL 2299642 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDUARDO ALFREDO VERA-MORLAS A.K.A. EDUARDO ALFREDO VERA-MORALES
FILE: AXX XX8 057 - LANCASTER, CA
July 12, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Edgardo Quintanilla, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angela M. Fontova
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 28, 2007. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), in the exercise of discretion. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not considering the respondent’s alcoholism and drug abuse as a disease. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (e)(5). We find that the respondent’s arguments on appeal fail to establish error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent failed to establish that he warranted cancellation of removal in the exercise of discretion. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7, 11 (BIA 1998) (finding that the Court “must balance the adverse factors evidencing the alien’s undesirability as a permanent resident with the social and humane considerations presented on his behalf to determine whether the granting of… relief appears in the best interest of this country.”). Although the respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge should have considered his alcoholism and drug abuse as a disease, we note that the respondent first raises this argument on appeal and has failed to establish that the documentary evidence submitted with his appellate brief to support this argument was not available and could not have been presented before the Immigration Judge. The Board does not consider evidence first offered on appeal as our review is a review of the record created before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Federenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). Inasmuch as the respondent did not raise this argument before the Immigration Judge and the respondent has not established that the evidence submitted on appeal to support this argument is new and previously unavailable, we decline to consider the respondent’s argument. Furthermore, while the respondent contends that his prior counsel committed ineffective assistance by not raising such an argument before the Immigration Judge, we note that an attorney’s tactical decisions in the preparation and presentation of an alien’s case do not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931,934 (9th Cir. 1986); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The decision of the respondent’s prior counsel to proceed on a different theory of the case does not establish a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel. Lastly, we find insufficient evidence to establish bias on the part of the Immigration Judge or a violation of the respondent’s due process rights. The portion of the transcript quoted in the respondent’s appellate brief does not indicate that the Immigration Judge improperly questioned the respondent or that the Immigration Judge exhibited bias in considering the evidence regarding the respondent’s alcohol and drug abuse.
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, we find that the respondent has failed to establish that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that the adverse factors of record, including his convictions for Use/Influence of a Controlled Substance, Theft, Burglary, Driving Under the Influence, Driving with a Suspended License, and Lewd Conduct, outweighed the favorable factors of record. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the best interests of this country would not be served by granting cancellation of removal to the respondent. See Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581,.584 (BIA 1978). Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Douglas B. Schoppert

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WEN LING JIANG A.K.A. XIAOMEI LIU
FILE: AXX-XX8-994 - SAN FRANCISCO, CA
July 9, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claire H. Kim, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James B. Gildea
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on November 22, 2005, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely. The respondent seeks reopening and remand to pursue her adjustment of status application. The respondent also appears to assert ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely and exceeds the number of motions to reopen permitted under the regulations. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent appears to acknowledge this, as she argues that the Board should take jurisdiction over the motion under our sua sponte authority in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). As noted by the respondent, the Board retains limited authority to reopen cases sua sponte, but this authority is only used in exceptional circumstances. This case does not present exceptional circumstances. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on July 31, 2000. The respondent did not seek to rescind the in absentia order until January 3, 2003. The Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s February 20, 2003 denial of that motion on November 28, 2003. The respondent then waited two years to file a motion to reopen, filing on August 22, 2005. The Board denied that motion in November 22, 2005. Waiting another 1 1/2 years, the respondent filed this untimely motion to reopen. The respondent has not exercised due diligence in pursuing her claim. Furthermore, the respondent raised the issue of her counsel’s fault in her motion to rescind, and the Board addressed the claim in its decision. The respondent did not file a motion to reconsider. The fact that she is now eligible to adjust her status through the interim regulations does not change the fact that all of her motions have been untimely. Accordingly, we find the respondent does not present exceptional circumstances.
 
Equitable tolling of the time limits for motions to reopen is available to aliens who have filed with the Board otherwise untimely motions in which they raise allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). Diligence must be exercised, however. Albillo-De Leon v. Gonzales, 410 F.3d 1090, 1099-1100 (9th Cir. 2005) (diligence must be shown to toll motions deadline). The respondent claims that her attorney informed her that she did not need to appear at the hearing as she could participate in a telephonic hearing. As noted above, the respondent waited more than 2 1/2 years to assert this claim. She was notified immediately of the order, and took no action. Furthermore, this claim was asserted and addressed in the Board’s November 28, 2003, decision, and the respondent did not move to reconsider. Lastly, in the motion filed on August 22, 2005, the respondent did not raise ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that the respondent has not exercised due diligence, and the matter has been addressed previously, and is abandoned by the failure to raise it in the intervening motion.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LINDA MICHEL
FILE: AXX XX5 407 - BALTIMORE, MD
July 9, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Oscar L. Amorow, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Remand; waiver of inadmissibility
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Haiti, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s November 29, 2005, decision insofar as her application for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), was denied. During the pendency of the appeal, she filed a motion asserting that she received ineffective assistance by her former attorney, Mr. Josselin Saint-Preux,¿FN1º regarding the preparation and presentation of her application for section 212(c) relief, and requesting that the record be remanded to permit her an opportunity to supplement the record regarding that application.
 
Regarding the motion, although the respondent asserts ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of Mr. Saint-Preux, her motion fails to satisfy the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) for making such a claim. Attached to the motion is a brief letter from the respondent’s current attorney to Mr. Saint-Preux regarding the representation she received in conjunction with her section 212(c) application, but this is no substitute for an affidavit by the respondent herself of the type discussed in Matter of Lozada, supra. There also has been no showing that the respondent filed a complaint against Mr. Saint-Preux with the appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding the claimed violations of his ethical or legal responsibilities, and she has not explained why she failed to do so. Id.
 
In her motion, she also asks for an opportunity to present additional evidence to support her section 212(c) application. However, she neither submitted nor sufficiently detailed the additional evidence of which she seeks consideration, and she has failed to persuasively explain how the consideration of additional evidence would alter the decision reached in her case. Accordingly, the motion to remand is denied.
 
Regarding the respondent’s appeal, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of section 212(c) relief in this case based on the record before her. Moreover, the appellate arguments being advanced do not persuade us to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, we adopt and affirm and adopt the Immigration Judge’s November 29, 2005, decision and dismiss the respondent’s appeal. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994). The request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2007).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to remand is denied and the appeal is dismissed. The request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2007).
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 2299612 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BERTHA RIVERA DE ZAVALA
FILE: AXX XX9 725 - SAN DIEGO, CA
July 9, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank T. Morell, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christopher J. Reeber
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings; cancellation of removal
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. She appeals an Immigration Judge’s March 22, 2006, decision finding her inadmissible to the United states under section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i), denying her application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), and ordering her removed to Mexico. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a response brief in support of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred by considering whether the respondent, a returning lawful permanent resident of the United States, was inadmissible without first determining whether she had engaged in illegal activity after having departed the United States. See section 101(a)(13)(C)(iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(13)(C)(iii). This argument is unpersuasive, as the Immigration Judge observed that the illegal activity in which the respondent had engaged was the very activity underlying the alien smuggling charge. See I.J. at 6; Exh. 1. Cf. United States v. Tsai, 282 F.3d 690, 696 n.5 (9th Cir. 2002) (holding that the returning lawful permanent resident alien was properly regarded as seeking admission to the United States because he “ha¿dº engaged in illegal activity after having departed the United States,” that being “the alien smuggling activity”). As the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had engaged in illegal activity after departing the United States, he properly found that she was subject to the grounds of inadmissibility enumerated at section 212 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182. See generally section 101(a)(13)(C)(iii) of the Act.
 
The respondent further argues that the Immigration Judge erred in relying on evidence that had been entered into the record as a result of former counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance. However, the respondent has neither substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), nor demonstrated a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” See Granados-Oseguera v. Gonzales, 464 F.3d 993, 997-99 (9th Cir. 2006); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002). Cf. Exh. 27 (confirming that current counsel was substituted in as attorney of record on June 9, 2005); Tr. at 29, 134 (current counsel’s confirmation that the respondent had filed no objections to the DHS’s proffered evidence). See also Tr. at 30 (current counsel’s explanation regarding why he failed to obtain copies of exhibits included in the record of proceedings prior to the June 30, 2005, hearing). We do not find merit in the respondent’s unsupported assertion that the Immigration Judge deemed prior counsel’s assistance to have been ineffective, particularly in light of the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent would be bound by the actions of prior counsel. See Tr. at 115 (Immigration Judge noted that the respondent was bound by the actions of prior counsel). Cf. Tr. at 113 (vague comment regarding effectiveness of prior representation, which does not reveal that the Immigration Judge found prior counsel to have provided ineffective assistance). Moreover, the respondent has not demonstrated that she suffered prejudice as a result of prior counsel’s failure to object to the DHS’s proffered evidence, as such evidence is routinely received in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.7(a) (providing that an Immigration Judge may receive into evidence prior statements such as those received during the respondent’s removal proceedings). See also Tr. at 124-27, 148-56 (evidencing respondent’s opportunity to cross-examine preparers of two key documents included in the record or proceedings). We therefore find that the respondent has failed to establish that she experienced prejudicial ineffective assistance of counsel during the course of her removal proceedings. See generally Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 We agree, however, with the respondent’s contention that the Immigration Judge should not have relied on the written admissions and concession submitted by former counsel (Exh. 6), as the Immigration Judge had indicated that the respondent would not be bound by the withdrawn admission and concession. See I.J. at 15-16; Tr. at 19, 113. Nevertheless, the Immigration Judge’s reliance on the withdrawn submission is harmless error, as the record supports the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent was involved in alien smuggling activity, as defined in section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Act, even if we disregard the respondent’s earlier concession.¿FN1º See, e.g., Exh. 18 (Report of Investigation, indicating that the respondent previously stated that she was asked to provide transportation to the smugglee, that she realized while in the vehicle that the passport held by the smugglee did not in fact pertain to the her, and that the driver of the vehicle had accompanied the respondent on the trip to Mexico simply because the respondent did not feel well enough to drive); Tr. at 67-68, 82-83, 97 (respondent’s testimony that, prior to her departure for Mexico, her husband requested that she transport the smugglee into the United States, and that she asked her husband about the smugglee’s immigration status and was told that the individual was a United States citizen), 83, 87, 93-94, 96, 105 (respondent’s testimony that she asked to see the smugglee’s documents, and realized that the photograph did not look like the smugglee), 148-56 (respondent’s counsel’s cross-examination of the officer who created the Report of Investigation).
 
The Immigration Judge properly distinguished the respondent’s participation in the smuggling activity under consideration in the instant case from the alien’s limited connection to the smuggling activity at issue in Altamirano v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 586 (9th Cir. 2005). See I.J. at 14-15. Whereas, in Altamirano v. Gonzales, supra, the alien simply had knowledge of the smuggling plan and was present in the vehicle in which her family members were attempting to smuggle an undocumented alien, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent was an active participant in the scheme to procure the smugglee’s entrance into the United States. See I.J. at 14-15. See also Tr. at 82-83, 97, 100 (respondent’s testimony that her husband asked her and a neighbor to transport his niece’s friend into the United States). Moreover, in Altamirano v. Gonzales, supra, the Immigration Judge did not question the alien’s credibility. See Altamirano v. Gonzales, supra, at 589 n.2. In contrast, the Immigration Judge gave specific, cogent reasons for disbelieving the respondent’s version of events. Notably, although the respondent disclaims involvement in the scheme to bring the smugglee into the United States, and testified that she did not know of the smugglee’s lack of authorization to enter the United States until after they were placed in secondary inspection, the record reveals that she previously had stated to an immigration officer that she had been asked to transport the smugglee from Mexico into the United States, and that upon picking up the smugglee she realized that the passport that the smugglee had in her possession did not in fact pertain to the smugglee. See I.J. at 10-12, 14, 17-18. Compare Tr. at 50 with Exh. 18 and Tr. at 142-56.
 
*3 Similarly, the respondent’s appellate assertion that the driver of the automobile involved in the smuggling incident was responsible for arranging the transportation of the smugglee is belied by her testimony before the Immigration Judge. See Tr. at 82-83 (respondent’s assertion that she did not ask the driver about the smugglee’s immigration status prior to departing the United States for Mexico because he “did not know ¿the smuggleeº. It ¿wasº not on the side of ¿the driverº”). Based on the foregoing, we find that the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent was an active participant in the plan to transport the smugglee into the United States is not clearly erroneous, and we therefore affirm the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent engaged in the illegal activity of alien smuggling after departing the United States. We consequently affirm the Immigration Judge’s corollary finding that the respondent is inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Act.
 
Turning next to the respondent’s application for relief from removal, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a)(2) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(2). The record does not support the respondent’s appellate contention that, but for brief and casual departures from the United States, she has continually resided in the United States since she was purportedly admitted as a nonimmigrant visitor when she was approximately 2 years of age. Cf. Exh. 21 (indicating that respondent first entered the United States in 1995 without inspection, and noting that she gave birth to three children in Mexico between the years of 1980 and 1986). Furthermore, we are unpersuaded by the respondent’s unsubstantiated assertion that Mexican culture requires a wife to live with and follow the direction of her husband and that, therefore, the husband’s period of residence should be imputed to the wife pursuant to an extension of the holding in Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2005). The record supports the Immigration Judge’s finding that the relevant date of admission is the date of the respondent’s admission to the United States as a lawful permanent resident, which was April 18, 2002. See I.J. at 18-20; Exh. 8. Pursuant to the “stop-time rule,” the respondent’s opportunity to accrue the requisite 7 years of residence after admission in any status halted when she was issued a Notice to Appear on April 9, 2004. See Exh. 1. See also sections 240A(a)(1), (d)(1) of the Act. The respondent therefore accrued a maximum of approximately 2 years of continuous, post-admission residence, rather than the necessary 7 years. See I.J. at 21. The Immigration Judge thus properly determined that the respondent is unable to establish statutory eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act.
 
*4 Finally, the respondent also asserts on appeal that the immigration court’s policy regarding change of venue is unconstitutional because it requires that an alien concede removability before a motion for change of venue will even be considered. However, we are without authority to consider such constitutional challenges. See, e.g., Matter of Fede, 20 I&N Dec. 35, 36 (BIA 1989).
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Likewise, any confusion regarding whether the smugglee was the niece of the respondent’s husband, or a friend of such niece (I.J. at 10, 14, 16-17; Tr. at 83), also amounts to harmless error, as the record supports the Immigration Judge’s ultimate resolution of the respondent’s case irrespective of the identity of the smugglee.


2007 WL 2299612 (BIA)

2007 WL 2197561 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GLENWOOD BROWN A.K.A. GEORGE BROWN
FILE: AXX XX5 315 - BOSTON, MA
July 3, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
CERTIFICATION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
In a decision dated December 2,2004, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed to Bermuda in absentia, and deemed his pending application for relief to be abandoned. The respondent thereafter filed an untimely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, which the Immigration Judge denied on August 11, 2005. The respondent then appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen, and in a decision dated March 16, 2006, we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for an evidentiary hearing on the issue of whether the respondent’s former counsel’s assistance was in fact ineffective. At the remanded evidentiary hearing, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent’s prior counsel had not provided ineffective assistance and, moreover, that the respondent had departed the United States prior to submitting the motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge has now certified the record back to the Board in a decision dated February 7, 2007, in order for us to determine whether the respondent’s departure from the United States served to divest the immigration court of jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
The respondent does not challenge the Immigration Judge’s finding that he physically departed the United States in November 2004, prior to his December 2, 2004, removal hearing.¿FN1º We therefore accept the Immigration Judge’s factual finding that the respondent so departed the United States, as the respondent has failed to establish that such finding was clearly erroneous. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); I.J. (February 7, 2007, decision) at 3. The regulations preclude the filing of a motion to reopen with an immigration judge by or on behalf of an individual who is the subject of removal proceedings, subsequent to such individual’s departure from the United States. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). See also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d) (governing motions filed before the Board). Consequently, the respondent’s departure from the United States in November 2004, while he was the subject of removal proceedings, had the effect of depriving the immigration court of jurisdiction over his subsequently-filed motion to reopen. As the Immigration Judge is without jurisdiction to grant the motion to reopen, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
In addition, the respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s finding that he received actual, in-person, prior notice of his December 2, 2004, removal hearing, which included a warning regarding the consequences of failure to appear. See I.J. (February 7, 2007, decision) at 4. Moreover, we find ample support in the record for the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s former counsel did not render ineffective assistance to the respondent. See I.J. (February 7, 2007, decision) at 3-5 (finding no ineffective assistance of counsel, and recounting respondent’s assertion that he would have departed the United States prior to his removal hearing irrespective of his attorney’s advice); Unidentified Appellate Submissions (January 4, 2006, letter from Massachusetts Office of the Bar Counsel, indicating that no disciplinary action would be taken against respondent’s former counsel and closing respondent’s file regarding his grievance against former counsel, and January 24, 2006, letter from the Massachusetts Board of Bar Overseers, upholding Office of Bar Counsel’s decision to close respondent’s file). The respondent has thus failed to establish that exceptional circumstances prevented him from attending his December 2, 2004, removal hearing. As the respondent neglected to attend his December 2, 2004, removal hearing notwithstanding the Immigration Judge’s explicit, in-person admonition regarding the consequences of such failure to appear, and the respondent has not established exceptional circumstances excusing his failure to appear, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), (iii). We will therefore sua sponte vacate that portion of our March 16, 2006, decision which remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Our decision dated March 16, 2006, is vacated to the extent that it remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for an evidentiary hearing on the issue of whether the respondent’s former counsel rendered ineffective assistance.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent is ordered removed from the United States pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s December 2, 2004, decision.
 
Edward R. Grant

See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (indicating that the Board may not engage in factfinding in the course of deciding appeals).

2007 WL 2197535 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LUIS SERRANO-FONSECA
FILE: AXX XX1 375 - HOUSTON, TX
July 2, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gene P. Tausk, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John McPhail
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s February 22, 2007, decision finding him removable from the United States and pretermitting his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229b. The appeal is dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that he was not convicted of an aggravated felony. Also, he avers that the Immigration Judge erred in ordering him removed during a master calendar hearing. In that regard, he asserts that the Immigration Judge should have allowed him additional time to present his case. Last, he claims that he was ineffectively represented by his former counsels.
 
The record reveals that the respondent sustained a 2003 conviction for delivery of marijuana in a drug free zone.¿FN1º See TEX. HEALTH & SAFETY CODE §§ 481.120, 481.134. The Immigration Judge determined that this offense qualified as an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B), and we find no reversible error in that determination. According to the United States Supreme Court, a state drug offense qualifies as a drug trafficking crime under 18 U.S.C. § 924(c) and, by extension, an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act if the offense would have been punishable as a felony under the Federal Controlled Substances Act (the “CSA”), 21 U.S.C. § 802 et seq. Lopez v. Gonzales, 549 U.S. 47, 127 S. Ct. 625, 630-31 (2006). The Texas offense of delivery of marijuana is comparable to the federal offense of marijuana distribution, which is punishable under 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(D) by a term of imprisonment of up to 5 years, making it a federal “felony.” Lopez v. Gonzales, supra, at 631 n.7 (holding that “for purposes of § 924(c)(2) the crimes the CSA defines as ‘felonies’ are those crimes to which it assigns a punishment exceeding one year’s imprisonment.”). 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(4) provides an exception for one who distributed a “small amount of marijuana for no remuneration” however; documents in the record reflect that the respondent transferred marijuana to a minor in exchange for a sum of United States currency.¿FN2º Hence, the offense did involve marijuana “trafficking” in the conventional sense of that term. Accordingly, we agree that as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony, the respondent is removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), and statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(3).
 
*2 Also, we do not find error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to proceed with the respondent’s removal hearing and order him removed during a master calendar hearing. The respondent had appeared before the Immigration Judge on 2 occasions. During the respondent’s second master calendar hearing, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent was ineligible for the relief that he sought and thus ordered him removed. There is nothing in the record to indicate that the Immigration Judge had acted in an inappropriate manner by proceeding with the removal hearing.
 
Last, the respondent raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, but fails to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also, Lara v. Trominiski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000). While he submitted copies of letters that he allegedly mailed to his former attorneys, there is nothing in the record to show that the letters had been delivered or received by his former counsels. Furthermore, he has not filed a complaint with the necessary entered into with his former counsels. Based on the record before us, we do not find that the respondent was ineffectively represented.
 
In conclusion, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is removable as charged and does not appear to be eligible for any form of relief. Accordingly, his appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record also reflects that on January 4, 2007, the respondent pied guilty to attempted possession of a controlled substance.

2

Pursuant to 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(4), a defendant who demonstrates that he distributed “a small amount of marihuana for no remuneration” may be punished as a misdemeanant rather than a felon. 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(4) does not define a discrete offense, however; rather, it is a sentencing provision that describes a “mitigating exception” to the otherwise-applicable 5-year statutory maximum called for by 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(D). See, e.g., United States v. Hamlin, 319 F.3d 666, 670-71 (4th Cir. 2003) (federal prosecutor seeking a 5-year sentence against a marijuana distributor not required by Apprendi v. New Jersey, 530 U.S. 466 (2000), to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the amount of marijuana distributed was “large” or that remuneration was present); United States v. Outen, 286 F.3d 622, 638 (2d Cir. 2002) (same). Thus, it is the defendant who must prove the additional facts (i.e., the “smallness” of the amount of marijuana and the absence of remuneration) that trigger the exception. In the absence of any evidentiary showing by the respondent that he would have qualified for this mitigating exception had he been prosecuted federally, we conclude that the proper federal analogue for his state crime is felony marijuana distribution. Cf. Matter of P-F-, 20 I&N Dec. 661, 663 n.2 (BIA 1993).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GURINDER SINGH
FILE: AXX XX3 053 - SAN FRANCISCO, CA
June 29, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martin Resendez Guajardo, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(C), I&NAct ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C)º - Controlled substance trafficker
  
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s March 7, 2006, decision ordering the respondent removed from the United States as an alien who the Attorney General knows or has reason to believe is or has been an illicit trafficker in any controlled substance. See section 212(a)(2)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C). The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7).
 
The respondent was apprehended in May 2005, when he applied for admission at San Francisco International Airport as a returning lawful permanent resident. At that time the respondent was paroled into the United States for deferred inspection. His parole was subsequently revoked, a Notice to Appear was issued and he was placed in removal proceedings. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the court documents of record support the immigration inspector’s conclusion that at the time of the respondent’s attempted entry into the United States, there was “reason to believe” that he was or had been an illicit trafficker in marijuana or cannabis. Id.; Fla. Stat. § 893.13(1)(c)(2). It is noted for the record that the respondent conceded that he sold marijuana in Florida in 1995. Tr. at 12.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he plead nolo contendere in his criminal proceedings, and therefore was not convicted within the meaning of the Immigration and Nationality Act, and thus not removable or inadmissible. This argument is without merit. The ground of inadmissibility under which the respondent was charged, is not one that requires the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) to prove an actual conviction exists under drug trafficking laws in order to sustain the charge, despite the respondent’s assertions to the contrary. See section 212(a)(2)(C); see also Molina v. Ashcroft, 368 F.3d 1206 (9th Cir. 2004); Alarcon-Serrano v. INS, 220 F.3d 1116 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
The respondent also claims that his prior attorney was ineffective. A claim based on ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the claim be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and, (3) that the claim reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553,556 (BIA 2003); cf. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 It is noted that respondent has failed to meet the above-noted requirements of prongs one and three. Additionally, although the respondent did forward a letter to prior counsel on October 31, 2006, no response is contained in the record of proceedings currently before this Board. It should also be noted that the record contains a letter from the respondent’s prior counsel, which serves as notification of his withdrawal. This letter states that on five occasions prior counsel attempted to contact the respondent and further, that the Respondent owes a substantial amount of money to his prior counsel.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Gerald S. Hurwitz
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 2299677 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: REY DAVID MUNOZ-VALLES A.K.A. REY MUNOZ
FILE: AXX XX2 044 - EL PASO, TX
June 28, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Stephen M. Ruhle
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case shall be remanded. The respondent has asserted ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has requested that the Board summarily affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The DHS argues that the respondent failed to meet the requirements as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The record of proceeding contains an EOIR-28, indicating that the respondent was represented by counsel at his hearing before the Immigration Judge. The respondent’s counsel was informed by the Immigration Judge of the due date for the respondent’s application for cancellation relief (Tr. at 36). The application was not filed by the March 16, 2007, deadline and by written decision dated March 19, 2007, the Immigration Judge deemed the application as abandoned.
 
In asserting ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has indicated that he was surprised to receive the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent asserts that he was under the impression that his attorney would comply with the deadline. The respondent has included copies of several letters he sent to his attorney via certified mail indicating that he was upset, and seeking clarification. We find that the respondent, who is pro se on appeal, has substantially complied with the Lozada requirements. The respondent’s letters indicate that there was an issue over respondent’s lack of full payment to the attorney of record. However, there is no letter of request to withdraw as respondent’s attorney. In fact, the record of proceeding does not show any further communication with the court by the respondent’s attorney of record after the December 19, 2006, hearing. Upon review of the record as a whole, we find that although the respondent has not fully complied with the requirements as set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, he has clearly demonstrated that he was prejudiced. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 533 (BIA 2003).
 
Finally, The respondent contests his detention and raises issues related to the determination of his bond. We note that any such issues are not properly before this Board as such concerns shall be separate and apart from any removal hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(d). We therefore remand the record to the Immigration Judge to afford the respondent a further opportunity to submit an application for cancellation relief based on the aforementioned.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley

2007 WL 2074457 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: REYNALDO PRECILLA CARADANG
FILE: AXX XX6 932 - SAN FRANCISCO, CA
June 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Diana Cobar, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael D. Steinberg
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 11182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
 
  
APPLICATION: Section 237(a)(1)(H) waiver
 
On March 20, 2006, an Immigration Judge issued a decision finding the respondent removable as charged under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A), as an alien inadmissible based on fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i). The Immigration Judge did not find that the respondent was subject to removal based on the charge under 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act as he was not an arriving alien. The Immigration Judge rejected the respondent’s request for a waiver under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Act of the sustained ground of removal, and ordered him removed to the Philippines.
 
The respondent appeals, asserting ineffective assistance of counsel. He moves to remand the proceedings so that the Immigration Judge can consider evidence relevant to the fraud underpinning his removability charge¿FN1º. The motion to remand will be denied, and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent sets forth his claim of ineffective assistance in accordance with the framework established in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition to the procedural requirements, however, he must also establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of counsel’s ineffectiveness. See Id. at 640. The respondent must demonstrate that counsel’s performance was so inadequate that it “may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004) (internal citation omitted). Here, the respondent asserts that he provided former counsel with evidence that, unbeknownst to him, his wife had previously entered into a marriage that was never terminated. He contends that, since his marriage was void ab initio, he was not in fact married when he entered the United States and thus did not make a false statement that he was the unmarried son of a lawful permanent resident. The respondent asserts that former counsel disregarded the evidence proving the marriage was void and instead advised him to admit he was legally married and pursue a waiver under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Act. He reasons that the evidence of his void marriage is new since counsel’s ineffective assistance prevented its submission earlier in the proceedings.
 
*2 The underlying removal charge at issue here, section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act, provides:
Any alien who, by fraud or willfully misrepresenting a material fact, seeks to procure (or has sought to procure or has procured) a visa, other documentation, or admission into the United States or other benefit provided under this Act is inadmissible.
 
Section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act. The respondent testified that when he entered the United States in 1995, he told immigration officials that he was unmarried despite marrying his wife in 1986, because he was uncertain whether the marriage was valid. Tr. at 16, 18-19. Further, when he filed a visa petition in 1996 on behalf of his wife, following his return to the Philippines and a second marriage to that same wife, he did not disclose the 1986 marriage. Id. at 19. Since the respondent admitted that he was unsure whether he was a party to a valid marriage, his assertion to immigration officials that he was not married, and his failure to disclose the fact of the 1986 marriage on the visa petition for his wife, were willful misrepresentations of a material fact in order to obtain visas. The dispositive factor is the respondent’s conduct in averring unequivocally that he was unmarried when actually he had entered into a marriage that may have been valid. That conduct constituted a willful misrepresentation to obtain an immigration benefit, irrespective of the respondent’s subsequent discovery that the marriage was not legitimate.
 
Since the respondent admitted that he had willfully misrepresented his marital status on two occasions, any failure by former counsel to submit evidence that the marriage was void did not result in prejudice because such evidence could not cure the misrepresentation. Thus, the respondent cannot sustain a claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel, and we see no basis to remand the record for consideration of the evidence which would not alter the outcome. We further find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent was removable, or that he was ineligible for a waiver under section 237(a)(1)(H). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposed the motion. The Immigration Judge, noting that he lacked jurisdiction over the case since the appeal had vested jurisdiction with the Board, forwarded the motion to us.

2007 WL 2074467 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ALBERTO MEDINA-RONDON A.K.A. JOSE MEDINA
FILE: AXX XX7 788 - LOS FRESNOS, TX
June 21, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Finees M. Casado, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gregory J. Ball,
Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on March 30, 2007, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider that decision. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.39; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(G) (summary dismissal of appeal appropriate where appeal is barred based on waiver of right to appeal).
 
The respondent now suggests that his waiver of appeal was invalid because of erroneous legal advice he was given. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001) (a party wishing to challenge the validity of an appeal waiver may file either a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge or an appeal directly with the Board). He argues that he received ineffective assistance from a professional inmate legal assistance group. However, he has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he or she was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has provided no affidavit setting forth the agreement with this legal organization, has not shown that this group has been given the opportunity to respond to the allegations, and has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority. The assertion in the motion that the respondent intends to comply with the procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel only if the case is first reopened, see Respondent’s Motion, at page 5, is insufficient as a reason to excuse the failure to comply with the Lozada requirements.
 
*2 Moreover, the respondent does not explain why he did not raise the claim of ineffective assistance in his appeal, when he knew or should have known of the alleged erroneous advice and had already retained counsel at that point. Consequently, we find no error in our prior decision summarily dismissing the appeal. See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir. 2000)(the decision to reopen proceedings is a discretionary decision, and this court applies a highly deferential abuse-of-discretion standard when reviewing the BIA’s denial of a motion to reopen).
 
We therefore lack jurisdiction over this case, and we find no error in our prior decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Accordingly, the motion is denied and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.52, 1240.53, and 1241.31.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2007 WL 2074476 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SYLVESTER ROBERT ALLEN
FILE: AXX XX6 126 - SAN PEDRO, CA
June 20, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaak Olesk, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - Nonimmigrant - remained longer than permitted (withdrawn)
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 8, 2007, is affirmed. On appeal, the respondent contends that he was subject to ineffective assistance of counsel by his criminal attorney. The pendency of post-conviction motions or other forms of collateral attacks, not constituting direct appeals, do not serve to negate the finality of the conviction or the charge of deportability, unless and until the conviction has been overturned pursuant to that motion. See Grageda v. INS, 12 F.3d 919, 921 (9th Cir. 1993)(holding that collateral attacks on convictions in state court have no impact on their finality, and finding that Immigration Judge did not err in denying request for continuance so respondent could pursue motion to vacate conviction in California state court); Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894 (BIA 1994); see also Okabe v. INS, 671 F.2d 863 (5th Cir. 1982); Aguilera-Enriquez v. INS, 516 F.2d 565 (6th Cir. 1975), cert. denied, 423 U.S. 1050 (1976). In addition, the respondent is precluded from collaterally attacking his criminal record of conviction in a removal proceeding. See Urbina-Mauricio v. INS, 989 F.2d 1085 (9th Cir. 1993). The respondent also contends that his immigration counsel rendered ineffective assistance during the removal hearing. The respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In some cases, the Ninth Circuit has not required strict compliance with Lozada requirements, especially when the record shows “a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir.2002). However, the respondent’s contention is conclusory in nature and he has made no such showing. See Puga v. Chertoff, 488F.3d812 2007 WL 1500308 (9th Cir. May 24, 2007).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent suggests that he may be eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), but it is evident that his criminal conviction renders him statutorily ineligible for such relief pursuant to section 240A(b)(1)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1)(C). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 2074476 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 2074553 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KHURRAM JEHANGIR KHAN
FILE: AXX XX2 144 - DETROIT
June 15, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sufen Hilf, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on October 31, 2006, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our July 10, 2006, decision in which we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s October 25, 2005, decision denying the respondent’s October 11, 2005, motions to reconsider and reopen proceedings.
 
On January 26, 2007, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen based, in part, on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent contends that this motion is timely filed. See Respondent’s Motion at 5. We disagree. Our July 10, 2006, decision (dismissing the respondent’s appeal) remains the final administrative order in this case.¿FN1º See Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999) (stating that an administrative decision becomes final when the Board reaches a decision on appeal). Thus, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before October 10, 2006. Also, as the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen (October 11, 2005, motion to reopen and reconsider filed before the Immigration Judge), his motion is number barred. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7) (stating that a party may file only one motion to reopen, and that such a motion must be filed within 90 days following the date of the final administrative decision); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
Therefore, the instant motion is time and number barred unless the respondent can establish that he falls within an exception to these statutory and regulatory limitations on motions to reopen. The United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, has held that equitable tolling can apply to the time limits on motions to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Tapia-Martinez v. Gonzales, 482 F.3d 417 (6th Cir. 2007). Although, the Sixth Circuit has not specifically held that equitable tolling applies to numerical limitations on motions to reopen. Id.
 
The pivotal issue before us is whether the respondent has established that equitable tolling is warranted based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent asserts that all three of his prior attorneys - Mr. Nadadur Sampath Kumar, Ms. Vera Weisz, and Mr. David Wegner were ineffective. The respondent has complied with the procedural steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Respondent’s Motion at 7-8, Attachments 6, 8, and 17. We need not consider the claims raised against all three attorneys because we find that Mr. Wegner’s representation was ineffective and prejudiced the respondent’s removal proceedings. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Given the respondent’s due diligence, we find equitable toiling is warranted in this case.
 
*2 On September 15, 2005, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had abandoned his applications for relief because they were not filed on or before the August 12, 2005, deadline and ordered the respondent removed from the United States. Mr. Wegner filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge explaining that he did not timely file the respondent’s applications because he believed that since the date of the merits hearing was postponed, the applications deadline would also be postponed. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion and on July 10, 2006, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision. Mr. Wegner cited no authority for his mistaken belief that the Immigration Judge’s application filing deadline was altered when the merits hearing was postponed. Yet, he continued to inform the respondent that the Immigration Judge’s decision was “totally unjust.” See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Attachment 8(c)(i) (Mr. Wegner’s September 27, 2005, letter to the respondent). Mr. Wegner continued to make this argument when he filed a motion to reconsider with this Board on August 9, 2006. But for Mr. Wegner’s mistake, the respondent’s adjustment application would have been timely filed. The respondent’s motion is supported by evidence that he is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Attachment 16.
 
The respondent has also demonstrated that he exercised due diligence from the time he discovered Mr. Wegner’s ineffectiveness. After we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider on October 31, 2006, the respondent apparently began to question Mr. Wegner’s judgment and conduct. By November 2006, he retained new counsel, filed a Freedom of Information Act request, and on January 26, 2007, he filed the instant motion to reopen.
 
Finally, we come to the issue of whether the respondent is precluded from applying for adjustment of status because he failed to voluntarily depart pursuant to a 120-day grant of voluntary departure by Department of Homeland Security (DHS) Officer John Brechtel on July 19, 2003. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Attachments 4 and 6(c)(i) (Form 1-210). In light of our recent decision in Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87 (BIA 2007), and given the facts and circumstances of this case, we find that the respondent’s failure to depart pursuant to the DHS order was not voluntary. Notably, the respondent has proffered evidence showing that Office Brechtel may have rescinded the grant of voluntary departure. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Attachments 6(d) and (e), and 7. Additionally, there is no evidence that voluntary departure as granted by the DHS in this case is contractual in nature as is the voluntary departure granted by an Immigration Judge. Matter of Zmijewska, spura at 93.
 
Based on the foregoing, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand the record to that the respondent can apply for relief from removal.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has informed us that he filed a Petition for Review of our July 10, 2006, decision with the Sixth Circuit. See Respondent’s Motion at 5. See Matter of Susma, 22 I&N Dec. 947 (BIA 1999) (finding that the filing of a petition for review in circuit court does not extend the time limit for filing a motion to reopen).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEGUNDO ANTONIO RODRIGUEZ RODRIGUEZ
FILE: AXX XX2 683 - NEWARK
June 15, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sigang Li, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on February 14, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s November 9, 2004, decision finding that the respondent had abandoned his applications for relief and ordering him deported to the Dominican Republic.
 
On May 1, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen these proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance by his prior counsel, Ms. Vinaya Saijwani. The motion was not filed within the statutory and regulatory 90-day time limit for filing a motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent argues that we should equitably toll the 90-day time limit on filing motions to reopen based on his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, has held that aliens raising an ineffective assistance claim in a motion can have the time limits on motions equitably toiled. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 248 (3rd Cir. 2005).
 
We find that equitable tolling is warranted in this case. The respondent has substantially complied with the procedural steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and has demonstrated that Ms. Saijwani’s conduct has prejudiced his case. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Ms. Saijwani admitted that due to her personal problems she failed to timely file the respondent’s applications for relief from removal. Nevertheless, she continued to represent the respondent on appeal and never asserted that her own conduct constituted ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also contends that Ms. Saijwani failed to inform him of our February 14, 2006, decision until after he was detained on April 23, 2007. Ms. Saijwani, who has been notified of the respondent’s claim, has not challenged the respondent’s assertions of misconduct. The respondent, by and through new counsel, exercised due diligence by filing the instant motion to reopen within days of learning of our prior order.
 
Although the respondent asserts that he is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), he has not proffered his application in support of this motion. In light of the facts of this case and the failure to the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) to object or otherwise respond to this motion, we will grant the respondent’s reopening to afford him the opportunity to apply for relief from removal. While we find reopening appropriate in this case, the respondent must establish his eligibility for cancellation of removal despite his 1991 conviction for conspiracy to illegally acquire and possess food stamps.
 
*2 The respondent also claims that Ms. Saijwani should have filed a motion to terminate so that he could pursue his pending naturalization application. See Respondent’s Motion at 4. While the respondent may file such a motion in remanded proceedings, we make no judgment on its merits given the respondent’s 1991 conviction. See generally Matter of Acosta Hidalgo, 24 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2007) (removal proceedings may only be terminated pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1239.2(f) where the DHS has presented an affirmative communication attesting to the alien’s prima facie eligibility for naturalization). The appropriate orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent, with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 2074428 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARMANDO BARRAGAN-TORO
FILE: AXX XX6 596 - LOS ANGELES
June 14, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonio M. Zaldana, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lee P. Crystal
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us when in a decision dated May 12, 1997, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 12, 1997, as untimely filed and not properly before us. On November 24, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, and seeking to reapply for relief pursuant to the former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), in light of the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001) (restrictions on section 212(c) relief do not apply retroactively to aliens who pled guilty prior to April 24, 1996, the effective date of the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (AEDPA)). The respondent’s motion is granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Although the motion is untimely filed, the respondent claims that he should receive the benefit of “equitable tolling” of the filing deadline because the attorney who earlier represented him, Jose M. Quinones, who was since suspended from practice before the Board, failed to file a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s February 12, 1997, decision, after allegedly placing the matter in the hands of a “notario,” and then failed to tell him of our final order. The respondent has not complied with all the procedural requirements we set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, but as this case falls under the jurisdiction of the United States Circuit Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which has held that rigid adherence to the Lozada requirements is not always required, we find the respondent has presented a prima facie case of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525-526 (9th Cir 2000) (Lozada requirements generally reasonable, but full compliance is not required if the record independently shows that the “ineffective assistance” claim is legitimate and substantial); see also Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir 1999) (the court found the statute of limitations to reopen an order of deportation is equitably tolled where the alien’s late petition is the result of the deceptive actions by a notary posing as an attorney). Consequently, even though the respondent’s motion is untimely filed, applying Ninth Circuit precedent, we find that the respondent’s situation falls within the Ninth Circuit’s “equitable tolling” policy. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001).
 
*2 A “motion to reopen proceedings seeks to reopen proceedings so that new evidence can be presented and so that a new decision can be entered, normally after a further evidentiary hearing.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 403 (BIA 1991). Such a motion may not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Finally, an alien must establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought before a motion to reopen will be granted. Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 482 (BIA 1996). Here, the respondent has demonstrated that consistent with the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, supra, and the new section 212(c) regulation found at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(a), he is no longer precluded from establishing his eligibility for a waiver on inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act, and that reopening of the proceedings is warranted.
 
Accordingly, the Board’s May 12, 1997, decision will be vacated, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings to allow the respondent the opportunity to establish his eligibility for a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to section 212(c) of the Act, or any other form of relief to which he may be entitled.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s May 12, 1997, decision is vacated, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Edward G. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
