** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HOSSAM HANAFY MAHMOUD EL AMWARY A.K.A. HOSSAM HANAFY MAHMOUD EL AMRAWI
FILE: AXX XX7 055 - CHICAGO
June 14, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard A. Bimbaum, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Seth B. Fitter
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status
 
The respondent has filed an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s order dated February 24, 2006. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Egypt. Prior to being placed in removal proceedings, he married a United States citizen. The respondent’s wife filed an immediate relative visa petition (Form I-130) on behalf of the respondent on September 16, 2003. The respondent filed an adjustment of status application at the same time. The Immigration Judge granted the respondent several continuances to allow the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) time to adjudicate the Form I-130 visa petition (Tr. at 1-2, 7, 10-12, 13-14). On August 14, 2005, the Immigration Judge stated that if the visa petition were still pending at the next scheduled hearing date (February 24, 2006), the respondent could move for another continuance (Tr. at 14).
 
On February 24, 2006, a new Immigration Judge was assigned to the matter (Tr. at 15). The respondent’s attorney advised the Immigration Judge that the DHS had instructed him that it would not adjudicate the respondent’s visa petition until after the Immigration Judge issued a decision in the case (Tr. at 18-22). The respondent’s attorney inquired about the possibility of an order of administrative closure, and the attorney for the DHS opposed such an order (Tr. at 16-17). When voluntary departure was raised as a potential form of relief, the respondent was asked what he wanted to do (Tr. at 23-26). The respondent stated that he wanted a continuance (Tr. at 26). A brief recess was held, and after the recess the respondent’s counsel advised the Immigration Judge that the respondent said he wanted a continuance (Tr. at 27-28). The Immigration Judge concluded that since the DHS was apparently refusing to adjudicate the visa petition, the Immigration Judge would have to issue an order of removal or an order of voluntary departure (Tr. at 28). The respondent was then qualified for voluntary departure and granted voluntary departure (Tr. at 29-35). Through counsel, the respondent waived appeal (Tr. at 36).
 
The respondent now alleges that he received ineffective assistance of counsel at his hearing because counsel should have moved for a continuance and if the motion were denied, should have appealed from the order of the Immigration Judge. The respondent contends that under controlling law, the Immigration Judge was obligated to grant continuances until the visa petition was adjudicated. See Benslimane v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 828 (7th Cir. 2005); Subhan v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 591 (7th Cir. 2004).
 
*2 In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), affd, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), we held that ineffective assistance claims require: (1) that the respondent submit an affidavit detailing the agreement between the respondent and his counsel; (2) that the attorney whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the respondent state whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the respondent establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims. See Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498, 501 (7th Cir. 2001). He has complied with the technical requirements and has also established that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance that rendered the proceedings unfair. The respondent’s former counsel should have been aware of controlling law that required the Immigration Judge to grant continuances in cases such as this one. Because of his former counsel’s actions, the respondent waived appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision, a decision that effectively, and improperly, denied the respondent an opportunity to have a hearing on his application for adjustment of status.
 
Accordingly, we will sustain the appeal and will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The order of the Immigration Judge dated February 24, 2006, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the issuance of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: SAMUEL SIGMUND CAMPBELL
FILE: AXX XX6 666 - PHILADELPHIA
June 13, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa A. Baird, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bruce B. Dizengoff
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; section 212(c) waiver
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from a December 19, 2005, decision of an Immigration Judge, denying his motion to reopen proceedings following the previous entry of an in absentia order of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be sustained.
 
The respondent argues that he was deprived of due process as a consequence of ineffective assistance of his former counsel and as such, he has not had a fair hearing in these proceedings. See section 240(b)(4)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(4)(A); see also Xu Yong Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127 (3rd Cir. 2001). The respondent has not complied with the mandatory requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and we thus decline to sustain the appeal based on ineffective assistance of counsel, for the reasons set forth in Matter of Lozada at 639-40.
 
We will, however, under the unusual circumstances of this case, remand the record for further proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). No single circumstance, on its own, leads us to conclude that a remand is necessary, but the sum total of the circumstances presented in this case leads us to the conclusion that the exceptional circumstances necessary for sua sponte reopening (or, analogously, remand) exist in this case. Cf. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We first note that the respondent does not have appeared to attempt to delay these proceedings in any way. Cf. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 108 (1988) (discussing the heavy burden a party filing a motion to reopen bears and the government’s legitimate interest in discouraging delays in immigration litigation). We also take notice of the asserted recent death of the respondent’s daughter (Respondent’s Br. at 5). Additionally, we agree with the respondent that there is a possibility that the Immigration Judge reached a conclusion in this case before all the evidence was presented (see, e.g. Tr. at 105, quoted at page 5 of the respondent’s brief), and we cannot be fully confident that the respondent has had a full opportunity for a fair hearing. Consequently, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
Edward R. Grant
*2 FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 2074434 (BIA)

2007 WL 2074503 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JACEK PAWKA
FILE: AXX XX8 329 - EL PASO
June 11, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mary Elizabeth Delli-Pizzi, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David Burke
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered deported from the United States on July 12, 1991, because he failed to appear for a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on May 20, 2004, but the Immigration Judge denied it on June 25, 2004. An appeal followed to the Board of Immigration Appeals (“Board”). On January 18, 2005, the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision without opinion. These proceedings were remanded to the Board on November 7, 2005, by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondent argues in his motion to reopen and before the Board that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel and therefore established that “reasonable cause” was responsible for his failure to appear for the hearing.¿FN1º The respondent does not deny that his former counsel, Mike Herrera, was notified of his hearing. He contends, however, that former counsel knew that he was living in Chicago when he received the notice, and he did not make an attempt to notify him of the hearing. He argues that he should not be penalized on account of his former counsel’s negligence. The respondent provided proof that he filed a complaint against his former counsel with the Texas Bar Association. Another former counsel of the respondent, Michael P. DiRaimondo, indicates in his statement that he served Mr. Herrera with a copy of the motion to reopen to presumably allow Mr. Herrera an opportunity to respond to the allegations. A response from Mr. Herrera is not contained in the record.
 
In an agreed upon motion to remand filed with the Fifth Circuit, the respondent and counsel for the government indicated that the Immigration Judge ignored evidence and considered facts not part of the record in denying the respondent’s motion. The motion to remand and order from the Fifth Circuit does not provide specific examples of such. However, on April 3, 2007, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) filed with the Board its position that the record should be remanded to the Immigration Judge to allow him another opportunity to address the respondent’s reopening request in accordance with the concerns raised by the Fifth Circuit. The respondent states in his most recently filed brief with the Board that he is in agreement with the remand request of the DHS. Inasmuch as both the respondent and the DHS have determined that it is appropriate for the Immigration Judge to again address the respondent’s motion to reopen, we shall honor the request. Our prior decision is therefore vacated, and these proceedings are remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993), this Board held that in all deportation cases where the Notice of Hearing for which the alien failed to appear was served after June 13, 1992, an alien must satisfy the standard for reopening cited in sections 242B(c)(3)(A) and 242B(f)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3)(A) and (f)(2). If an alien was served a Notice of Hearing prior to June 13, 1992, he must satisfy the standards for reopening set forth in former section 242(b)of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b). Under section 242(b) of the Act, the alien must establish that he had reasonable cause for his absence from the proceedings. Matter of Ruiz, 20 I&N Dec. 91 (BIA 1989); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988). The regulations do not contain any time or numerical limitations on aliens who wish to reopen deportation proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 242(b) of the Act. See Matter of Mancera, 22 I&N Dec. 79 (BIA 1998); Matter of Cruz-Garcia, 22 I& N Dec. 1155 (BIA 1999); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii).

2007 WL 1794189 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARCOS ALFONSO SOLIS-ENRIQUEZ
FILE: AXX XX7 212 - SEATTLE
June 5, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonio Salazar, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated March 29, 2007, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his deportation proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 242B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b. The respondent filed a timely appeal from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
A hearing notice which is sent by certified mail to the alien’s last known address is sufficient to establish by clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence that the alien received “written notice” of the deportation hearing within the meaning of section 242B(c)(1) of the Act. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). Here, the Immigration Judge found that the Notice of Hearing (NOH) was mailed to the respondent by certified mail, which return receipt was signed by the respondent (Exh. 2).
 
An order of deportation issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 242B of the Act may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed with the Immigration Judge either within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that he failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances (8 C.F.R. 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(1)) or at any time if the alien demonstrates he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own (8 C.F.R. 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(2)).
 
In his motion to reopen filed on March 16, 2007, and again on appeal, the respondent claims that he did not receive notice of his hearing and that he was never informed of the requirement to inform the Immigration Court (IC) of his address changes. He further indicates that he was represented by counsel in Los Angeles, who presumably failed to inform the respondent of his hearing.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the record establishes that the respondent was advised of the requirement to notify of address changes and that he received the NOH. Specifically, the Order to Show Cause (OSC) was personally served on the respondent on July 31, 1993 (Exh. 1). The OSC contained explicit warnings of keeping the IC apprised of his address changes, and the warning was also provided in Spanish. The record also contains a signed postal return receipt indicating that he received the NOH (Exh. 2).
 
We find no merit to the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge is not a fingerprinting expert, and thus cannot discern whether the respondent indeed received the NOH. The Immigration Judge made no fingerprinting analysis, but instead correctly noted the striking similarity between the respondent’s signature on the OSC, request to change venue and the NOH return receipt. It is the respondent’s burden to show that reopening is warranted and thus any “expert” analysis should come from him. We concur with the Immigration Judge that the signatures appear to be the respondent’s. Compare Chaidez v. Gonzales, 2007 WL 1438455 (9th Cir. 2007).
 
*2 To the extent the respondent claims ineffective assistance by former counsel, we find no merit. First, the record reflects that the respondent signed the return receipt for the NOH, so he was aware of the hearing date. Second, the December 8, 1993, in absentia order was mailed to the respondent at the same address as that contained in the NOH, with a courtesy copy mailed to his counsel, and there is no indication in the record that it was returned undelivered. Lastly, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel fails because the respondent has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In that case, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned is informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, where this case arises, has found the application of the noted requirements to generally be reasonable, but has held that “they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). Here, however, the respondent has failed to even minimally fulfill any of the above-noted requirements. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
For the aforementioned reasons, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen these deportation proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1794189 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUKHWINDER SINGH
FILE: AXX XX8 669 - SAN FRANCISCO
June 1, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tsz-Hai Huang, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on August 26, 2005, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely and number-barred. On May 31, 2006, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit granted the respondent’s unopposed motion to remand for consideration of previously unavailable evidence. The motion will be denied.
 
The only evidence cited in support of the motion is that the respondent’s first attorney, Randhir Kang, has been disbarred. However, this development does not affect our previous disposition of the respondent’s case. We have repeatedly considered and denied the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with respect to Mr. Kang, and the fact of his disbarment does not cure the unexplained untimeliness of his prior motions or his failure to properly notify Mr. Kang of the allegations against him and his failure to provide an opportunity to respond. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). At most, it may excuse the filing of a bar complaint, but in this case, the complaint was filed. Moreover, the disbarment does not, in itself, establish that Mr. Kang provided ineffective assistance to the respondent. In fact, after investigating the bar complaint, the State Bar of California concluded that the respondent’s claim “does not warrant action,” as the Mr. Kang’s conduct “does not provide a basis for imposing discipline.” Therefore, the fact that Mr. Kang has been disbarred for conduct in other cases has little bearing on the respondent’s particular case. Finally, the respondent’s current motion does not address our finding that the respondent himself was primarily responsible for the circumstances leading to the denial of the ineffective assistance of counsel motions filed against Mr. Kang by his second attorney, Olumide Obayemi. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Gerald S. Hurwitz
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 1794367 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FEDERICO PEREZ-SANCHEZ
FILE: AXX XX7 132 - PIERCE LAW DETENTION FACILITY
May 31, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Teresa A. Coles-Davila, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carmen A. Leal
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal will be dismissed. The transcript of proceedings shows that the respondent, by counsel, conceded that he was inadmissible and subject to removal due to his 2005 Minnesota Controlled Substance Crime in the Third Degree-Sale convictions (Tr. at 37-39), and as he was statutorily ineligible for relief on account of these drug trafficking aggravated felony convictions (Tr. at 44-45), he waived his right to appeal (Tr. at 45). The Immigration Judge noted in his February 12, 2007, decision that the respondent had waived appeal. On March 14, 2007, the respondent nevertheless filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26).
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(G) (summary dismissal of appeal appropriate where appeal is barred based on waiver of right to appeal). The respondent on the Notice of Appeal argues that his former counsel did not adequately explain his options to him, and this led to his accepting the waiver. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001) (a party wishing to challenge the validity of an appeal waiver may file either a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge or an appeal directly with the Board).
 
However, despite making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has complied with none of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making such a claim.¿FN1º See also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162 (5th Cir. 2006)(citing Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-498 (5th Cir. 2000)(upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada)). However, even if were to find the respondent had substantially complied with the Lozada requirements, the respondent must also establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his former attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640; Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 (5th Cir. 2006) (“alien’s right to due process is violated when ‘the representation afforded ¿ himº was so deficient as to impinge upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing,’ and, as a result, the alien suffered substantial prejudice.”). Specifically, the respondent’s former counsel acknowledged that the respondent’s 2005 Minnesota Controlled Substance Crime in the Third Degree-Sale convictions were aggravated felony convictions that precluded the respondent from establishing his eligibility for relief and waived appeal stating that “it’s not in ¿the respondent’sº best interest to appeal and stay in jail unnecessarily (Tr. at 45).”¿FN2º See Assaad v. Ashcroft, 378 F.3d 471, 475-76 (5th Cir. 2004) (former counsel’s alleged failure to file an appeal from an Immigration Judge’s denial of discretionary relief was not ineffective since alien was not entitled to relief that is available only in the exercise of the Attorney General’s discretion). There is nothing in the record, nor has the respondent provided any evidence to support his contention that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s actions so as to constitute ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The assertion in the appellate brief that the respondent intends to comply with the procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel only if requested, see Respondent’s Mot., at p. 4, is insufficient as a reason to excuse the failure to comply with the Lozada requirements.

2

In this regard, we note the record reflects the respondent was convicted of the offense of Controlled Substance Crime in the Third Degree in violation of MINN. STAT. ANN. § 152.023 subdiv.1(1) (Exh. 2). Section 152.023 provides as follows:
§ 152.023 Controlled substance crime in the third degree
Subdivision 1. Sale crimes. A person is guilty of controlled substance crime in the third degree if:
(1) the person unlawfully sells one or more mixtures containing a narcotic drug;
As the language of MINN. STAT. ANN. § 152.023 subdiv.1(1) makes clear, a conviction thereunder necessarily involves “illicit trafficking” in a controlled substance (as defined in section 102 of the Controlled Substances Act (CSA)), and constitutes an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B), thereby precluding his eligibility for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(3).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALI YOUSSEF KOBEISSI
FILE: AXX XX7 293 - DETROIT
May 31, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert M. Birach, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tara L. Good
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. In a decision dated June 9, 2006, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s applications for withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”) and voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, as well as protection under the Convention Against Torture. On July 11, 2006, the respondent appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision, and we rejected the appeal as untimely on July 28, 2006. On August 4, 2006, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider our July 28, 2006, decision, which we denied on September 26, 2006. On November 6, 2006, the respondent filed a “Motion to Re-open and Request for Stay of Removal.” On January 17, 2007, we denied the motion as a motion to reconsider because it was numerically barred and as a motion to reopen for lack of jurisdiction. The respondent now seeks to reopen proceedings alleging that his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance by failing to file a timely appeal. The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Even assuming that the respondent’s motion to reopen is not numerically barred under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), we nonetheless find that it is untimely. The regulations governing motions to reopen before the Board provide that a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days after the date on which a final administrative decision is rendered. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(e)(2). In this case, the final administrative decision was rendered on July 28, 2006. A timely motion to reopen was thus due on or before October 28, 2006. The respondent’s motion, however, was not filed until February 15, 2007.
 
Moreover, although the respondent alleges in his motion to reopen that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, he has failed to establish that the doctrine of equitable tolling should be applied to excuse the time limitation applicable to his motion. Specifically, we find that the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by his prior attorney’s failure to file a timely appeal. See Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857, 865-66 (6th Cir. 2006) (holding that prejudice must be established even when an alien has been denied the opportunity to appeal because of an attorney’s actions or inactions); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In his motion, the respondent did not raise any specific claims that the Immigration Judge erred in the underlying decision. Thus, the respondent has not established that a timely appeal would have been successful. See Huicochea-Gomez v. INS, 237 F.3d 696, 699-700 (6th Cir. 2001) (finding that an alien presenting an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must establish that, but for his prior attorney’s failures, he would have been entitled to remain in the United States). Moreover, we find that the respondent failed to establish that he fully complied with the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Although the respondent presented a copy of a letter he purportedly sent his prior attorney relating to the alleged ineffective assistance, this letter was sent only 1 day prior to the filing of the letter with the Board. Thus, the attorney would not have had time to respond to the allegations of ineffective assistance prior to the filing of the letter, and the respondent has not filed any subsequent documents or pleadings to indicate whether he received any response from the attorney. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. To the extent that the respondent relies in his motion to reopen on Scorteneau v. INS, 339 F.3d 407 (6th Cir. 2003), we find that it is distinguishable because it involved an alien’s failure to exercise due diligence in filing a motion to reopen after learning about his attorney’s alleged misfeasance, which is not at issue here.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 1794353 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NOE CASTELLANOS-TORRAL
FILE: AXX XX8 578 - ATLANTA
May 31, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Myra L. Hammond, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James R. McHenry III
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s April 26, 2006 decision denying his most recent motion to continue and ordering him removed. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed a memorandum in support of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
A review of the hearing transcripts shows that the respondent was granted various continuances, for a variety of reasons, since the hearing on August 18, 2003. The most recent motion for a continuance was for the adjudication of a Labor Certification that had been pending with the State of Alabama since April 30, 2001. The respondent had declined to apply for any other form of relief (Tr. at 24).
 
The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge and based on whether good cause is shown. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; see also Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996). At the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent had had approximately 3 years, since June 2003 when the Notice to Appear was issued, to have the Labor Certification adjudicated favorably (and approximately 5 years since the Labor Certificate apparently was filed with the State of Alabama). We find that the Immigration Judge did not err in denying a continuance for the purpose of obtaining a Labor Certification which might lead to a visa. See Zafar v. Attorney General, 426 F.3d 1330 (11th Cir. 2005). The pertinent facts in the current case are similar to those in Zafar, specifically that the Labor Certification is still pending and has not been approved yet. We are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that the facts in Zafar are distinguishable in that Zafar’s request for a continuance was denied the first time it was requested, unlike the facts in the current case where there were previously granted motions to continue. We find this not a significant, distinguishable fact. Accordingly, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s exercise of discretion.
 
To the extent that the respondent has attempted to raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (see respondent’s appellate brief at 7-8), we find that he has failed to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 1794213 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NOE MUNOZ ORTIZ
FILE: AXX XX6 017 - PORTLAND
May 30, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dan R. Larsson, Esquire
*1 This case is presently before us on remand by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. On February 8, 2007, the Ninth Circuit granted the parties’ joint motion to remand this case to the Board. The parties jointly agreed that a remand “is appropriate to allow the Board to reassess the jurisdictional issue raised in this case in light of Cardoso-Tlaseca v. Gonzales, 460 F.3d 1102 (9th Cir. 2006) and Weidersperg v. INS, 896 F.2d 1179 (9th Cir. 1990).” See Joint Motion on Remand to the Board dated December 13, 2006.
 
The procedural history of this case is as follows. On January 12, 1999, the respondent was ordered removed in absentia. On February 24, 1999, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s February 16, 1999, motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii). Two days later, on February 26, 1999, the respondent was removed from the United States. See Respondent’s March 23, 2005, Motion to Reopen at 51.
 
On or about April 1, 1999, the respondent reentered the United States. Years after his return to the United States, on March 23, 2005, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel and lack of notice. On May 18, 2005, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion for lack of jurisdiction because the respondent had been removed from this country. See 8 C.F.R. §1003.23(b)(1). We affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
Pursuant to the Ninth Circuit’s directive, we will reassess the jurisdictional issue presented in this case. We also reconsider this case in light of the Ninth Circuit’s recent decision in Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979 (9th Cir. 2007). In that case, the Ninth Circuit held that an alien who was lawfully removed from the United States after the conclusion of immigration proceedings, and who then re-entered illegally and filed an untimely motion to reopen his original deportation proceedings with the Immigration Judge was not barred by the provision in 8 C.F.R. §1003.23(b)(1) which provides that a motion to reopen “shall not be made by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal, deportation, or exclusion proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States.”
 
On reconsideration and in light of the Ninth Circuit’s decision in Lin v. Gonzales, supra., we find that the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, we will remand the record of proceedings to the Immigration Judge to adjudicate the respondent’s March 23, 2005, motion on its merits and in light of Ninth Circuit precedent.
 
*2 Pursuant to the Ninth Circuit’s February 8, 2007, remand order, and upon further consideration of the issues presented therein, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The Board’s decision dated November 21, 2005, and the Immigration Judge’s May 18, 2005, order are vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Review Granted, Decision Reversed by Shamim v. Holder, 9th Cir., April 28, 2009
2007 WL 1724866 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMMED SHAMIM
FILE: AXX XX6 017 - SAN FRANCISCO
May 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Zachary Nightingale, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on March 1, 2007, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s December 1, 2006, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen with the Board. The motion will be denied.
 
In our March 1, 2007, decision, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his proceedings based upon the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii), and that his admissions and waivers during his proceedings on October 19, 2006, without representation, were intelligent and voluntary. In particular, we concurred in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent’s 1994 conviction for assault with a deadly weapon by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury in violation of section 245(a)(1) of the California Penal Code and his 2006 conviction for vandalism in violation of section 594(b)(1) of the California Penal Code each constitutes a conviction for a crime involving moral turpitude rendering the respondent removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act. As a result, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal.
 
On April 16, 2007, the respondent, through new counsel, filed the instant motion to reopen alleging that his motion before the Immigration Judge was denied and appeal before the Board dismissed due to ineffective assistance of prior counsel. See Respondent’s Motion at 9-20. According to the respondent’s motion, prior counsel’s failure to gather and introduce evidence regarding the respondent’s delusional psychotic disorder was ineffective assistance of counsel because this evidence, says the respondent, establishes that he did not knowingly or intelligently concede removability or waive his right to appeal. Id. at 9. The respondent also avers that he has shown prejudice because the Board and the Immigration Judge were not able to consider this evidence in making their respective decisions and that the evidence likely would have changed the outcome of his motion to reopen and/or appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen. Id. at 11-20. In this regard, the respondent argues that he is entitled to contest his removability and/or apply for relief from removal even if he is removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act. Id. at 20-27. Further, the respondent disputes the Immigration Judge’s October 19, 2006, decision finding him removable as an aggravated felon under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act based upon his 2006 conviction for resisting an executive officer in violation of California Penal Code section 69.1 Id. at 25-27.
 
*2 Initially, we note that the respondent has substantially complied with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), in making his ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Respondent’s Brief at 10. However, we find that the respondent has failed to establish the substance of his claim against prior counsel. In particular, the respondent now claims through his current motion that his siblings notified prior counsel of his psychological problems and delusions during their November 7, 2007, appointment with prior counsel. Thus, the respondent alleges that prior counsel had notice of his condition and that he failed to pursue evidence in this regard resulting in the denial of his motion to reopen and dismissal of his appeal before the Board. In contrast to this claim, prior counsel has submitted a response to the respondent’s allegations in which he states that he was not notified of the respondent’s condition until the end of December 2006, merely 2 weeks before the respondent obtained new counsel and ordered prior counsel to cease work on his case. While we agree that had prior counsel had sufficient notice of the respondent’s delusional condition and said delusional condition contributed to the respondent’s refusal to apply for relief for which he appeared eligible, the respondent may have had a valid claim of ineffective representation, we find that the evidence presented does not sufficiently establish that prior counsel had notice of the respondent’s condition such that he was able to respond. As a result, we cannot find that the respondent has, satisfied his burden in establishing his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel based upon the record before us.2
 
Despite the respondent’s failure to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, however, we find that the respondent’s motion to reopen could be granted based upon his submission of new evidence which may materially affect the outcome of his proceedings. Specifically, the respondent has presented medical evidence establishing that he suffers from a delusional psychotic disorder which prevents him from being able to decipher reality from his own paranoid delusions. This evidence suggests that the respondent likely did not have the mental capacity to act properly on his own behalf during his proceedings when he dismissed his opportunity to apply for relief from removal. In this regard, we reiterate our prior decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act which renders the respondent’s concessions regarding removability under this charge irrelevant. However, inasmuch as we find that the respondent has met the technical requirements for reopening based upon his motion, we now find it necessary to address the Immigration Judge’s additional finding of removability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act based upon his conclusion that the respondent’s 2006 conviction for resisting an executive officer in violation of section 69 of the California Penal Code is a “crime of violence” and, thus, an aggravated felony rendering the respondent ineligible for cancellation of removal.
 
*3 California Penal Code section 69 creates criminal liability for “¿e ºvery person who attempts, by means of any threat or violence, to deter or prevent an executive officer from performing any duty imposed upon such officer by law, or who knowingly resists, by the use of force or violence, such officer, in the performance of his duty.” Section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act defines a crime of violence as any other offense that has an element the use, attempted use, or threatened use of physical force against the person of another. A conviction under section 69 has been construed to cover only threats or use of violence. See People v. Superior Court of San Francisco, 151 Cal.App.3d 893 (Cal. App. Dep’t Super. Ct. 1984); In Re Manuel G, 941 P.2d 880 (Cal. 1997). Therefore, it is a categorical “crime of violence.” As a result, the respondent is removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act and ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240b(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), due to his conviction for an aggravated felony. In light of this, we find no reason to reopen the respondent’s proceedings inasmuch as he is not eligible for the relief sought and is removable as charged.
 
Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

2

2007 WL 1724875 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO JAVIER GUDINO-CASTELLANOS A.K.A. FRANK CASTELLANOS GUDINO
FILE: AXX XX9 096 - SAN DIEGO
May 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy J. Cody
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) º - Controlled substance violation
Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(9)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(A)(i)º - Previously ordered removed at arrival
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination of Removal Proceedings
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s January 11, 2007, decision sustaining the charges of inadmissibility against him and ordering him removed from the United States to his native Mexico. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, entered the United States without inspection in 1978 but adjusted his status to that of a lawful permanent resident in 1989. In November 1996 an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent deported from the United States in absentia on the basis of a 1992 California conviction for possession of a controlled substance, and in August 1998 the respondent was physically deported from the United States pursuant to that order. On an unknown date thereafter, the respondent re-entered the United States without being admitted or paroled, and in September 2002 he was convicted pursuant to a guilty plea in California of the offenses of possession of marijuana for sale and transportation of marijuana, for which he was sentenced to a 4-year term of imprisonment. See sections 11359 and 11360(a) of the California Health & Safety Code. On the basis of the aforementioned facts, the Department of Home, land Security (the “DHS”) initiated the present removal proceedings, in which the respondent is charged with inadmissibility to the United States as an alien who: (1) has been convicted of a controlled substance violation; (2) is present in the United States without having been admitted or paroled; and (3) has been removed and who prematurely seeks admission without the Attorney General’s consent. See sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), 212(a)(6)(A)(i), and 212(a)(9)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), 1182(a)(6)(A)(i), and 1182(a)(9)(A)(i)(2000). After conducting a number of evidentiary hearings, the Immigration Judge sustained the charges and ordered the respondent removed. The respondent appeals.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent advances two principal arguments. First, he challenges the Immigration Judge’s determination that his 2002 drug conviction is a valid factual predicate for the inadmissibility charge under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act. According to the respondent, the conviction upon which that charge is based was unconstitutional for various reasons. Furthermore, the respondent claims that the records of conviction proffered into evidence by the DHS do not establish by clear and convincing evidence that he was in fact convicted of possession of marijuana for sale. And second, the respondent contends that his 1998 deportation was unlawful because in November 1996—when the Immigration Judge ordered him deported in absentia—he was in State prison in California serving a 32-month sentence for narcotics possession and was therefore unable to attend his deportation hearing through no fault of his own. Accordingly, the respondent appears to maintain that he retains his lawful permanent resident status (i.e., the status he held before he was ordered deported) and that he is therefore not properly charged with inadmissibility under section 212(a) of the Act. We shall address each argument in turn.
 
The respondent asserts that his 2002 drag conviction is not a valid factual predicate for the charge of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act because his underlying arrest by state law enforcement officers was effected without probable cause and because his criminal defense attorney provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel by ostensibly misleading him as to the scope of his plea agreement. It is well-settled, however, that the Immigration Judges and this Board lack authority to adjudicate the constitutionality or substantive validity of state convictions that are alleged as the bases of removal charges. De La Cruz v. INS, 951 F.2d 226, 228 (9th Cir. 1991)(following Ocon-Perez v. INS, 550 F.2d 1153 (9th Cir. 1977)); Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). Accordingly, the respondent’s 2002 conviction is valid for immigration purposes unless and until it is vacated by a court of competent jurisdiction. Matter of Madrigal, supra.
 
The respondent also contends that the conviction documents contained within the administrative record do not establish by clear and convincing evidence that he was convicted of possession of marijuana for sale. This contention is belied by the evidence. Specifically, the record contains certified copies of an Information charging him with possession of marijuana for sale (Exh. 3, unnumbered p. 1); a judicial minute order reflecting the entry of a plea of guilty to that charge (Exh. 3, unnumbered p.4); an abstract of judgment reflecting a conviction for possession of marijuana-for sale (Exh. 3, unnumbered p.7); and a signed plea agreement reflecting a plea of guilty to a charge of possession of marijuana for sale and a statement of the basis for the plea which includes an admission that the respondent “willfully and unlawfully possessed marijuana for sale.” (Exh. 3, unnumbered pp.9-10). In short, the evidence could not be clearer that in 2002 the respondent did in fact enter a plea of guilty to the offense of possession of marijuana for sale in violation of section 11359 of the California Health & Safety Code.
 
*3 We now turn to the respondent’s argument regarding the validity of his prior deportation. In a supplemental filing appended to his appellate brief, the respondent has submitted a number of documents which substantiate his assertion that he was in State prison on the date in November 1996 when the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia deportation order against him. However, the respondent has evidently not filed a motion to rescind that order with the Immigration Court having jurisdiction over his prior deportation proceedings. Yet by statute the filing of such a motion is the sole means by which an in absentia deportation order can be rescinded. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). This Board has no authority to rescind an in absentia deportation order on our own motion, Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999), and until the respondent secures rescission in accordance with the Act, the present charges will remain valid. For the following reasons, moreover, we do not find under the circumstances of this case that a remand of the record is warranted for further consideration of this issue by the Immigration Judge.
 
Were the respondent to file a motion to rescind and have it acted upon favorably, it may be that the current charges of removability would have to be modified to some extent. For example, if the respondent’s prior deportation order is rescinded, the respondent will be restored to his prior lawful permanent resident status, likely invalidating the present charges that are predicated on his unlawful presence in the United States. See sections 212(a)(6)(A)(i) and 212(a)(9)(A)(i) of the Act. Even if the respondent’s lawful permanent resident status were restored to him, however, it is by no means clear that the charge predicated on his controlled substance violation would have to be modified. See section 101(a)(13)(C)(v) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101 (a)(13)(C)(v)(permitting a lawful permanent resident to be charged with inadmissibility as an arriving alien where the alien “has committed an offense identified in section 212(a)(2), unless since such offense the alien has been granted relief under section 212(h) or 240A(a)…”).1 Furthermore, regardless of whether the respondent is charged with removability under section 212(a) of the Act or section 237(a), his 2002 conviction for possession of marijuana for sale disqualifies him from consideration for a waiver under former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(h)(3), and for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), which is unavailable by its terms to aliens who cannot prove that they have not been convicted of an aggravated felony: Section 240A(a)(3) of the Act.
 
*4 According to the United States Supreme Court, a state drug offense qualifies as an aggravated felony if it proscribes conduct punishable as a felony under the Federal Controlled Substances Act (the “CSA”), 21 U.S.C. § 801 et seq. Under that standard, the respondent’s California conviction for possession of marijuana for sale plainly qualifies as an aggravated felony. Specifically, the CSA provides that possession of any quantity of marijuana with intent to distribute is punishable by a term of imprisonment of 5 years. 21 U.S.C. §§ 841(b)(1)(D). An offense punishable by 5 years in prison is a “felony” under the federal criminal law, 18 U.S.C. § 3559(a)(5), and therefore also qualifies as a felony for purposes of the aggravated felony definition. See Lopez v. Gonzales, supra, at 631 n.7.2
 
In conclusion, because the respondent is removable and ineligible for relief (and would remain so in any event), no constructive purpose would be served by remanding the record for further proceedings. Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Chris Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The 1992 conviction underlying the respondent’s prior deportation proceedings would qualify as an “offense identified in section 212(a)(2),” as would the 1996 conviction for which he was imprisoned at the time of his November 1996 deportation heating and the 2002 conviction alleged in the Notice to Appear.

2

If a federal criminal defendant demonstrates that his particular violation of 21 U.S.C. § 841(a) involved distribution of “a small amount of marihuana for no remuneration,” 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(4) provides that the offense is to be “treated” for sentencing purposes as if it were simple possession, thereby transforming it into a federal misdemeanor punishable by no more than one year in prison. 21 U.S.C. § 844. According to the Federal Courts of Appeals, however, 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(4) does not define the elements of a discrete offense; rather, it describes a “mitigating exception” to the otherwise-applicable 5-year statutory maximum specified by 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(D). United States v. Hamlin, 319 F.3d 666, 670-71 (4th Cir. 2003); United States v. Bartholomew, 310 F.3d 912, 925 (6th Cir. 2002); United States v. Walker, 302 F.3d 322, 324-25 (5th Cir. 2002); United States v. Outen, 286 F.3d 622, 638 (2d Cir. 2002). Hence, in the federal criminal context it is the defendant who must prove the additional facts (i.e., the “smallness” of the amount of marijuana and the absence of remuneration) that trigger the exception. A federal prosecutor who seeks a 5-year sentence against a marijuana distributor need not prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the amount of marijuana distributed (or sought to be distributed) was “large” or that remuneration was present. Because 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(4) is an ameliorative sentencing provision that is unrelated to the elements of any federal drug offense, we conclude that 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(D) (and not 21 U.S.C. § 8410)(4)) is the proper federal analogue for a state marijuana distribution (or possession with intent to distribute) offense under the “hypothetical federal felony” approach.

2007 WL 1724843 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAJAN SINGLA
FILE: AXX XX6 785 - CHICAGO
May 23, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Fawell, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joseph Yeung
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening, termination
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on December 7, 2005, when we summarily affirmed without opinion an Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 19, 2005. In that decision the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable pursuant to sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), and (B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) and (B)(i), as having been convicted of a controlled substance violation; and which he also found to been a conviction for an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B), based on the respondent’s record of conviction (Group Exh. 2). Having found the respondent convicted of an aggravated felony under the Act, the Immigration Judge pretermitted his request for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), and ordered his removal to India (I.J. at 3-4). The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen seeking to terminate his proceedings. The respondent requests reopening on the grounds that the conviction which forms the essential basis for the grounds of removal found by the Immigration Judge and his determination as to the respondent’s statutory ineligibility for relief, has now been vacated. The Board’s December 7, 2005, decision will be vacated, and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion and the issuance of a new decision.
 
The respondent has offered a certified copy of an order from the Circuit Court of the Eighteenth Judicial Circuit, DuPage County, Wheaton, Illinois, dated February 16, 2007, granting the respondent’s motion to vacate his plea of guilty as to his February 24, 2004, conviction in the Circuit Court of the Eighteenth Judicial Circuit, DuPage County, Wheaton, Illinois, for the offense of Unlawful Delivery of a Controlled Substance, to wit: Cocaine, in violation of section 570/401(c)(2) of chapter 720 of the ILCS. See Respondent’s Exh. “H.”
 
The Board in Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), held that if a court vacates a conviction based upon a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings, there is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes. See also Pickering v. Gonzales, 465 F.3d 263 (6th Cir. 2006) (To prove deportability, the government must produce evidence of a conviction that remains valid for immigration purposes, and in order to meet its burden, the government must prove, with clear and convincing evidence, that the respondent’s conviction was quashed solely for rehabilitative reasons or reasons related to his immigration status, i.e., to avoid adverse immigration consequences); Cruz-Garza v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d 1125, 1130 (10th Cir. 2005). In rendering his vacatur order, the criminal court Judge found support for the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and determined that the respondent’s plea was tendered involuntarily and was violative of constitutional due process. See Sandoval v. I.N.S., 240 F.3d 577, 578-79 (7 th Cir. 2001) (court found under Illinois law, if a defendant enters a plea of guilty in reasonable reliance upon the erroneous advice of counsel that the defendant’s plea would have no collateral deportation consequence, reliance on this misleading advice can render the defendant’s plea involuntary, citing People v. Correa, 485 N.E.2d 307, 309-12 (1985), and People v. Luna, 570 N.E.2d 404, 406-07 (1991) (holding that post-conviction petition, which alleged that counsel failed to advise alien that a felony conviction could result in deportation, was sufficient to state a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel)).
 
*2 The United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, acknowledges and accepts the Board’s holding in Pickering that a conviction vacated solely for rehabilitative reasons or to avoid adverse immigration consequences remains a conviction for immigration purposes. See Ali v. Ashcroft, 395 F. 3d 722, 728-729 (7th Cir. 2005); see also Gill v. Ashcroft, 335 F.3d 574, 578 (7th Cir.2003) (finding a state conviction dismissed pursuant to state rehabilitative statutory scheme, and not because of any procedural or substantive defect in the conviction, remained a conviction for immigration purposes). However, the Seventh Circuit concomitantly recognizes that where an alien’s conviction is vacated pursuant to a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying criminal proceedings, it may no longer constitute a conviction for immigration purposes. See Ali v. Ashcroft, supra at 729 (citing with approval its decision in Sandoval v. I.N.S., 240 F.3d 577, 584 (7th Cir. 2001) and BIA decisions cited therein to hold that an alien whose conviction had been modified because of ineffective assistance of counsel at the plea stage did not remain convicted for immigration purposes).
 
Although the criminal court’s vacatur was issued pursuant to a post-conviction motion under the applicable statutes, the Seventh circuit has found that because the very purpose of the Illinois Post Conviction Hearing Act is to remedy constitutional violations in the underlying criminal proceedings, the Illinois scheme is not a state rehabilitative statute, so that it falls outside the category of statutes that would preserve a conviction for immigration purposes. Sandoval v. I.N.S., supra at 582-83. Therefore, there remains no dispute that the criminal court’s action in this case was premised upon what it perceived to be a constitutional infirmity in the underlying proceedings, rather than some form of rehabilitative post-conviction relief. See e.g., Rumierz v. Gonzales, 456 F.3d 31, 34-35(1st Cir. 2006) (a vacated conviction is no longer a “conviction” within the meaning of the immigration laws only “if a court with jurisdiction vacates ¿theº conviction based on a defect in the underlying criminal proceedings.”); see also Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006) (holding that a conviction vacated under Ohio Revised Code § 2943.031 for the trial court’s failure to advise the alien defendant of the possible immigration consequences of a guilty plea is no longer a valid conviction for immigration purposes) and Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 1378, 1379-80 (BIA 2000) (holding that a conviction vacated pursuant to Article 440 of the New York Criminal Procedure Law, which is neither an expungement statute nor a rehabilitative statute, did not constitute a conviction for immigration purposes); Matter of Pickering, supra at 624.
 
*3 Finally, the DHS has opposed the respondent’s motion on the grounds that it is untimely and that the respondent’s remaining 2004 conviction, for delivery of a “Look-alike” substance in violation of section 570/404(b) of chapter 720 of the ILCS, constitutes an “aggravated felony” within the meaning of the Act. See generally U.S. v. Robertson, 474 F. 3d 538 (8th Cir. 2007). However, the Immigration Judge expressly declined to consider this alternative theory of removability (I.J. at 3-4). Under these circumstances, and considering especially that the conviction underlying the outstanding order of removability has been vacated, the Board will exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen and remand for further proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s December 7, 2005, decision is vacated, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
Jeffrey L. Romig
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1724843 (BIA)

2007 WL 1724855 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CRISTIANE MARIA ARAUJO
FILE: AXX XX3 385 - PHOENIX
May 22, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Socheat Chea, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Holya,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 18, 2006, denying her motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear at the hearing on April 2, 2003. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear was issued on October 23, 2002, and personally served on the respondent on that date. Furthermore, the certificate of service on the Notice to Appear indicates that the respondent was provided oral notice in the Portuguese language of the consequences of a failure to appear, and it is signed by the respondent to acknowledge her receipt of it. See Exh. 1.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent received notice of the hearing as required by section 239(a)(2) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(2). Written notice is considered sufficient if provided at the most recent address provided under section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act. Section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent received proper notice of the hearing, as it was mailed on November 25, 2002, to the most recent address provided by the respondent, and she acknowledges that she received it.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge failed to consider all of the evidence regarding the developments that occurred regarding notice to the respondent. She avers that her husband received a change of venue and his case was transferred to Atlanta, but the respondent’s case was not. The respondent states that she was told that venue for both of their cases had been changed, but the respondent has not provided any evidence that the Immigration Court provided her with such information, nor is there any evidence in the record that the respondent ever sought a change of venue to Atlanta, Georgia. The respondent also indicates that her husband was ordered removed in absentia, but the Immigration Judge subsequently granted his motion to reopen. The respondent has not provided a copy of the motion to reopen or the Immigration Judge’s decision regarding her husband’s case. Therefore, it is unknown on what basis his proceedings were reopened by the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondent also argues that she never received a hearing notice from the Atlanta Immigration Court, which indicates the existence of a notice problem. However, the record does not reflect that the respondent ever filed a motion to change venue with the Phoenix Immigration Court requesting that her case be transferred to the Atlanta Immigration Court, nor that the Immigration Judge granted a motion to change venue. Consequently, the respondent’s case was never transferred to the Atlanta Immigration Court. Therefore, her argument regarding lack of notice from the Atlanta Immigration Court is without merit.
 
*2 Finally, the respondent asserts that subsequent to receiving the hearing notice from the Phoenix Immigration Court, she relied on information from an individual who spoke Portuguese, and who incorrectly informed her that her case was transferred to Atlanta. There is no indication that the respondent ever contacted the Phoenix Immigration Court regarding her scheduled hearing. The respondent’s reliance on an unknown individual’s incorrect advice that the respondent’s case had also been transferred to Atlanta does not constitute exceptional circumstances. Furthermore, the respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel for her failure to appear. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999) (the statute of limitations to reopen an order of deportation is equitably tolled where the alien’s late petition is the result of deceptive actions by a representative). Even if the respondent had alleged and established ineffective assistance of counsel for her failure to appear, she failed to exercise due diligence in seeking to reopen her removal proceedings. See e.g. Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Socop-Gonzalez, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Lopez v. INS, supra. She acknowledges receiving the notice of hearing, dated November 25, 2002, yet the respondent did not seek to determine the status of her removal proceedings and file a motion to reopen until July 3, 2006, over three years after she was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent has failed to establish that reopening is warranted in this matter. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1724855 (BIA)

2007 WL 1676942 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DANIEL DESIRE TEBA KODJO
FILE: AXX XX6 440 - BALTIMORE
May 21, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda A. Dominguez, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal under section 240A; voluntary departure
 
The respondent has filed a timely1 appeal of the Immigration Judge’s written decision dated October 12, 2005, denying the respondent’s applications for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), and voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), and ordering him removed to the Ivory Coast. On appeal, the respondent argues that he suffered prejudice due to ineffective assistance of counsel. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent has submitted new evidence and has requested that his case be remanded to the Immigration Judge. The complete and well-written decision of the Immigration Judge will be affirmed, the respondent’s appeal dismissed, and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
The respondent has failed to meet the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Most importantly, he has failed to show that he suffered prejudice resulting from his prior counsel’s conduct in regard to his claim of cancellation of removal. Exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to his United States citizen son has not been shown. See section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act; Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001) (hardship that is substantially beyond that ordinarily expected to result from removal from the United States).
 
Furthermore, the respondent has failed to show that he would have qualified for voluntary departure, and thus failed to show prejudice in that regard as well. Specifically, contrary to the respondent’s argument on his Notice of Appeal, the record before us does not contain a complete copy of a valid passport issued in the respondent’s name, and he has not provided a copy of such on appeal. Thus he has failed to show that he had the means to depart the United States, and he thus failed to qualify for voluntary departure. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(2). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Contrary to the respondent’s arguments on appeal, the appeal was not due on November 11, 2005, a federal holiday, but rather was due the following business day, Monday, November 14, 2005, the date his appeal was received at the Board.

2007 WL 1676943 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTORINA CRUZ-DE MELGAR
FILE: AXX XX4 591 - NEW YORK
May 21, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States because she failed to appear for a hearing on March 12, 2002. She filed a motion to reopen on August 25, 2006, but an Immigration Judge denied the request on September 15, 2006. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that she did not receive notice of her hearing from either the Immigration Court or her former attorney. The record reflects that on or about February 21, 2002, an attorney filed a Notice of Appearance on behalf of the respondent. We further note that the Immigration Court served former counsel with the Notice of Hearing advising counsel that the respondent’s hearing would be held on March 12, 2002 (See Exh. #2/Certificate of Service). In addition, the record contains a letter dated March 11, 2002, from former counsel indicating his request to withdraw as the respondent’s attorney. The motion to withdraw was not received by the Immigration Court until 3 days after the entry of the in absentia order of removal. Attached to that letter was a copy of the Notice of Hearing. We therefore conclude that the respondent’s former counsel had notice of the respondent’s hearing before the Immigration Court. A Notice of Hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is proper notice. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5 and Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (1985). We therefore affirm the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the notice requirements were satisfied in these proceedings.
 
We have considered the respondent’s argument that her former attorney did not notify her of her hearing. However, the nature of the respondent’s argument involves attorney negligence. Such a claim is based on exceptional circumstances, and had to be filed within 180 days of the issuance date of the order of removal. See Mater of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998); section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. It was not timely submitted, and the respondent’s request is therefore time-barred from consideration. We further note that the respondent received personal service of the Notice to Appear and was aware that she was in removal proceedings. Inasmuch as more than four years elapsed before the respondent requested reopening, she has failed to pursue her claim with due diligence. Chen v. Gonzales, 437 F.3d 267 (2d Cir. 2006); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006). See also Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133 (2nd Cir. 2007) (discussing the burden required for proving ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Jeffrey L. Romig
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1676943 (BIA)
