2007 WL 1724841 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NASREEN SHAH SOHAIL SHAH FARHAN SHAH REHAN SHAH MARYAM SHAH
FILES: AXX XX2 289 - BUFFALO
AXX XX2 290
AXX XX2 291
AXX XX2 292
AXX XX2 293
May 18, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy A. Merriman,
Of Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated September 21, 2006, the Immigration Judge denied the respondents’ motion to reopen their removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). The respondents filed a timely appeal from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record contains Forms I-826, Notice of Rights and Request for Disposition, for each of the respondents, in which they request a hearing before the Immigration Court, and which are also signed by each respondent on June 11, 2002. The record contains Forms I-213, Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien, dated June 11, 2002, which reflects the respondents addresses as: 50 Wyoming Street, Buffalo, NY 14215. The Forms I-862, Notices to Appear (NTA) were mailed to the respondents at the above address on June 19, 2002, which ordered the respondents to appear at a removal hearing on August 21, 2002. The respondents failed to appear and an in absentia removal order was entered on that date.
 
In their August 15, 2006, motion to reopen, the respondents do not allege that they did not receive notice of their hearing. Instead, they aver that since they were attempting to immigrate to Canada, they were advised by the Red Cross not to appear at their hearing, and so they left prior to the removal hearing. On appeal, they contend that Viva Le Casa advised them to depart to Canada, and they indicate that they want their removal orders “removed” because they are now Canadian residents and they want to travel to the United States.
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 723 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” is defined by the Act as “exceptional circumstances (such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
*2 A review of the record reflects that the Immigration Judge’s order of removal was entered in absentia on August 21, 2002. There is no indication in the record that the respondents advised the Immigration Judge that they had departed the United States. The respondents failed to file their motion to reopen within 180 days after issuance of the order of removal, as is required under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. To the extent they allege ineffective assistance of counsel (insofar as they were purportedly advised not to appear at their hearing), they have failed to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and re-affirmed in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See also Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46 (2d Cir. 2005) (finding that the failure to comply with the Lozada requirements constitutes forfeiture of the ineffective assistance claim). Moreover, they have failed to show that they have been diligent since they purportedly discovered their removal orders in 2004.1 See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006)(court concluded that aliens had demonstrated that their counsel was ineffective in informing them that they did not need to appear at a hearing, but they had not met their burden of showing why they had not been diligent). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On appeal, the respondents maintain that it was only when their attorney in Canada reviewed their documents in 2004, that they realized they had a court date in August 2002. This claim is nonsensical, because if they had documents in their possession which reflected the 2002 hearing date, they obviously received notice of their hearing or the removal order. Consequently, they should have been aware of their hearing. This statement is also inconsistent with their other averment that they were advised by the Red Cross (or Viva Le Casa) not to appear at their hearing.

2007 WL 1676912 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALVADOR ORTEGA-SANDOVAL
FILE: AXX XX2 016 - SAN PEDRO
May 17, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray D. Hilts, Esquire
*1 This case is before us on remand from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. Pursuant to a joint motion to remand, in its November 14, 2006, unpublished decision, the Ninth Circuit remanded the case to the Board. Upon further consideration, the Board again declines to reopen proceedings in this case.
 
The respondent is from Mexico. On October 20, 1993, in California, he was convicted of sale/transportation/offer to sell heroin. On May 22, 2001, the respondent was convicted of possession of heroin in California. On January 10, 2002, an Immigration Judge found that the respondent had been convicted of an aggravated felony and was ineligible for relief from removal. The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal as untimely on August 29, 2003.
 
On October 14, 2005, the Board issued an order which found that the respondent had not submitted a proper motion to reopen on June 14, 2005, as it contained no legal argument, but rather had presented several “Attachments”, including the respondent’s affidavit.
 
On January 20, 2006, the Board considered a reconsideration motion filed by the respondent. The Board reconsidered the reasons for the earlier denial of the motion to reopen, based on the explanation that the respondent’s attorney’s office made a clerical error in omitting the actual motion to reopen. Upon reconsideration, however, the motion to reopen was again denied. The Board found that, despite blaming former counsel for filing an untimely appeal, the respondent failed to show diligence in bringing his claims.
 
In any event, the Board found, the respondent failed to show prejudice resulting from his attorney’s actions. Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1189 (9th Cir. 2004); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The Board noted that the Immigration Judge found the respondent ineligible for relief from removal based on his conviction of an aggravated felony. The motion to reopen argued that the respondent was eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) concerning the 1993 conviction, which would then allow him to pursue cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, at pp. 10-12).
 
The Board’s January 20, 2006, decision found that this contention was incorrect. The United States Supreme Court held that the restrictions on section 212(c) relief do not apply retroactively to aliens who pled guilty to offenses prior to April 24, 1996. See INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). The respondent was found to have committed an aggravated felony based on his 2001 conviction for possession of heroin, after a previous conviction, and relief under 212(c) of the Act was therefore unavailable even under that court ruling.
 
*2 The joint motion to remand, approved by the Ninth Circuit, states that the Board is to “consider¿º whether, in connection with a proper analysis of a controlled substance violation precluding relief, including but not limited to consideration of properly noticed documents, ¿the respondentº has shown prejudice sufficient to support ¿the respondent’sº claim of ineffective assistance of counsel” (case citations omitted).
 
As the Board noted in its January 20, 2006, decision, the respondent was convicted in 2001 for possession of heroin, after a previous conviction. The respondent’s 2001 conviction for possession of heroin was preceded by the 1993 heroin conviction, so the 2001 heroin possession conviction is an aggravated felony under federal law. See 21 U.S.C. § 844(a); Lopez v. Gonzales, __ U.S. __, 127 S.Ct. 625 (U.S. 2006). The 2001 crime is not subject to a section 212(c) waiver under INS v. St. Cyr, supra, as it was committed long after April 24, 1996. Moreover, as the respondent was convicted of the aggravated felony in 2001, he is not eligible for cancellation of removal. See section 240A(a) of the Act.
 
Additionally, even if a section 212(c) waiver was potentially available concerning the 1993 narcotics conviction, it would not eliminate the existence of that conviction and its ability to render the 2001 conviction an aggravated felony. See Becker v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 1000, 1003-04 (9th Cir. 2007) (“… the grant of section 212(c) relief merely waives the finding of deportability rather than the basis of the deportability itself. Therefore, the crimes alleged to be grounds for deportability do not disappear from the alien’s record for immigration purposes”). Therefore, as the Board stated in its January 20, 2006, decision, the respondent is ineligible for relief under section 212(c) of the Act, as well as section 240A(a) of the Act, and the respondent has not shown prejudice regarding alleged ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
ORDER: As set out in the joint remand motion approved by the Ninth Circuit on November 14, 2006, we have given further consideration to the respondent’s motion to reopen, and find that it should again be denied. The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL

George P. Mann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael B. Dobson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
By a decision dated January 22, 2007, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s second motion to reopen his immigration proceedings. The respondent has filed a timely appeal of that decision. On appeal, the respondent avers that this Board should apply equitable tolling to the numerical limitations on motions to reopen in order for him to seek redress for his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally section 240(c)(6)(A), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(A); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the respondent’s motion. We have reviewed the record of proceedings, the Immigration Judge’s decision, and the arguments advanced on appeal. Upon consideration of the totality of the factors presented in this case, we will adjudicate the instant motion in the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1134-36 (BIA 1999). See Tapia-Martinez v. Gonzales, ___ F.3d ___, 2007 WL 627822 (Feb. 27, 2007). Moreover, given the circumstances, we find that the reopening of the respondent’s proceedings is warranted. See, e.g., Respondent’s’Brief, sub-exh. D; cf. I.J. Dec. (Sept. 18, 2006) at 2. See generally Motion to Reopen (Dec. 7, 2006), sub-exhs. A and F.
 
With regard to the DHS’s argument that the respondent is precluded from seeking adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), as a result of his failure to voluntarily depart from the United States, we disagree. There is no evidence to suggest that the respondent ever posted the voluntary departure bond required by section 240B(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(3). See generally I.J. Dec. (Sept. 18, 2006) at 3. Assuming arguendo that the respondent did not pay the bond by September 25, 2006, his alternate order of removal would have taken effect at that time and he would still be eligible to seek adjustment of status. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3); Matter of Diaz-Ruacho, 24 I&N Dec. 47 (BIA 2006).
 
*2 Accordingly, we find it appropriate to grant the respondent’s motion and remand the matter to the Immigration Court. In the remanded proceedings, the parties will be provided with an opportunity to present additional evidence relevant to the respondent’s eligibility for adjustment of status, including testimony and documentary materials.
 
In view of the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Lauri S. Filppu

2007 WL 1520804 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROBERTO ANTONIO CHAVEZ-GRANADOS
FILE: AXX XX5 843 - SAN PEDRO
May 11, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Shan D. Potts, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on September 25, 1997, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s September 9, 1996, decision finding the respondent ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c).
 
On January 17, 2007, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen seeking the opportunity to apply for 212(c) relief. A special motion to reopen for 212(c) relief must have been filed on or before April 26, 2005. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(h). The respondent’s motion was filed over one year after the deadline. Although it appears that the respondent is eligible for 212(c) relief, his motion will be denied because it was not timely filed.
 
We also find the respondent has failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel that would warrant equitable tolling of the time limits on motions to reopen under 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.44(h). See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899-900 (9th Cir.2003) (no claim for ineffective assistance where an alien was unable to demonstrate both a deprivation of due process and resulting prejudice). Nothing in the record demonstrates that the respondent was actually deprived of due process. See Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985) (ineffective assistance of counsel is a denial of due process “if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case”).
 
The respondent asserts an ineffective assistance claim against both his prior attorneys: Ms. Gloria Weil-Herrera, who represented him before the Immigration Judge and the Board, and Mr. George A. Verdin, who represented him in bond proceedings. The respondent asserts that Ms. Weil-Herrera failed to inform him of our September 25, 1997, order, and, consequently, he was precluded from filing a motion to reopen and seeking judicial review. See Respondent’s Motion at 7. The respondent asserts that Mr. Verdin failed to keep him informed of further 212(c) developments. Id. at 8. According to the respondent’s declaration, he obtained a copy of his file in 2006 and eventually obtained current counsel to file a motion to reopen. See Motion to Reopen, Attachment at 3 (respondent’s declaration in support of his motion).
 
We find that the respondent has failed to exercise due diligence in his case such that he warrants the equitable tolling of the motions deadlines. Even if, as he asserts, Ms. Weil-Herrera failed to notify him of our September 25, 1997, decision, the respondent failed to take any action whatsoever to check the status of his case for nearly 10 years. According to the respondent, he was on notice that Ms. Weil-Herrera could not be relied upon since by December 1996, just two months after his hearing, he had to retain Mr. Verdin because he was unable to contact Ms. Weil-Herrera. See Motion to Reopen, Attachment 2 (respondent’s declaration in support of his motion). The respondent also states that Ms. Weil-Herrera did not advise him to file for any other form of relief from deportation, but he fails to assert or establish that he was prima facie eligible for any other form of relief at the time of his September 9, 1996, deportation hearing. Additionally, the respondent knew from Mr. Verdin that his case was pending with the Board, and he did nothing to check the status of his case. Based on the respondent’s own assertions, he has failed to establish that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his case.
 
*2 Regarding his claim against Mr. Verdin, who assisted him in bond proceedings in December 1996, the respondent does not claim that he made any effort during the following 10 years to contact Mr. Verdin regarding the status of his case. See Motion to Reopen, Attachment 1-3 (respondent’s declaration in support of his motion). He does not claim to have checked in regularly with Mr. Verdin or that he kept Mr. Verdin appraised of his address. Nor does he provide any evidence of the contractual duties owed him by Mr. Verdin. The respondent, who knew he was deportation proceedings, had a duty to diligently pursue his immigration case. Yet, according to his own statement, he did not do anything for about 10 years. Based on the respondent’s own statement and assertions, we find that his case does not warrant equitable tolling where he failed to exercise due diligence. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied as untimely filed.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1520804 (BIA)

2007 WL 1520807 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHELA ROSE P. BUTLER
FILE: AXX XX9 339 - HARTFORD
May 10, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arthur A. Campomanes, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s September 6, 2005, decision denying the respondent’s counsel’s request for a continuance for filing of a new I-130 visa petition, and ordering the respondent removed to the Philippines. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s counsel’s request for a continuance after determining that the respondent had abandoned a visa petition that had been filed on her behalf and that she did not have, at the time of the hearing, an approved, or even pending, visa petition (I.J. at 2).1 One of the pre-requisites to qualify for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), is that the alien demonstrate that an immigrant visa is immediately available to him or her at the time that the application is filed. See Matter of Garcia, 16 I&N Dec. 653, 654 (BIA 1978). In the present case, it is an established, undisputed fact that, at the time of the hearing, no immigrant visa was immediately available to the respondent, as there was no approved, or even pending, visa petition. The respondent has merely argued, unpersuasively, that the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion for continuance was erroneous and constituted a due process violation. See Respondent’s Brief at 3-5. We disagree. An Immigration Judge, in his or her discretion, may grant a motion for a continuance for good cause shown. Good cause is not demonstrated by requesting a continuance to await the results of a collateral event, which may occur at some indefinite time in the future, and the outcome of which may or may not be favorable to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987). We find that the Immigration Judge did not abuse his discretion in denying the respondent’s motion for a continuance in this case because, at the time of the hearing, the respondent was statutorily ineligible to adjust status.
 
This Board finds puzzling, as well as troublesome, the respondent’s unfounded suggestion, through counsel, of bias and/or misconduct by the Immigration Judge, claiming that the Immigration Judge “seemed more interested in finding a justification to deport her rather than finding out the facts in her situation and giving her an opportunity to seek an available relief.” See Respondent’s Brief at 4. We have found no evidence in the record of any bias or misconduct by the Immigration Judge. This Board also notes that the respondent has suggested, through counsel, that she received ineffective assistance from her prior counsel, who allegedly failed to appeal the October 2001 denial of a visa application that was reportedly filed on her behalf by her husband. See Notice of Appeal, attachment; Tr. at 15-16, 24; see also Respondent’s Brief, Exh. B. The respondent, however, has failed to set forth a prima facie claim of ineffective assistance of counsel because she has not met the minimum requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), petition for review denied, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133, 142 (2d Cir. 2007). The respondent also seems to have implied, through counsel, that the burden to prove ineffective assistance of counsel is on the government. See Respondent’s Brief at 4. A simple reading of Lozada reveals that that is not the case. We also find unpersuasive the respondent’s unsupported and unconvincing argument on appeal that the Immigration Judge “erred in compelling the respondent to be a witness against herself on … fraud allegations.” See Respondent’s Brief at 5.
 
*2 Finally, this Board acknowledges the argument and evidence submitted by the respondent on appeal suggesting that a visa application, purportedly filed by the respondent’s current husband after the hearing in this case, has already been approved. See Respondent’s Brief at 5, and its Exhs. B and D. Even if actual proof of the approval of a visa petition had been submitted, the dismissal of this appeal would nevertheless stand, as the respondent’s appeal is limited to the question of whether the Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion for a continuance was appropriate. Any subsequent filing and approval of a visa petition of which the respondent is the beneficiary is clearly irrelevant for purposes of this appeal.2 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 1520811 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YASSINE SRAYI
FILE: AXX XX4 477 - ARLINGTON
May 10, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antoinette J. Rizzi, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status, remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is a native and citizen of Morocco. On June 13, 2005, we reinstated proceedings, due to a finding of prior ineffective assistance of counsel, thus, the respondent’s appeal will now be adjudicated on the merits. The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 1, 2004, decision. The respondent claims that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion by failing to grant an additional continuance and by failing to administratively close proceedings. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion to remand will be denied. However, the respondent will be given the privilege of voluntarily departing.
 
The respondent acknowledges that he was granted three different continuances, over a period of 1 year, in order to pursue an application for adjustment of status and additionally for the pursuit of a motion to reopen and reconsider the denial of the I-140 petition and the I-485 application. The record reflects that the I-140 petition was denied due to the inability of the employer/petitioner to establish the ability to pay the proffered wage or salary. The respondent, in a reply (dated January 14, 2005) to a response concerning an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, acknowledged that the attorney Hadeed did inform the employer/petitioner of a request for further information about the ability to pay the proffered wage. There is a letter, dated December 3, 2004, from May Shallal Kheder, indicating that, prior to the issuance of the Immigration Judge’s order dated April 1, 2004, the respondent’s first motion to reopen and reconsider the I-140 denial was also denied. Such letter also indicates that the respondent quit working for the petitioner. Such letter further indicates that the employer/petitioner later decided not to continue to pursue the I-140 petition. We find that the respondent failed to adequately establish good cause for a fourth continuance on April 1, 2004. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; see also Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987) (noting that a decision to deny a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing). There is insufficient evidence that the I-140 or I-485 were prima facie approvable at the time of the respondent’s April 1, 2004, hearing.
 
*2 Furthermore, we do not find that the respondent has established that the Immigration Judge erred by failing to administratively close his proceedings. The respondent admits that both parties did not agree to the administrative closure of this case. We have long held that an Immigration Judge may not grant administrative closure where it is opposed by either party. See Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 480 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lopez-Barrios, 20 I&N Dec. 203 (BIA 1990). The respondent asks that we revisit and reconsider our holding in Matter of Lopez-Barrios, supra. However, we decline to do so at this time.
 
The respondent has requested that we remand the record so that he may pursue a second I-140 petition. However, we note that such petition and supporting documentation, which the respondent indicates are attached to the appellate brief, are not in fact attached to such-brief and are not in the record. Hence, as there is insufficient evidence that the pending I-140 is prima facie approvable, the respondent’s motion to remand is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed and his motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 90 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS, formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 1520890 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MANUEL PRIETO-ROMERO
FILE: AXX XX4 042 - TACOMA
May 7, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brian Patrick Conry, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tammy L. Fitting
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on September 22, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s May 6, 2005, decision. The Immigration Judge found the respondent ineligible for relief under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), because the respondent’s conviction for second degree sexual abuse by a jury trial does not fall within the ambit of the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001); and the respondent’s conviction for an offense constituting an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(A), has no statutory counterpart in the grounds of inadmissibility under section 212(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a).
 
On March 28, 2007, the respondent filed the instant untimely motion to reopen based on an ineffective assistance claim against his prior attorney, Ms. Erin Hall. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) (with certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal). A final order was entered in this case on September 22, 2005.
 
Because this case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit and the respondent presents an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we will consider whether this case warrants equitable tolling. The Ninth Circuit has held that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
Although the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), he has failed to establish that his case was prejudiced as a result of Ms. Hall’s conduct. Nor is it clear that Ms. Hall’s assistance was ineffective.
 
We recognize that the respondent’s motion is supported by new evidence which was not submitted to the Immigration Judge, namely, a transcript of the respondent’s April 15, 1989, sentencing proceeding in the Circuit Court of the County of Multnomah, in Portland Oregon. The respondent submits this document to establish that although he was convicted at trial, he relied on the availability of 212(c) relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(h) (aliens are not eligible to apply for 212(c) relief with respect to convictions entered after trial). However, the proffered evidence would not necessarily alter the result in this case. To date, in the Ninth Circuit, wherein this case arises, an alien who is convicted at trial is not eligible for section 212(c) relief. See Armendariz-Montoya v. Sonchik, 291 F.3d 1116, 1121-22 (9th Cir. 2002), cert. denied, 539 U.S. 902 (2003). Although Ms. Hall did not submit this transcript to the Immigration Judge, she submitted a memorandum of law arguing the respondent’s 212(c) eligibility even though he was convicted at trial.
 
*2 Even if the respondent could establish his 212(c) eligibility despite the fact that he was convicted at trial, he is ineligible for a 212(c) waiver because his aggravated felony conviction lacks a comparable ground of inadmissibility. See Matter of Blake, 23 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 2005). In his motion, the respondent asserts that Ms. Hall should have argued that the facts of his case are different from those in Matter of Blake, supra. See Respondent’s Motion, Attachment ER-8 (Bar Complaint to the Washington State Bar). The respondent also asserts that Ms. Hall should have argued that Blake was wrongly decided. See Respondent’s Motion, Attachment ER-5 at 39-44 (Respondent’s Brief filed with the Ninth Circuit). Ms. Hall’s failure to raise these particular arguments did not prejudice the respondent’s case. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004) (counsel’s actions must have rendered the proceedings fundamentally unfair such that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case). Under the principles enunciated in Matter of Blake, supra, and Matter of Brieva, 23 I&N Dec. 766 (BIA 2005), the respondent’s aggravated felony conviction lacks a comparable ground of inadmissibility, and he is ineligible for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Act. We find no reason to disturb those decisions.
 
Accordingly, we find that the respondent has failed to present a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that warrants equitable tolling of the time limits on motions to reopen. The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Gerald S. Hurwitz
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1520890 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 1520859 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUDASSIR MOHAMED BHIWANDIWALA
FILE: AXX-XX1-905 - HOUSTON
May 4, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carol H. Sloss, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen and reconsider our decision dated February 8, 2007. Our prior decision summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal for failure to provide specific reasons in his Notice Of Appeal for challenging the Immigration Court’s decision, and failure to submit a separate brief or statement in support of the appeal when he checked a box on the Notice Of Appeal indicating his intent to do so. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(A) and (E). The respondent’s motion to reopen is based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Initially, we find that the respondent has failed to comply with any of the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that are set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The record shows that the respondent’s appeal brief was due on September 21, 2006. On September 19, 2006, former counsel attempted to file a motion to withdraw as the respondent’s representative, but the motion was denied because former counsel failed to provide the respondent’s last known address.1 The respondent’s current motion states that former counsel attempted to withdraw as his representative “because counsel had been continually unable to contact Respondent for several months” (Motion at 3). On October 11, 2006, former counsel filed a new Entry of Appearance As Attorney and requested an extension of time to file an appeal brief. The briefing extension request was denied as untimely because it was filed after the September 21, 2006, filing deadline originally set by the Board. An appeal brief filed by former counsel on November 13, 2006, was also rejected as untimely.
 
The facts presented in the respondent’s motion do not reflect that former counsel was negligent or otherwise ineffective in representing the respondent. According to the chronology outlined in the respondent’s motion, former counsel was unable to contact the respondent despite repeated efforts to do so, and finally attempted to withdraw as the respondent’s representative. The respondent has not identified any action or inaction of former counsel that caused the rejection of his untimely brief and the dismissal of his appeal. We can only conclude that the dismissal of the respondent’s appeal resulted solely from the respondent’s own failure to maintain communication and assist his former attorney with the preparation of his appeal. Under the circumstances, the respondent has not shown that any prejudice resulted from the representation of his former counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 The respondent’s motion is defective, insofar as it requests reconsideration of our prior order, because it fails to identify any legal or factual errors in our previous decision that warrant reconsideration. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

As reflected in a Notice of Action by the Board, dated October 4, 2006. The deficient motion apparently was returned to former counsel, and is no longer in the record.

2007 WL 1520860 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADAN RANGEL-GARCIA A.K.A. ADAN GARCIA RANGEL A.K.A. ADAN GARCIA RAMGER A.K.A. ADAM GARCIA RANGEL A.K.A. ADAN GARCIA RANGER A.K.A. ADAN RANGEL A.K.A. GARCIA ADAN RANGEL A.K.A. ADAN RANGEL GARCIA
FILE: AXX XX1 091 - LANCASTER
May 4, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul A. Schelly, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey D. Clark,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is dismissed. The respondent appealed from the January 10, 2007, oral decision of the Immigration Judge, which found him removable; determined that he did not establish eligibility for cancellation of removal, voluntary departure, or any other relief from removal; and ordered him removed from the United States to Mexico. A reply brief was submitted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) on March 30, 2007.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusions regarding the respondent’s removability and ineligibility for relief. The Immigration Judge properly found the respondent removable as charged, pursuant to sections 212(a)(6)(A)(i), 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(II), and 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(a)(6)(A)(i), 1182(a)(9)(C)(i)(II), and 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II). The record reflects that the respondent was convicted on May 31, 1996, of possession for sale of a controlled substance, namely cocaine, under California law. We note that this conviction is for a drug trafficking offense that is both an aggravated felony and a particularly serious crime. See Matter of Y-L-, A-G-, and R-S-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 270 (A.G. 2002). The respondent stated that he did not have a fear of persecution or torture from governmental authorities in his native country of Mexico (Tr. at 10).
 
On appeal, the respondent suggests that he is eligible for cancellation of removal, asserting that he arrived in the United States in 1992 rather than the April 2004 date listed on the Notice to Appear. Consistent with the DHS’s appellate position, the respondent is ineligible for both cancellation of removal and voluntary departure due to his aggravated felony conviction, regardless of whether he arrived in this country in 1992 or 2004. See sections 240A(b)(1)(C) and 240B(b)(1)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229b(b)(1)(C) and 1229c(b)(1)(C).
 
Finally, the respondent alleges on the Notice of Appeal (“NTA”) that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent indicates that he himself filed the NTA after his attorney did not do so. With respect to this ineffective assistance claim, which is in remand posture, we note that the respondent has not satisfied any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F. 2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has not forwarded an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement between himself and his attorney; has not shown that the attorney whose competence is being impugned has been informed of the respondent’s allegations against him and has been given an opportunity to respond; and has not communicated whether he has filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities against the attorney. See also Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592 (9th Cir. 2004) (finding that notification to counsel is “required” for an ineffective assistance claim and that a complaint letter to the California State Bar with the notation “cc” to opposing counsel does not satisfy the requirement). We also do not find that a remand is warranted in light of Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002) (holding that rigid adherence to the Lozada requirements is not always required, especially when the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance). We observe that, even assuming the respondent’s Lozada compliance, he has not established that he is eligible for any relief from removal. To the extent that the respondent contends that his removal hearing was unfair, we find that he has not demonstrated any error by the Immigration Judge in conducting the hearing. We also find that the respondent has not demonstrated any resultant prejudice such as would constitute a due process violation. See Almaghzar v. Gonzales, 450 F.3d 415 (9th Cir. 2006).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2007 WL 1520834 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AINSWORTH ANTHONY *S-JOHNSON
FILE: AXX XX8 463 - MIAMI
April 30, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(S))
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(U))
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(A)) (withdrawn)
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, is a lawful permanent resident of the United States. The record establishes that on July 1, 2004, the respondent was convicted in the Circuit Court of Broward County, Florida, of the offenses of: (a) attempted sexual battery in violation of Florida Statutes (F.S.) §§ 777.04 and 797.011(5); and (b) tampering with a witness, in violation of F.S. § 914.22; and he received a sentence of two years incarceration (I.J. at 1-2; Exh. 2). The respondent appeals an Immigration Judge’s February 7, 2007, decision finding that his conviction for tampering with a witness constitutes an aggravated felony, as defined in section 101(a)(43)(S) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U. S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(S), to wit, an offense relating to the obstruction of justice for which the term of imprisonment is at least one year (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge further found that the respondent failed to demonstrate his eligibility for any form of relief from removal (I.J. at 3). The appeal is dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he may not be removed because he has a criminal appeal pending. However, the record establishes that the respondent’s appeal of his criminal conviction was dismissed (Tr. at 2, 11, 17-18; Exh. 3, Appendix G). We acknowledge that the respondent has filed a Petition for Writ of Habeas Corpus with the District Court of Appeal for the Fourth District of Florida, asserting ineffective assistance of counsel in connection with his criminal appeal (Exh. 3, Appendix E). In this regard, we have held that an alien who has exhausted his right to a direct appeal of his criminal conviction is subject to deportation for that conviction, and that the potential for further discretionary review will not affect the finality of the conviction for the purpose of immigration proceedings. See Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894 (BIA 1994). Therefore, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to consider the respondent’s conviction final for purposes of these immigration proceedings. Moreover, the respondent has identified no error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that his conviction for tampering with a witness constitutes an aggravated felony, or asserted that he is eligible for any type of relief from removal (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 12).
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 1430335 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HANI KAZBAR
File: AXX XX4 222 - Los Angeles
April 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aria Shariati, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(G)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(G)(ii)º - Failed to fulfill marital agreement (withdrawn)
Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status (withdrawn)
 
 
Lodged: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Remand; adjustment of status
 
In a decision dated November 16, 2005, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a continuance in order to continue processing an application for adjustment of status; she ordered the respondent removed to Syria. The Immigration Judge had previously granted the respondent numerous continuances in order to await processing of a labor certification required in order to qualify for adjustment of status, but after nearly 3 years had passed, she declined to hold the case in abeyance any longer. The respondent claims that the Immigration Judge made procedural and substantive errors and requests a remand which will be granted. His request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2006).
 
The respondent raises the question of which charging document applies to his case, although for reasons relating to his eligibility for asylum and not to challenge his removability (Respondent’s Br. at 25-27; see also Respondent’s Br. at 28 (arguing he was denied a fair hearing)). The respondent was originally charged under sections 237(a)(1)(G)(ii) and 237(a)(1)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(1)(G)(ii), 1227(a)(1)(C)(i), although these charges were withdrawn by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and substituted with a charge under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i) (Tr. at 2; see Exhs. 1 & 2). The respondent later conceded to removability — we presume — under the section 212 charge (Tr. at 6). However, the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 16, 2005, lists only the section 237 charges in the heading and appears to have found the respondent removable under the section 237 charges; the Immigration Judge notes that exhibit 2 — the lodged 212 charge — was not pled to (I.J. at 2; see Tr. at 32). We find it necessary to remand proceedings in order to obtain a more accurate picture of the respondent’s removability. As the respondent correctly points out, which charging document is sustained affects the respondent’s eligibility for relief, although we note that his failure to file an asylum application belies his claim that he was prevented from filing one due to confusion in the charging documents about when he arrived here. At any rate, he is correct that if he conceded the charges in the original Notice to Appear, he would be time-barred from applying for asylum; if he conceded to the lodged charge, he would be ineligible for adjustment of status. See Eligibility of Arriving Aliens in Removal Proceedings to Apply for Adjustment of Status and Jurisdiction to Adjudicate Applications for Adjustment of Status, 71 Fed. Reg. 27,585-592 (May 12, 2006). Furthermore, he cannot have conceded to both of the charging documents as they present different facts about when the respondent arrived and whether he was admitted.
 
*2 The issue of removability is also related to the respondent’s other claims alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, various procedural due process claims, and his claim that the Immigration Judge erred in finding him ineligible for adjustment of status. First, with regard to the respondent’s claim that he was not adequately represented by his three previous attorneys, all apparently from the same law firm, we can find no basis to remand. We note that the respondent has submitted a supplemental filing which indicates that he has complied with most of the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).1
 
However, we are not able to determine that he suffered prejudice by his attorneys’ actions. He alleges that his former attorneys never investigated the manner in which the respondent was arrested and that, due to the illegality of the arrest, all evidence obtained should be have suppressed (Respondent’s Br. at 21-22). He also argues that former counsel never presented proper evidence of the respondent’s eligibility for adjustment of status (Respondent’s Br. at 22-24). The respondent does not specify what evidence should have been suppressed and it is unclear how his arrest by United States Marshals necessarily affects his removability. However, to the extent he wishes to revisit this issue, he may state his claim before the Immigration Judge on remand and can revisit the issue of eligibility for relief as well. See Matter of Patel, 16 I&N Dec. 600, 601 (BIA 1978) (noting that with certain exceptions, the remand of a case by this Board is effective for not only the stated purpose of the remand, but also for consideration of any and all matters which the Immigration Judge deems appropriate). We decline, however, at this time to find that he was subjected to ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has failed to establish that his arrest was illegal or that the manner in which he was arrested provided a basis for terminating proceedings. With regard to his attorneys’ attempt to aid the respondent in gaining relief from removal, there was no approved labor certification which was necessary for the respondent to adjust his status based on an employment-related visa petition. The respondent has failed to establish that his former counsels’ acts prejudiced him.
 
We also decline to find error in the Immigration Judge’s decision not to grant yet another continuance, although, again, our remand, in effect, provides the respondent with a continued proceeding and, therefore, with the result he seeks. As we have stated before, granting a motion for a continuance is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge, and her decision denying such a motion will not be reversed on appeal unless the alien establishes, by a full and specific articulation of the particular facts involved or evidence which he would have presented, that the denial caused him actual prejudice and harm, and materially affected the outcome of his case. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). Here, the respondent had already been granted several continuances and there remained no evidence that the respondent was eligible for any relief other than voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge properly denied the request for what amounted to an indefinite request to continue proceedings.
 
*3 We note the respondent’s argument that a continuance was warranted in order to allow his current attorney, who was, at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision, new to the case, an opportunity to prepare himself and become familiar with the record of proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 27-28; Tr. at 26). Relatedly, he asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in applying San Francisco local operating rules (the Immigration Judge hailed from that area) while sitting in the Los Angeles immigration court (Respondent’s Br. at 31-32). According to the respondent, previous counsel did not formally withdraw from representing him and should have been present at the hearing; the Immigration Judge’s decision without their presence allegedly violated procedural due process (Respondent’s Br. at 32; see Tr. at 30). We find any error to be harmless because Los Angeles local operating procedures (like the San Francisco rules) specifically state that substitution of counsel is allowed where the request is made orally or in writing by the attorney to be substituted. This was done (Exh. 16).
 
ORDER: The record of proceedings is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings to clarify the charges of removability and to allow the respondent to apply for any relief for which he may be eligible.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Matter of Lozada requires that a motion claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must provide evidence (1) setting forth in detail the agreement entered into between client and counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion to remand or reopen reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. While the respondent has provided evidence that complied with the first and third requirements, we are unable to determine whether he has informed former counsel of the allegations.

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Order Vacated by IN RE: SUPNET, EUGENE PE BENITO, BIA, June 14, 2007
2007 WL 2074430 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EUGENE PE BENITO SUPNET
FILE: AXX XX0 177 - SAN DIEGO
April 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on September 2, 2005. In a motion that was originally received on March 2, 2007, the respondent requested reopening of the proceedings. The respondent alleges in the motion, among other things, that he was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The motion, which is untimely, will be denied.
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or by September 30, 1996, whichever is later. The pending motion was therefore untimely filed, and there is no basis for abrogating the motions deadline here. An individual seeking to rely upon “equitable tolling” of a filing deadline must show that he or she has acted with due diligence. See Albillo-De Leon v. Gonzales, 410 F.3d 1090, 1099-1100 (9th Cir. 2005); Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1186 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
The respondent’s actions do not evidence diligence, as he has not explained why he failed to complain about the assistance of Marc Wigul in a timely manner. Mr. Wigul filed a notice of appearance (Form EOIR-27) on August 19, 2005, after the respondent himself had filed his appeal form. An associate of Mr. Wigul’s, Cindy C. Yu, filed an EOIR-27 on August 19, 2005, and submitted an appellate brief on the respondent’s behalf, although we considered Mr. Wigul still the attorney of record, for purposes of service of our order. In any event, the respondent’s explanation of his complaint against Mr. Wigul’s representation indicates that he had tactical differences with his attorney, which does not necessarily constitute ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º
 
Although the respondent also asserts that he was afforded ineffective assistance of counsel from Robin Carr, Ms. Carr never filed an EOIR-27 with the Board and was never the attorney of record in this case. Any legal assistance provided by Ms. Carr, in regard to a petition for review before the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit would, in any event, be beyond our jurisdiction to consider.¿FN2º
 
Even had the motion been timely filed, we would decline to address the first two issues presented in the motion, since the respondent was not charged with removability as an aggravated felon on account of his conviction for wilful cruelty to a child, and he has presented no evidence that the Ninth Circuit has held, since the Immigration Judge issued his decision, that a violation under section 2800.2 of the California Vehicle Code would not be a crime involving moral turpitude.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied, as is the request for a stay of removal.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

In a letter dated May 24, 2006, the California State Bar informed Mr. Wigul that there were insufficient grounds for disciplinary action, in regard to a complaint that the respondent had apparently filed.

2

In his explanation regarding Ms. Carr’s representation, the respondent curiously states that “On July 2005 ¿sicº I hired Ms. Robin Carr” to represent him in his appeal. Since Mr. Wigul submitted his EOIR-27 in June 2005, and his associate, Ms. Yu, submitted hers in August 2005, it is unclear why Ms. Carr would have failed to submit a formal notice of representation, if she in fact was retained a month after Mr. Wigul filed his notice.

2007 WL 1430787 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ENRIQUE TAPIA GARCIA
File: AXX XX3 646 - El Centro
April 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gregory Olive, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; reissuance of Board decision; stay of deportation
 
This case was last before us on November 1, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s February 23, 2006, decision denying the respondent’s motion to terminate removal proceedings against him. On February 15, 2007, the respondent filed this untimely motion to reopen, including a request to stay his deportation, based on his former counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance in representing him at the administrative level. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (2006). In the alternative, the respondent moves for a reissuance of this Board’s prior decision due to his former counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance in failing to timely file a petition for review before the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The Department of Homeland Security has filed no opposition or other response to this motion, and the motion is deemed unopposed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3). The motion will be granted in part, and denied in part.
 
The respondent concedes that his motion to reopen is untimely; however, he asserts that equitable tolling applies based on the ineffective assistance he received from his former counsel, Robert F. Jacobs. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and upheld in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). We surmise that the respondent argues he should be excused from full compliance with the requirements for presenting an ineffective assistance claim under Lozada because the ineffective assistance is clear on the face of the record. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003) (involving former counsel who failed to file a timely brief before the Board). We disagree. Although the respondent has complied with the first two Lozada requirements, he has not complied with the third requirement, to wit, indicating that a bar complaint has been filed against Mr. Jacobs. “This requirement ensures a certain level of seriousness in the assertion of such charges and ‘highlights the standards which should be expected of attorneys who represent persons in immigration proceedings.”’ Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra, at 825 (quoting Lozada, supra, at 639). Ordinarily, failure to comply with all of the Lozada requirements can be fatal to an alien’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir.2000) (explaining that rigid compliance is not always necessary). We consider this third requirement crucial in discerning a legitimate claim of ineffective assistance from the numerous, baseless claims of ineffective assistance filed with the Board. In Rojas-Garcia, the court found that the claim for ineffective assistance was not fatal because: (1) the ineffective assistance was obvious from the face of the record; and (2) the alien had substantially complied with Lozada since his former counsel self-reported his defective representation to the bar. Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra, at 825-26. Here, the respondent has not met either of these factors because there is no indication of a bar complaint either by the respondent or Mr. Jacobs, and there is no patent ineffective assistance of counsel at the administrative level.1 Therefore, his claim for ineffective assistance of counsel in support of the motion to reopen must fail.
 
*2 We find that even if the respondent had complied with Lozada, he has not established that Mr. Jacobs’ representation at the administrative level was ineffective. The respondent contends that Mr. Jacobs provided ineffective assistance at the administrative level when he did not object to the admission of the smugglee’s statements, and did not submit affidavits or witness testimony from the respondent’s family members to corroborate his story. As evidenced by Mr. Jacobs’ response to questions posed by the respondent’s current counsel, he made a tactical decision to not object to the smugglee’s statements hoping to highlight both internal and external inconsistencies. See Attachment to Motion “February 14, 2007 Letter from Mr. Jacobs.” Although there were inconsistences, as the Immigration Judge’s and our prior decision confirmed, the unmistakable similarities between the smugglee’s and the respondent’s statements regarding the essential elements of the smuggling scheme discredited the respondent’s version of the events at the hearing. Moreover, the Immigration Judge explained how the respondent’s own testimony was replete with implausibilities, inconsistencies, and thereby not credible (I.J. at 2-12; compare Tr. at 10 (sole purpose was to pick up son-in-law’s mother) with Tr. at 19-20 (purpose of trip was to also pick up smugglee and stating he was told to pick up the smugglee 8 days prior to the date he picked her up) with Exh. 2 (respondent’s Record of Sworn Statement). Subsequent dissatisfaction with a strategic decision of counsel is not grounds to reopen. See Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931 (9th Cir. 1986); Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (absent a showing of egregious circumstances, a tactical decision by an attorney is binding on the alien). Regarding the failure to submit affidavits from family members, Mr. Jacobs attempted to obtain such affidavits, including witness testimony, and advised the respondent of their necessity. See “February 14, 2007 Letter from Mr. Jacobs.” In addition, Mr. Jacobs advised the respondent that he should obtain proof such as telephone records, the flight itinerary of his son-in-law’s mother, declarations, and a doctor’s letter stating the mother was too ill to travel that day. Id. The respondent’s failure to obtain any of this evidence or produce any witnesses on his behalf is not attributable to Mr. Jacobs. Although the respondent has submitted documents purporting to be affidavits with this motion, he has not shown how these were unobtainable at the time of his merits hearing; in addition, the respondent has not provided any new evidence which would corroborate even one version of his story. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). ‘Therefore, the respondent has not shown patent or latent ineffective assistance by Mr. Jacobs in his representation at the administrative level, and there exists no good cause to reopen these proceedings.
 
*3 We now turn to the respondent’s motion to reissue our prior decision based on Mr. Jacobs’ ineffective assistance in failing to file a timely petition for review in the Ninth Circuit. We emphasize that any such ineffective assistance is based on Mr. Jacobs’ representation before the Ninth Circuit, and does not implicate any action by the Immigration Judge or the Board. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(b), (c). Nonetheless; the Ninth Circuit has found that such ineffective assistance necessitates that the Board reissue a decision to allow an alien to then timely file an appeal in that forum. See Dearinger ex. rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042, 1045-46 (9th Cir. 2000) (upholding a district court decision ordering the Board to reissue its decision where counsel filed a petition for review 1 day late). Although the respondent has not complied with Lozada to bring an ineffective assistance claim within the scope of the Board’s authority, based on the Dearinger holding and the appellate court’s de novo review of such claims, we find that Mr. Jacobs’ failure to file a petition for review would constitute ineffective assistance before the Ninth Circuit, thereby raising a rebuttable presumption of prejudice. See Dearinger ex. rel. Volkova v. Reno, supra, at 1045. Accordingly, we will grant the motion to reissue.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied, and the request for stay of deportation is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reissue is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated November 1, 2006, is hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.
 
Gerald S. Hurwitz
FOR THE BOARD

1

Mr. Jacobs’ failure to file a petition for review in the Ninth Circuit has no bearing on the analysis of his representation at the administrative level. However, we will revisit such failure in our discussion of the respondent’s motion to reissue our prior decision.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SOON WOO SEO
File: AXX XX8 002 - Haskell
April 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mathew L. Millen, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F..R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated October 26, 2006. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent was ordered removed from the United States because she failed to appear for a hearing on May 16, 2006. She filed a motion to reopen, but the Immigration Judge denied the request on July 28, 2006. That decision was appealed to the Board, but it was dismissed on October 26, 2006. The respondent filed the instant motion to reopen on March 21, 2007. Therein, the respondent argues that these proceedings should be reopened based on “exceptional circumstances” causing her failure to appear. According to the respondent, she was the victim of negligence by her prior counsel who did not adequately advise her of the obligation to appear for her hearing on May 16, 2006. See also Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, 2007 WL 1113912 (5th Cir. April 16, 2007).
 
The instant motion to reopen was filed with the Board more than 90 days after the date in which the final administrative decision by the Board was rendered on October 26, 2006. It is therefore untimely and is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We have also determined that the exceptions to these filing requirements set forth in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3) are inapplicable. In addition, 8 C.F.R. §1003.23(b)(4)(ii) provides that an alien may file only one motion to seek reopening of an order of removal entered in absentia.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Board should equitably toll the time and number requirements for reopening an order of removal entered in absentia based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383 (5th Cir. 2001); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497 (5th Cir. 2000). In the instant case, the respondent indicates in her declaration that she was aware of her hearing. She states that she contacted her former attorney who told her that it should be okay if he represented her at the May 16, 2006, hearing by telephone. The respondent further indicates that former counsel did not instruct her to appear for the hearing, and implies that he may have even told her to not appear. See Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, 2007 WL 1113912 (5th Cir. April 16, 2007). Even if such conduct was negligent, we note that the respondent’s current attorney represented her on appeal with the Board and filed a brief on her behalf. Current counsel should therefore have been familiar with the facts of the respondent’s case at the time, and clearly had the opportunity to discuss the case with the respondent within 90 days of the Board’s prior decision. Under these circumstances, we do not find it appropriate io equitably toll the 90-day period for seeking reopening even if we had the authority to do so. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARNEL PULIDO HAGAPE
File: AXX XX4 151 - Eloy
April 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rodel E. Rodis, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Termination; Waiver of Deportability
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s January 12, 2007, decision ordering the respondent removed from the United States to his native Philippines as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation. Section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i) (2000).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred by ordering him removed on the basis of his 1997 conviction for methamphetamine possession despite evidence that the conviction was the subject of a collateral challenge in California Superior Court. This argument lacks merit, however, because the pendency of a collateral challenge to a criminal conviction has no effect for immigration purposes unless and until the conviction is in fact overturned or materially modified. Grageda v. U.S. I.N.S., 12 F.3d 919, 921 (9th Cir. 1993) (quoting Morales-Alvarado v. INS, 655 F.2d 172, 175 (9th Cir. 1981)); Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). We have received no indication that the respondent’s conviction has been vacated or materially modified, and therefore it constitutes a valid factual predicate for the charge of deportability.
 
The respondent also contends that he was deprived of his due process right to a full and fair hearing because he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his former attorney, who failed to file an application for a waiver of deportability under former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), by a court-imposed deadline.1 Assuming arguendo that counsel’s failure to file the relevant application was defective performance, we nonetheless find no due process violation because counsel’s omission did not materially prejudice the respondent. Specifically, had the application in question been timely filed, it would have been subject to mandatory denial because the ground of deportability sought to be waived was based on a criminal conviction that resulted from a plea agreement entered into after April 1, 1997, the date when former section 212(c) of the Act was repealed. Sections 304(b) and 309(a) of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996 (IIRIRA), Division C of Pub. L. No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009.2 Accordingly, we reject the contention that prior counsel’s ostensibly defective performance violated the respondent’s due process right to a full and fair hearing.
 
*2 In conclusion, the respondent is deportable as charged and has not met his burden of demonstrating that he is eligible for any form of relief from removal. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). Furthermore, the respondent has not proven that his due process rights were violated during the course of his removal proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent also suggests that his former attorney was ineffective by failing to provide the Immigration Judge with evidence reflecting the pendency of his collateral challenge to the conviction underlying the charge of deportability. As we have already noted, however, the pendency of that challenge had no effect on the validity of the removal proceedings.

2

Specifically, the conviction record reflects that the respondent was not arrested for his methamphetamine possession offense until April 6, 1997 (Group Exh. 5, unnumbered page 4), approximately 1 week after former section 212(c) was repealed, and his plea agreement was obviously not entered into until some time thereafter. Consequently, IIRIRA § 304(b) has no impermissible retroactive effect vis-a-vis the respondent.
