2007 WL 1192122 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEVERIANO CARDENAS-MENDOZA, A.K.A. PEDRO RENTERIA-MENDOZA
File: AXX XX8 002 - San Francisco
March 27, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anthony Nwosu, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joseph Y. Park
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(C)(i)(II) º- Reentry without admission after being ordered removed
  
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
On March 24, 2006, an Immigration Judge issued an order granting a motion by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) to reinstate the July 24, 2000, order removing the respondent from the United States. The respondent moved to reopen, asserting ineffective assistance because counsel did not respond in opposition to the DHS’s reinstatement motion. On May 23, 2006, the Immigration Judge issued a decision denying the motion to reopen and rejecting the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim based on his inability to demonstrate that he was prejudiced by counsel’s conduct. The respondent appeals the May 23, 2006, decision. The record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
We briefly recite the procedural history of this case. The respondent was ordered removed on July 24, 2000, after being found inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i), as an alien who falsely represented himself to be a United States citizen for any purpose or benefit under the Act, and under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, as an immigrant not in possession of a valid entry document. On December 8, 2005, he was served with a Notice to Appear, charging him under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(C)(i)(II), with reentering the United States without admission after being removed. The Immigration Judge reinstated the original removal order upon the DHS’s motion, and subsequently denied the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Although the May 23, 2006, order denying the respondent’s motion to reopen is the subject of this appeal, we necessarily must also examine the March 24, 2006, reinstatement order underpinning the motion to reopen. In that order, the Immigration Judge granted the DHS’s motion which sought reinstatement of the respondent’s prior removal order and which also included the filing of a new Notice to Appear, charging the respondent under the reentry-without-admission provision in section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(II). We recently held that an Immigration Judge has no authority to reinstate a prior removal order pursuant to section 241(a)(5) of the Act, which provides
If the Attorney General finds that an alien has reentered the United States illegally after having been removed or having departed voluntarily, under an order of removal, the prior order of removal is reinstated from its original date and is not subject to being reopened or reviewed, the alien is not eligible and may not apply for any relief under this Act, and the alien shall be removed under the prior order at any time after the reentry.
 
*2 Section 241(a)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(a)(5). See Matter of W-C-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 118, 120 (BIA 2007). Here, the motion for reinstatement was accompanied by a Notice to Appear, and once a Notice to Appear has been served on the Immigration Court and jurisdiction has vested therein, the Notice to Appear may not be cancelled. See id. at 120; see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 239.2(a), 1003.14(a). However, the DHS may move to terminate the proceedings for certain enumerated valid reasons, including the fact that the Notice to Appear was improvidently issued. See Matter of W-C-B-, supra, (citing 8 C.F.R. § 239.2(a)(6)).
 
In the instant case, based on new authority set forth in Matter of W-C-B-, the Immigration Judge lacked jurisdiction to reinstate the respondent’s July 24, 2000, removal order. The DHS was authorized to remove him under that order at any time after determining that he had reentered illegally. Instead, the DHS improvidently issued the new Notice to Appear and initiated proceedings. The Immigration Judge could have terminated the proceedings upon a motion by DHS in accordance with the regulations, but no such motion was filed. As the Immigration Judge had no jurisdiction to reinstate the removal order, her March 24, 2006, order will be vacated. The lack of jurisdiction extends to the motion to reopen which was concomitant to the March 24, 2006, reinstatement order, and consequently the May 23, 2006, decision denying that motion similarly will be vacated. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s March 24, 2006, and May 23, 2006, decisions are vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this order and the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1192122 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 1180542 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EMMETT GRENALD
File: A36 489 029 - Newark
March 19, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on October 27, 2006, at which time the Board denied the respondent’s appeal. On December 13, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,”) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent argues that he is a United States citizen based on the United States citizenship of his father. We observe that the respondent’s motion to reopen is timely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). A motion to reopen will not be granted where the evidence supporting the motion was previously available and could have been presented at the previous hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). In the instant case, the respondent presents an affidavit from his father and a copy of this man’s birth certificate. See Motion to Reopen. First, this evidence is not new or previously unavailable evidence. See Tr. at 52 (discussing the respondent’s father). This evidence could have been previously presented to the Immigration Judge and this Board. To the extent that the respondent is arguing that he has an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his prior attorney for failing to submit this evidence, we observe that the respondent has not complied with any of the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Furthermore, as discussed below, the evidence submitted does not establish his eligibility for relief, so the respondent has not shown the required prejudice. See Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98, 107 (3rd Cir. 2005).
 
The respondent’s argument that he is entitled to United States citizenship based on his United States citizen father is incorrect. The burden of proof in establishing alienage in removal proceedings is on the DHS. United States ex rel. Bilokumsky v. Tod, 263 U.S. 149 (1923); United States v. Neelly, 207 F.2d 281 (7th Cir. 1953). However, one born abroad is prima facie an alien. Matter of A-M-, 7 I&N Dec. 332 (BIA 1956). Furthermore, where it is undisputed that an alien sought to be deported was born in a foreign country and came to the United States from that country, the burden of proof to establish United States citizenship is upon the alien. United States v. Neelly, supra. Such an alien is required to affirmatively establish his citizenship claim. Matter of A-M-, supra.
 
*2 In the instant case, it is uncontested that the respondent was born in Panama and was born out of wedlock. See Exh. 1; Motion to Reopen. A child born out of wedlock to a United States citizen father, bears the additional burden of establishing a relationship to the father which is legally recognized under our immigration law. Specifically, the respondent must satisfy the requirements outlined in current section 309(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1409.
 
We observe that the respondent was born on December 23, 1971 (Exh. 2). Children born after November 14, 1971, must comply with the requirements of the current version of section 309 of the Act. See Pub. L. 100-525, § 23(e)(3), 102 Stat. 2619. Current section 309 of the amended Act provides, in pertinent part:
(a) The provisions of paragraphs (c), (d), (e), and (g) of section 301, and of paragraph (2) of section 308 shall apply as of the date of birth to a person born out of wedlock if -
(1) a blood relationship between the person and the father is established by clear and convincing evidence.
 
(2) the father had the nationality of the United States at the time of the person’s birth.
 
(3) the father (unless deceased) has agreed in writing to provide financial support for the person until the person reaches the age of 18 years and
 
(4) while the person is under the age of 18 years -
(A) the person is legitimated under the law of the person’s residence or domicile.
 
(B) the father acknowledges paternity of the person in writing under oath, or
 
(C) the paternity of the person is established by adjudication of a competent court.
 
 
 
 
We observe that the evidence submitted by the respondent, who is now 35 years-old, does not establish that the respondent meets the requirements under current section 309(a)(4) of the Act. Specifically, the affidavit submitted does not suggest that the respondent’s father had legitimated the respondent, acknowledged his paternity under oath or had his paternity established in court prior to the respondent’s eighteenth birthday. See Motion to Reopen, affidavit of Emmet Ryan King, (noting that he is not listed on the respondent’s birth certificate).
 
In setting forth the effective date of the ““new”” section 309(a), the Immigration Technical Corrections Act of 1988 (
Pub. L. 100-525, 102 Stat 2609
(Oct. 24, 1988), amended the INA to provide that “except as provided in paragraph (2)03)
 
the new section 309(a)
 
shall apply to persons who have not attained 18 years of age as of the date of the enactment of this Act” (i.e., by November 14, 1986). That paragraph provides that “(2) [t]he old section 309(a) shall apply (A) to any individual who has attained 18 years of age as of the date of enactment of this Act, and (B) any individual with respect to whom paternity was established by legitimation before such date.” See section 23(e)(1) of the Immigration Technical Corrections Act of 1988 (P.L. 100-525, 102 Stat 2609 (Oct. 24, 1988).
 
*3 We next turn to the question of whether the respondent has shown that he was legitimated before November 14, 1986 (the INAA effective date) for purposes of “old” section 309(a) of the Act. Under the Act, legitimation can take place either under the laws of the respondent’s place of birth or under the laws of the state of the respondent’s father’s domicile. See section 101(b)(1) of the Act. In addition to the terms of the “(2)(B)” exception requiring legitimation to take place by November 14, 1986, legitimation must take place prior to the child reaching the age of 21. See former section 309(a) of the Act. In this case, the affidavit from the respondent’s father indicates that he was not included on the respondent’s birth certificate and left Panama shortly after the respondent’s birth. See Motion to Reopen, affidavit of Emmet Ryan King. There is no evidence to suggest that the respondent’s father acknowledged his paternity in Panama before November 14, 1986. See Matter of Sinclair, 13 I&N Dec. 613 (BIA 1970)(finding the a declaration of paternity in Panama before the Mayor of Colon sufficient to legitimate an out-of-wedlock child). The record indicates the respondent’s father’s resided in New Jersey from 1971-1985. The record is unclear as to which state he resided in from 1985 until November 14, 1986. However, there is no evidence establishing that the respondent’s father legitimated him under the laws of any state during this time period. We note that the burden is on the respondent, who was born in Panama, to establish his citizenship. For reasons discussed above, we find that the respondent has not met his burden. We therefore decline to reopen these proceedings. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1180542 (BIA)
End of Document

APPLICATION: Reopening

2007 WL 1114122 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA SABAS GOMEZ-NAVA
File: A72 309 675 - Los Angeles
March 19, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elsa I. Martinez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James M. Left
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on August 17, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s November 30, 2004, decision denying the respondent’s second motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered deported in absentia on June 27, 1997.1 The respondent filed a petition for review of our decision with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. On September 19, 2006, the Ninth Circuit granted an unopposed motion to remand and returned the record to the Board for further proceedings. The unopposed motion sought a remand because the Board had not sufficiently articulated its reasons for dismissing the appeal. The Board’s August 17, 2005, decision will be vacated and the respondent’s appeal reinstated. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent raises a variety of issues, each of which will be addressed in turn. In its appellate brief, the DHS states that the respondent’s Notice of Appeal was not timely filed. It contends that the Board lacks jurisdiction over the appeal as a result of the untimely filing.
 
We first address the issue of jurisdiction. The record reflects that although the Immigration Judge rendered his decision on November 30, 2004, the decision was not mailed to the parties until December 7, 2004. The respondent’s appeal was received at the Board on January 6, 2005. Thus, the respondent’s motion was filed within 30 days of the mailing of the decision and is therefore timely.
 
We next examine the merits of the respondent’s appeal. The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that her motion was numerically barred when there are no time and number restrictions on motions to reopen in which an alien claims to have lacked notice of a scheduled hearing and was ordered deported in absentia. We agree. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(A)(2). However, the respondent raises in her second motion essentially the same argument that she raised in her first motion - namely that the notice was not properly addressed.2 Moreover, she was aware of the fact that she had been ordered deported in absentia at the time that she filed her first motion to reopen yet waited over 7 years to file a second motion to reopen in which she again alleged a lack of notice. An alien cannot file what is essentially the same motion to reopen over and over. Thus, this issue has been fully litigated.
 
The respondent further contends on appeal that her lack of notice constitutes an exceptional circumstance which serves to equitably toll the numerical restrictions on motions to reopen. She states that she provided her correct address to the then Immigration and Naturalization Service (INS) officer and that due to an INS clerical error, her apartment number was omitted from her address which resulted in her not receiving notice of her scheduled hearing. We disagree. As a motion to reopen premised upon a claim of exceptional circumstances, the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely because it was not filed within 180 days of the respondent’s in absentia order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(B)(4)(ii). We also do not find that this untimeliness is subject to equitable tolling. Although the respondent contends that her former attorney did not advise her of the Immigration Judge’s denial of her first motion to reopen, she had not established that she exercised due diligence in this case. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion on September 18, 1997, yet the respondent did not file a second motion to reopen until October 29, 2004, over 7 years later. The respondent does not state when, or how often, she contacted her former attorney or describe the efforts that she undertook to remain apprised of the status of the motion to reopen that her former attorney had filed on her behalf. We find that these facts do not support a finding that the respondent exercised due diligence in this case. As she has not demonstrated the requisite due diligence, the untimeliness of her exceptional circumstances claim is not subject to the doctrine of equitable tolling.
 
*2 She also asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in stating that he could not construe the Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS) lack of response to her motion as agreement to reopen since the regulation provides that a motion is deemed unopposed unless a timely response is made. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(iv). The lack of a response from the non-moving party does not mean that a party’s motion to reopen must be granted regardless of its content or merits. In all cases, a party moving to reopen proceedings has the burden of demonstrating that proceedings should be reopened. The respondent in this case failed to meet that burden for the reasons set forth in this decision. Therefore, if the Immigration Judge erred on this point, such error is harmless.
 
The respondent additionally claims that her former attorney, Donald K. Smith, Esquire, did not inform her of the contents of the affidavit that she signed in her first motion to reopen, included in the motion incorrect facts regarding whether she ever lived on the Valley View Street, and failed to inform her of the Immigration Judge’s denial of her first motion to reopen. From these arguments, the respondent appears to be claiming that she received ineffective assistance of counsel from Mr. Smith. She has not, however, complied with the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and re-affirmed in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that motions to reopen alleging ineffective assistance can be denied by the Board where the alien does not meet the Lozada requirements as such rules are reasonable in ordinary circumstances). The respondent does not explain her failure to comply with the Lozada requirements. As a result of such failure, Mr. Smith is deprived of an opportunity to respond to these allegations and the Board is deprived of Mr. Smith’s responses.
 
Lastly, the respondent states that she is adjustment of status eligible because she has an approved visa petition (Form I-130) which was filed by her lawful permanent resident husband. She advises that her husband is in the process of applying for naturalization. However, the respondent has provided no evidence showing that she is married, her husband is a lawful permanent resident, her husband filed a visa petition on her behalf, or that a visa petition for her has been approved. Statements of counsel are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Thus, the respondent has not established that she is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The Board’s August 17, 2005, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is reinstated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Juan P. Osuna
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Following the issuance of the in absentia order, the respondent filed with the Immigration Judge, on August 18, 1997, a motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on September 18, 1997. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen with the Immigration Court on October 29, 2004. It is the denial of this second motion which the respondent appealed to the Board and which was the subject of our August 17, 2005, decision.

2

We note that the respondent stated in her first motion that she did not live at the address provided on the hearing notice whereas she said in her second motion that she did live at that address but that such address omitted her apartment number. We consider these arguments, when viewed in the context of the entire record, as a minor difference of degree since the thrust of her arguments is that the “7777 Valley View Street” address was incorrect since it was incomplete.

IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elsa I. Martinez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James M. Left
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on August 17, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s November 30, 2004, decision denying the respondent’s second motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered deported in absentia on June 27, 1997.1 The respondent filed a petition for review of our decision with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. We find that these facts do not support a finding that the respondent exercised due diligence in this case. As she has not demonstrated the requisite due diligence, the untimeliness of her exceptional circumstances claim is not subject to the doctrine of equitable tolling.
 
*2 She also asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in stating that he could not construe the Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS) lack of response to her motion as agreement to reopen since the regulation provides that a motion is deemed unopposed unless a timely response is made. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(iv). The lack of a response from the non-moving party does not mean that a party’s motion to reopen must be granted regardless of its content or merits. In all cases, a party moving to reopen proceedings has the burden of demonstrating that proceedings should be reopened. The respondent in this case failed to meet that burden for the reasons set forth in this decision. Therefore, if the Immigration Judge erred on this point, such error is harmless.
 
The respondent additionally claims that her former attorney, Donald K. Smith, Esquire, did not inform her of the contents of the affidavit that she signed in her first motion to reopen, included in the motion incorrect facts regarding whether she ever lived on the Valley View Street, and failed to inform her of the Immigration Judge’s denial of her first motion to reopen. From these arguments, the respondent appears to be claiming that she received ineffective assistance of counsel from Mr. Smith. She has not, however, complied with the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and re-affirmed in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that motions to reopen alleging ineffective assistance can be denied by the Board where the alien does not meet the Lozada requirements as such rules are reasonable in ordinary circumstances). The respondent does not explain her failure to comply with the Lozada requirements. As a result of such failure, Mr. Smith is deprived of an opportunity to respond to these allegations and the Board is deprived of Mr. Smith’s responses.
 
Lastly, the respondent states that she is adjustment of status eligible because she has an approved visa petition (Form I-130) which was filed by her lawful permanent resident husband. She advises that her husband is in the process of applying for naturalization. However, the respondent has provided no evidence showing that she is married, her husband is a lawful permanent resident, her husband filed a visa petition on her behalf, or that a visa petition for her has been approved. Statements of counsel are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Thus, the respondent has not established that she is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The Board’s August 17, 2005, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is reinstated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Juan P. Osuna
FOR THE BOARD

The Immigration Judge denied the motion on September 18, 1997. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen with the Immigration Court on October 29, 2004. It is the denial of this second motion which the respondent appealed to the Board and which was the subject of our August 17, 2005, decision.

We consider these arguments, when viewed in the context of the entire record, as a minor difference of degree since the thrust of her arguments is that the “7777 Valley View Street” address was incorrect since it was incomplete.

2007 WL 1169638 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AHMAD HAMZE REDA
File: AXX XX7 299 - Detroit
March 15, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tamara French, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on August 24, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s December 15, 2004, decision denying his motion to reopen. Previously on July 20, 2004, the Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s sole application for relief in the form of voluntary departure and granted such relief pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), but required that the respondent depart by November 17, 2004. On November 28, 2006, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen in order to apply for adjustment of status based on an approved I-130 immigrant petition filed by his United States citizen wife. The motion will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, given that we previously dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s December 15, 2004, decsion1 due to the respondent’s failure to present a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, our August 24, 2005, decision remains the final administrative order. See Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999) (stating that an administrative decision becomes final when the Board reaches a decision on appeal); see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Further, as the respondent failed to file his current motion within 90 days of our prior decision, his motion is untimely. Also, as the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen in these proceedings, he is barred by the numerical limitations for a motion to reopen. See sections 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7) (stating that a party may file only one motion to reopen, and that such a motion must be filed within 90 days following the date of the final administrative decision); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
Even considering the respondent’s current motion, which is primarily based upon suffering ineffective assistance by his former attorney, Mr. Terence Hoerman, we do not find that the respondent complied with the procedural requirements for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel.2 The respondent alleges, inter alia, that his previous counsel provided ineffective assistance given that the prior motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge “lack¿edº substance, fail¿edº to comply with all ¿theº requirements of Matter of Lozada, and lack¿edº his application for relief” (Respondent’s Motion at 5-9). The respondent also alleges that he was prejudiced by his former attorney’s actions given that Mr. Hoerman “failed to file a timely motion” to the Immigration Court and provided him with inaccurate advice (Respondent’s Motion at 8). We note, however, that while the respondent submitted a complaint to the Michigan Attorney Grievance Commission (MAGC) regarding Mr. Hoerman’s alleged misconduct (Respondent’s Motion at Tab H) — which was ultimately dismissed, as discussed below — there is no evidence that the respondent conveyed such information to Mr. Hoerman himself. Rather, while the respondent states that he “mailed his complaint to the ¿MAGCº who in turn mailed it to Mr. Hoerman” (Respondent’s Motion at 8), he has failed to provide any case law or other legal authority stating that a State Bar’s correspondence to the impugned attorney is a sufficient substitute for directly informing the attorney of the alleged misconduct. See Matter of Assaad, supra, at 556 (“the alien must inform counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance and give him or her the opportunity to respond”); see also Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407, 414 (6th Cir. 2003) (“Scorteanu needed only to notify former attorney Muresan of the charge of ineffective assistance of counsel.”). Thus, as the respondent has not explained why he chose to rely on the MAGC to inform Mr. Hoerman of the complaint, rather than contacting Mr. Hoerman directly, he has not met the criteria of demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Notwithstanding the respondent’s failure to satisfy the aforementioned procedural requirements, we find that he has not demonstrated prejudice by his former counsel’s representation. See Allabani v. Gonzales, 402 F.3d 668, 677-78 (6th Cir. 2005). The record shows that the MAGC — after investigating the respondent’s charges against Mr. Hoerman — issued a decision on August 24, 2006, concluding that Mr. Hoerman’s actions, as alleged by the respondent, did not constitute professional misconduct (Respondent’s Motion at Tab J). Moreover, the respondent fails to show any error in the MAGC’s decision, nor does he reconcile its conclusion with his continued allegations of ineffective assistance regarding Mr. Hoerman’s representation (Respondent’s Motion at 5). See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638; see also Denko v. INS, supra, at 723-24 (“A party must prove prejudice by alleging facts that permit the court to infer that competent counsel would have acted otherwise.”) (quoting Taniguchi v. Schultz, 303 F.3d 950, 958 (9th Cir. 2002)).3 Further, the fact that the respondent is now dissatisfied with Mr. Hoerman’s strategy in pursuing relief suggests that he is disappointed in his tactical decisions rather than the substance of his representation. As we have previously held, complaints based on specific techniques in pursuing relief are not grounds for reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998). Thus, based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion does not clearly demonstrate that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s representation.
 
In addition, we are unpersuaded by the respondent’s argument that he merits equitable tolling of the time and number limitations for his current motion to reopen (Respondent’s Motion at 9-11). Specifically, given that the respondent has not presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as discussed above, any argument requesting that the time or numeric limitations for reopening be equitably tolled is unavailing. See generally Harchenko v. INS, 379 F.3d 405, 409-10 (6th Cir. 2004). The respondent also alleges that he was prevented from filing the instant motion until an error committed by the Citizenship and Immigration Services of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS, formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) — in processing the respondent’s wife’s I-130 petition — was corrected, thereby resulting in his failure to meet the time limitation for filing a motion to reopen (Respondent’s Motion at 9-11; Tab D). However, we need not address the significance of the DHS’s error as allegedly causing a delay in the respondent’s filing of the current motion given that he does not meaningfully address his violation of the numerical limitations described above, or demonstrate that reopening would be warranted except for the DHS’s processing error (Respondent’s Br. at 9-11). See Scorteanu v. INS, supra, at 413 (“this court need not decide” whether equitable tolling should apply given the petitioner’s failure “to prove entitlement to equitable relief”). To the extent that the respondent argues that he was denied due process as a result of the aforementioned errors by the DHS and his former attorneys (Respondent’ s Motion at 11-12), we first note that the Board has no jurisdiction over the applications and matters considered by the DHS. Moreover, the respondent has not demonstrated how he was denied fundamental fairness in these removal proceedings. See id. (“The alien must prove that ineffective assistance of counsel resulted in prejudice or denial of fundamental fairness in order to prove a denial of due process.”): Rather, the record shows that he had ample opportunities to demonstrate ineffective assistance regarding his previous attorneys, but that all such complaints were found to be meritless. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640 (“The respondent received a full and fair hearing at which he was given every opportunity to present his case.”).
 
*3 To the extent that the respondent seeks a stay of removal (Respondent’s Motion at 10), we note that he has not shown why the execution of his removal order should be stayed and accordingly deny his motion in this regard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(f). Finally, we find that the circumstances presented in the respondent’s motion are not “exceptional” as to justify a sua sponte reopening of the matter. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, which was filed on November 12, 2004, on three grounds based on the respondent’s (1) untimely filing of the motion, (2) failure to comply with the motion to reopen requirements, including his failure to provide any application for relief or supporting evidence, (3) failure to comply with the procedural requirements for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel. See I.J.’s December 15, 2004, decision.

2

We have held that a motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639-40. In addition to complying with the procedural requirements, a respondent must also show that “he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance.” See id. at 638; Denko v. INS, 351 F.3d 717 (6th Cir. 2003).

3

Additionally, we note that the MAGC twice dismissed the respondent’s complaints regarding the alleged ineffective assistance of a prior attorney, Mr. James Hoare (Respondent’s Motion at Tabs L, M), whose supposed misconduct, the respondent now argues, was not adequately presented by Mr. Hoerman. Specifically, in a decision dated September 29, 2004, the MAGC found the respondent’s allegations against Mr. Hoare to be insufficient to warrant further proceedings — a conclusion that was reaffirmed in its May 24, 2006, decision—while stating that a “failure to achieve a ¿successfulº result” is not “by itself, evidence of misconduct” (Respondent’s Motion at Tabs L, M).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KWABENA ESSUMAN
File: AXX XX9 412 - Denver
March 15, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Laura L. Lichter, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion seeking to have the Board reopen his proceedings sua sponte. The motion, not opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
These proceedings were last before us on July 14, 2005, at which time we denied the respondent’s timely motion to reopen on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.1 While we found, therein, that the respondent had met the procedural requirements pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we ultimately found that we had no jurisdiction to allow the respondent to seek recourse before the DHS for any alleged ineffective assistance he provided in seeking the respondent’s visa petition. Prior to this July 14, 2005, decision, we also found, on January 26, 2005, that the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s July 25, 2003, decision was without merit; specifically, we found that the respondent’s request for a continuance to adjudicate his third visa petition was without good cause due to the fact that the evidence indicated that the underlying marriage was not bona fide.
 
As the respondent explains in his current motion, the basis of his prior ineffective assistance of counsel motion, the basis of the Immigration Judge’s 2003 denial of his adjustment claim and our dismissal of his appeal therefrom, and the ongoing denial of his continuance requests, all involve a finding that the respondent’s first visa petition involved fraud (Tr. at 18-19). As the respondent explained at his 2003 hearing, and in numerous subsequent briefs, a fraudulent birth certificate was filed with this petition without his knowledge. Subsequently, the respondent explained that his prior counsel failed to address this fraud in his request to approve a second petition.2 Notwithstanding his inability to properly explain the circumstances surrounding this first fraudulent visa petition, the adjudication of his adjustment of status application was continually pretermitted due to the correct assumption that he would be barred from an approved visa petition due to section 204(c) of the Act.
 
In his current motion, the respondent has submitted evidence that his third visa petition has been approved, notwithstanding the bar set forth at section 204(c) of the Act. While no evidence exists in the record save the respondent’s approval notice, and we are aware that the respondent still must demonstrate eligibility for relief as a matter of discretion, the respondent has now demonstrated prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. We previously found that the respondent had met the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, and his claim therein involved the events surrounding his first visa petition, we believe it appropriate to provide the respondent the opportunity to present his case before the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 Accordingly, we will reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). On remand to the Immigration Judge the respondent will have an opportunity to present his application for adjustment of status, including his worthiness for such relief as a matter of discretion. The DHS shall have the opportunity to examine and verify the validity of the respondent’s submissions and present evidence.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2007 WL 1114138 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMAD YAHYA WALID
File: AXX XX2 496 - New York
March 15, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anton F. Mikel, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Timothy Maguire
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed from an Immigration Judge decision denying a motion to reconsider the denial of a motion to reopen. As we agree with the Immigration Judge, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on November 22, 2000. He then flied a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on April 7, 2006. A motion to rescind an order entered in absentia, must be filed within 180 days of the entry of the order and demonstrate extraordinary circumstances for the respondent’s failure to appear, or the respondent must show he or she did not receive proper notice of the hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown that any of the exceptions apply to this case. We first note that, despite his claim that he lacked proper notice due to inadequate translation, he was represented by counsel previously and proper notice of the hearing was provided to both his attorney of record and to the address the respondent provided to the court (Exh. 3). Contrary to further arguments on appeal, this situation does involve the respondent alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, although this is a questionable claim, as the court also informed the respondent directly of the hearing date at the address provided. Further, the respondent was certainly aware he was in proceedings and took no steps to determine the status of his case for over 5 years. In this regard, while he argues that he has exercised due diligence in acting shortly after his claim for asylum became available to him, he did not act with due diligence in determining the status of his immigration case.
 
We next turn to the respondent’s argument, citing Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), that these regulations relate to the recission of the previous order, rather than the reopening he seeks. In Matter of M-S-, supra, the Board held that where an alien who did not receive oral warnings of the consequences of failing to appear at a deportation hearing pursuant to section 242B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(a), moves to reopen deportation proceedings held in absentia under section 242B(c) of the Act in order to apply for a form of relief that was unavailable at the time of the hearing, the rescission requirements prescribed by section 242B(c)(3) of the Act are not applicable. Instead, the motion to reopen is subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F R. § 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3). We note that the respondent is in removal rather than deportation proceedings. Nonetheless, even considering the respondent’s motion as one to reopen rather than to rescind, he was required to have filed it in a timely fashion, within 90 days of the final order.
 
*2 We also find the respondent’s arguments that his motion should be allowed based on changed country conditions, to be unpersuasive. We take note of the horrors his current spouse endured and of her subsequent grant of asylum. Nonetheless, the fact that the respondent recently married her in the United States, is not a change of country conditions in their native Syria, but rather a personal change here in the United States. This is the case, despite the problems the respondent alleges his family has had as a result of his marriage. Federal regulations lift the time and number requirements for motions to reopen:
based on changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality or in the country to which removal has been ordered, if such evidence is material and could not have been discovered and presented at the previous hearing ….
 
8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not adequately established “changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality” to qualify for the exception to the time and number requirements for motions to reopen. The regulatory language in question is narrowly written, and refers exclusively to changed conditions “arising in the country of nationality” which have emerged after a final order of deportation or removal. There is no room to read in a broader interpretation, such as changes an alien experiences in the United States, or any events which occur before the final deportation order.1 The respondent’s marriage is a personal change of circumstances which has arisen outside of his country of nationality. And despite the alleged repercussions felt by his family in Syria, we cannot reasonably read the regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4) as encompassing this change in personal status.
 
In closing, we first note that to the extent the respondent is arguing that our holding in Matter of A-N- and R-M-N-, 22 I&N Dec. 953 (BIA 1999)should apply to this case, our holding in that decision involved exclusion proceedings and was decided under a different statutory and regulatory framework than the one applicable to the case before us. And, finally, while we acknowledge the respondent’s arguments concerning the application of international law to his situation, the jurisdiction of both the Immigration Judge and this Board is defined by regulation and cannot be enlarged without the express authorization of Congress or the Attorney General. We find that, here, the Immigration Judge appropriately applied the laws and regulations as written. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Gerald S. Hurwitz
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We compare this to the exception to the one year filing deadline for asylum applications, which refers to “¿cºhanges in objective circumstances relating to the applicant in the United States ….” 8 C.F.R. § 1208.4(a)(4)(i); see also section 208(a)(2) of the Act. This broader standard does not apply to motions to reopen.

2007 WL 1114143 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUCIO ANGEL PINTO A.K.A. LUCIO PINTO BUSTILLOS
File: AXX XX2 217 - Las Vegas
March 13, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ulrich Smith, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles W. Baccus
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent is a male native and citizen of Peru. At his final hearing on February 14, 2006, the respondent, through counsel, discussed his options for applying for adjustment of status. See Tr. at 47-61. In a decision dated February 14, 2006, an Immigration Judge granted the respondent 120 days in which to voluntarily depart with an alternate order of removal to Peru. On his order, the Immigration Judge wrote “¿aºny motion to reopen for any other relief that may be available to respondent must be filed by COB on 5-15-2006, with the Court waiving the $110.00 filing fee.” The Immigration Judge’s decision noted that the respondent’s voluntary departure period would expire on June 14, 2006.
 
On June 12, 2006, the respondent, through counsel, filed a “Respondent’s Proof of I-130 Filing” which was accompanied by a completed visa petition (Form I-130) filed on his behalf by his father, proof that his father obtained lawful permanent resident status on May 2, 2006, a translated copy of the respondent’s birth certificate, and proof of filing and payment of fees regarding the visa petition. In an order dated June 15, 2006, the Immigration Judge found that the document filed by the respondent was not a motion to reopen, noted that since there was no motion to reopen pending the respondent’s voluntary departure period was not tolled, and stated that his February 14, 2006, order was administratively final for all purposes. The respondent filed a timely appeal of this decision on Monday, July 18, 2006.
 
On appeal, counsel for the respondent states that he filed proof that the respondent had a pending visa petition which provided a basis for his not voluntarily departing. He notes that such proof was filed prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period but after the deadline for the filing of a motion to reopen. Counsel also states that he was not aware that he was required to file a motion to reopen and would have done so had he known it was required. He asks that his mistake not be held against the respondent. The respondent seeks adjustment of status and does not claim to be eligible for any other form of relief from removal.
 
*2 Counsel’s statements on appeal clearly indicate that the document filed on June 12, 2006, was not a motion to reopen and was not intended to be viewed as a motion to reopen since there would be no reason otherwise for counsel to state on appeal that he would have filed a motion to reopen had he been aware that such a motion was required. Therefore, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the document was not a motion to reopen and that it did not serve to toll the respondent’s voluntary departure period.
 
Moreover, the record establishes that the Immigration Judge and counsels for the respondent and the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) discussed the options available to the respondent. See Tr. at 54-61. The Immigration Judge clearly stated that he would grant the respondent 120 days of voluntary departure but would reopen proceedings if a motion to reopen were filed accompanied by evidence that the respondent was eligible for adjustment of status. Id. This same information, along with the deadline for the timely filing of a motion to reopen, was clearly written on the Immigration Judge’s decision, a copy of which was provided to the respondent. Neither the respondent nor his attorney dispute these facts. In light of the undisputed facts of record, counsel’s statements on appeal that he was unaware of the need to file a motion to reopen and would have done so if required reflects ineffective assistance by counsel. See Maravilla-Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004) (holding that an attorney provides ineffective assistance of counsel if competent counsel would have acted otherwise).
 
Because we find that counsel for the respondent was ineffective, we next examine whether the respondent was prejudiced by counsel’s actions. Id. Substantial prejudice must be shown and such prejudice means that the alleged ineffectiveness must have affected the outcome of proceedings. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000). We find that the respondent was not prejudiced by counsel’s actions. At his February 14, 2006, hearing, the Immigration Judge and the parties discussed the respondent’s potential for immediate eligibility for adjustment of status through his mother if 1) she quickly adjusted her status under the Cuban Adjustment Act based upon her marriage to a Cuban citizen, and 2) the respondent obtained a divorce from his wife, whom he could not locate, prior his reaching his 21st birthday on May 30, 2006. See Tr. at 54-61. The visa petition that was submitted with the document filed by counsel reflects that the beneficiary’s father filed the Form I-130 on the respondent’s behalf. It reflects the respondent as “divorced” but shows the termination date of his marriage as “pending.” Thus, this petition does not clearly reflect that the respondent is eligible for a visa since visas are available only for married sons and daughters of United States citizens (as third preference visas). Since the respondent’s father became a lawful permanent resident in May 2006 and the respondent has provided no evidence that his divorce is final, he cannot establish eligibility for adjustment of status since he cannot show that a visa is immediately available to him. Because the respondent is not eligible for adjustment of status, he cannot show prejudice from counsel’s failure to file a motion to reopen for adjustment of status with the Immigration Judge.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Gerald S. Hurwitz
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1114143 (BIA)

2007 WL 1153926 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUILLERMO LENIN GARCIA MONTENEGRO
File: A97 955 387 - Miami
March 12, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rick Hujber, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on October 12, 2006, because he failed to appear for his hearing. He requested reopening with an Immigration Judge on November 2, 2006, but the Immigration Judge denied the motion on November 6, 2006. On December 6, 2006, the respondent requested reconsideration with the Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on December 7, 2006. On December 12, 2006, the respondent requested that the Immigration Judge reopen these proceedings sua sponte. Therein, the respondent argued that his former counsel did not inform him of the October 12, 2006, hearing date. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on December 14, 2006, and this appeal followed. The record will be remanded.
 
The respondent argues on appeal and in his remand request that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. In addition, we note that the Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeals requires substantial if not exact compliance with the procedural requirements set forth above. See Dakane v. U.S. Atty.Gen., 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005); Gbaya v. U.S. Atty.Gen., 342 F.3d 1219 (11th Cir. 2003).
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondent and his wife indicated that the respondent’s former counsel did not inform him of his hearing date. The respondent, however, did not provide an affidavit, but we note that the record contains a sworn statement from the respondent’s wife. In addition, the record contains a statement from the respondent’s former counsel, Jorge Rivera, that was provided on appeal. Therein, Mr. Rivera admits that his former employee, Joseph Lackey, Esquire, was handling the respondent’s case, and did not notify the respondent of the hearing scheduled for October 12, 2006. Based on this information, we find that the respondent’s failure to appear for his hearing was caused by ineffective representation of counsel. We further note that the respondent is married to a United States citizen, she has filed a visa petition on his behalf, and the respondent has diligently sought reopening of these proceedings. Based on these factors, we find that an exceptional situation exists herein to warrant sua sponte reopening of proceedings in the exercise of discretion. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
Charles Adkin-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1153926 (BIA)

IN RE: GUILLERMO LENIN GARCIA MONTENEGRO

March 12, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rick Hujber, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on October 12, 2006, because he failed to appear for his hearing. He requested reopening with an Immigration Judge on November 2, 2006, but the Immigration Judge denied the motion on November 6, 2006. On December 6, 2006, the respondent requested reconsideration with the Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on December 7, 2006. On December 12, 2006, the respondent requested that the Immigration Judge reopen these proceedings sua sponte. Therein, the respondent argued that his former counsel did not inform him of the October 12, 2006, hearing date. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on December 14, 2006, and this appeal followed. The record will be remanded.
 
The respondent argues on appeal and in his remand request that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. In addition, we note that the Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeals requires substantial if not exact compliance with the procedural requirements set forth above. See Dakane v. U.S. Atty.Gen., 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005); Gbaya v. U.S. Atty.Gen., 342 F.3d 1219 (11th Cir. 2003).
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondent and his wife indicated that the respondent’s former counsel did not inform him of his hearing date. The respondent, however, did not provide an affidavit, but we note that the record contains a sworn statement from the respondent’s wife. In addition, the record contains a statement from the respondent’s former counsel, Jorge Rivera, that was provided on appeal. Therein, Mr. Rivera admits that his former employee, Joseph Lackey, Esquire, was handling the respondent’s case, and did not notify the respondent of the hearing scheduled for October 12, 2006. Based on this information, we find that the respondent’s failure to appear for his hearing was caused by ineffective representation of counsel. We further note that the respondent is married to a United States citizen, she has filed a visa petition on his behalf, and the respondent has diligently sought reopening of these proceedings. Based on these factors, we find that an exceptional situation exists herein to warrant sua sponte reopening of proceedings in the exercise of discretion. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).

2007 WL 1192273 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VENKATASARAS CHANDRA MANGILIPUDI A.K.A. BABU MANGILIPUDI
File: AXX XX8 358 - Krome
March 9, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se1
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kathy Giraitis
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(C), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C)º - Controlled substance trafficker
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 31, 2006, finding him inadmissible under sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and (II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and (II), based on his record of conviction; as well as pretermitting his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), will be dismissed.
 
The respondent has presented no arguments on appeal that would cause us to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. At the outset, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s contention that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he had not shown that his 2002 Florida delivery of hydrocodone conviction,2 was not to be considered a conviction for an aggravated felony as defined by section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act3, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B), thereby precluding his eligibility for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a)(3) of the Act., 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(3). The respondent argues that since hydrocodone is not listed as a controlled substance under the Controlled Substance Act (“CSA”), his conviction is not an aggravated felony under the Act.4
 
Initially, we agree that hydrocodone does not appear in any of the CSA schedules. We observe, however, that Drug Enforcement Agency (“DEA”) regulations, promulgated after passage of the CSA and published in the Code of Federal Regulations, have expressly classified hydrocodone as a Schedule II controlled substance, 21 C.F.R. § 1308.12(b)(1) (1993). Moreover, we note that these accretions have the force of law pursuant to 21 U.S.C. § 811(a) (authorizing the Attorney General to add substances to the CSA schedules) and 28 C.F.R. pt. 0.100(b) (delegating that authority to the DEA). Thus, we find that hydrocodone is a Schedule II controlled substance within the purview of the CSA.
 
*2 The respondent, seeks discretionary relief in the form of cancellation of removal and bears the burden of proving that he is statutorily eligible for such relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). Consequently, as we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown that his 2002 Florida delivery of hydrocodone conviction is not a conviction for an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B) ofthe Act, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C.§ 1229b(a)(3).
 
Furthermore, contrary to the respondent’s appellate contentions, his 2002 Florida delivery of hydrocodone conviction is final for immigration purposes. The respondent’s general collateral attack on his criminal conviction based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in his criminal proceedings does not establish that the respondent does not have a conviction that is final for immigration purposes. It is clear that an Immigration Judge and the Board cannot entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction, unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to examine an alien’s claims regarding his guilt or innocence. See Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996).
 
Moreover, the respondent’s contention that he will be filing a petition for a belated appeal of his criminal conviction, does not alter our conclusion that his 2002 Florida delivery of hydrocodone conviction is final for immigration purposes. We found in Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894 (BIA 1994), that an alien who has waived or exhausted the right to a direct appeal of a conviction is subject to deportation, and the potential for discretionary review on direct appeal will not prevent the conviction from being considered final for immigration purposes. Like the alien in Matter of Polanco, supra, the respondent has indicated that he intends to file a motion for permission to file a late appeal, but he has not shown that the motion has actually been filed, much less granted. The respondent’s conviction is therefore final for immigration purposes. In addition, the respondent admitted the conviction at the hearing through his representative (Tr. at 6). Absent egregious circumstances, a concession made by counsel of record is binding upon the alien as an admission. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Accordingly, we find that his conviction is final for immigration purposes. See Matter of Chairez, 21 I&N Dec. 44 (BIA 1995); see also Matter of Onyido, 22 I&N Dec. 552 (BIA 1999).
 
*3 Based on the record before us, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that he is not inadmissible under sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and (II) of the Act, and is subject to removal from the United States based on the conviction record submitted in this case (Exh. 2). See section 240(c)(2)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(2)(A). Moreover, the respondent has failed to establish his eligibility for any relief from removal. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 1154087 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE GUADALUPE SOSA-RODRIGUEZ
File: AXX XX1 398 - San Diego
March 6, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Catalina Leff, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 Calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was rendered orally on January 5, 2007. The appeal was accordingly due on or before February 5, 2007. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was mailed after the due date and filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on February 12, 2007.
 
The respondent urges the Board to accept the appeal out of time. The respondent, through current counsel, states that he was under the impression that his former attorney would file his appeal before the due date. However, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Lozada, supra, requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002). No evidence has been submitted in support of the motion submitted on the respondent’s behalf by current counsel.
 
We find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is accordingly now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15 (1999).
 
We note that because we have dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J- J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 David B. Holmes

2007 WL 1168488 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAHMOOD SHAFIEE
File: AXX XX7 368 - Phoenix
March 2, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert B. Jobe, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sandra B. Miles
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude (withdrawn)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on July 20, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his motion to remand. On October 18, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be granted.
 
As an initial matter, we disagree with the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) that the respondent’s motion is untimely and number-barred, as his previous motion was deemed to be a motion to remand filed during the pendency of his second appeal, and the final administrative order was issued on July 20, 2006. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4).
 
Moreover, the respondent reasserts the claim that he is not removable as charged, and he now asserts a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former attorney for conceding removability. The respondent avers that “to the best of my knowledge I never discussed with ¿former counselº the issue of my removability and how they should plead to the charges” (Motion, attachment A). The attorney, in his response, does remember discussing this strategy with the respondent, and states that the respondent “had no interest in contesting the charges and wanted a final merit hearing to request relief as soon as possible” (Motion, attachment V). However, a client’s insistence on expediting a case is no excuse for failing to research and advise a client that there is no sound basis for the charges. A concession of removability under these circumstances cannot be considered a reasonable tactical decision. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (holding that absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission; and that there is a strong presumption that such a decision is a reasonable tactical decision).
 
The respondent has satisfied the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for ineffective assistance of counsel claims. Furthermore, he has submitted sufficient evidence that he was prejudiced by his counsel’s actions, as it appears that his 1985 conviction under California Penal Code § 273a(a) for child endangerment does not constitute a crime involving moral turpitude. See People v. Sanders, 10 Cal. App. 4th 1268, 1274 (5th Dist. 1992); see also Carty v. Ashcroft, 395 F.3d 1081, 1084 (9th Cir. 2005) (holding that when a statute is divisible into several crimes, some of which may involve moral turpitude and some not, it is appropriate to examine the “record of conviction” to determine which part applies to the defendant). Accordingly, the motion will be granted, proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
