2007 WL 1168487 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAIME GONZALEZ-ALARCON
File: AXX XX4 748 - San Diego
March 2, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL/MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Olga Nazimova, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
J. Grant
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on November 2, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s April 5, 2005, decision to denying his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order entered in his case on April 1, 2003, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel and lack of notice, for lack of jurisdiction.1 See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent petitioned for review of our decision in the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises. Pursuant to that court’s order dated August 10, 2006, remanding the case to the Board, we will vacate our decision dated November 2, 2005, rescind the Immigration Judge’s April 1, 2003, in absentia order in this matter, and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Upon further review, we find that pursuant to the decisional authority of the Ninth Circuit, the Immigration Judge was not “without jurisdiction” to consider the respondent’s motion under the particular circumstances presented in this case, despite the language in the regulations2 seeming to preclude consideration of a motion filed by an alien after his deportation from the United States. See Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979, 981-982 (9th Cir. 2007) (where the court recently held that the regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), prohibiting an alien who departs the U.S. from filing a motion to reopen deportation proceedings only applies to a person who departs the U.S. while he or she is the subject of pending deportation proceedings and does not apply in a case where the proceedings have been completed.); see also Cardoso-Tlaseca v. Gonzales, 460 F.3d 1102 (9th Cir 2006) (reaffirming the court’s holding in Wiedersperg v. INS, 896 F.2d 1179 (9th Cir. 1990) (court found that the regulatory bar to consideration by the Board of a deportation order after the alien departed the United States applied only if the departure was legally executed)); Thorsteinsson v. INS, 724 F.2d 1365, 1367 (9th Cir. 1983) (referring to its decision in Mendez v. INS, 563 F.2d 956, 958 (9th Cir. 1977), wherein it recognized that a departure in contravention of due process” was not a “legally executed departure,” precluding further review.).
 
Furthermore, we find the record strongly suggests a case of ineffective assistance by the respondent’s former counsel. See Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 587 (9th Cir. 2006) (recognizing that “¿bºecause due process requires more than the formal availability of counsel,” the court “held that an alien is denied due process when his attorney provides ineffective assistance.”). Therefore, despite the respondent’s failure to meet the formal requirements set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), we find that on the face of the record before us, there has been substantial compliance with the rule, and the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Lara-Torres v. Ashcrofi, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
*2 Therefore, in accordance with the decisional authority of the Ninth Circuit set forth above, as the record supports the respondent’s contention that he was denied due process and a fair hearing due to the ineffective assistance of his former counsel and lack of notice3 of the scheduled hearing, a departure in execution of an order of deportation entered pursuant to those proceedings, was a “departure in contravention of due process” and not a “legally executed departure,” and does not preclude our favorable consideration of the respondent’s request to reopen the proceedings. See Mendez v. INS, supra at 958; see also Cardoso-Tlaseca v. Gonzales, supra; Wiedersperg v. INS, supra. Furthermore, as the respondent was no longer the subject of pending deportation proceedings at the time he filed his motion with the Immigration Judge, he was not precluded under the regulations from having the motion considered solely because it had been filed after his deportation from the United States. See Lin v. Gonzales, supra at 981-982 (the regulatory bar to consideration by the Board of a deportation order after the alien departed the United States does not apply in a case where the proceedings have been completed).
 
Accordingly, pursuant to the Ninth Circuit’s August 10, 2006, remand order, and upon further consideration of the issues presented therein, the following orders will be entered:
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated November 2, 2005, is vacated, and the Immigration Judge’s April 1, 2003, in absentia order of deportation in this matter, is rescinded.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

However, on remand, in a decision dated April 1, 2003, the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia order of deportation due to the respondent’s failure to appear for his scheduled hearing.
